<!-- page 5 -->
Kitab al-Riyad
A judgment between the two reformers: al-Islah and al-Nusra
Authorship: the Ismaili Da'i al-Ajall
Hamid al-Din Ahmad ibn Abdallah al-Kirmani, Hujjat al-Iraqayn
Investigation and Verification: Arif Tamir
Dar al-Thaqafa — Beirut

<!-- page 6 -->

<!-- page 7 -->
Introduction

The Islamic world at the end of the third century of the Muhammadan Hijra was distinguished by the character of its sciences, philosophy, and literature, which shaped its cultural sphere and society. New international currents, complex doctrinal disagreements, and prolonged debate did not come to rest with any single party nor settle in any single place. How useful it is, then, for the researcher who wishes to study the origins of these differences in depth — to weigh the opinions of the writers and scholars who lived in that distant era, who left scientific traces and novel modes of expression characteristic of their time and of their understanding of philosophical issues, and the extent of their effort in interpreting texts and explaining verses — so that he may then arrive at a reasonable view that brings him closer to reality and truth.

The disputes mentioned were often not confined to the oral discussions formerly raised in salons and scientific centers; they grew into long treatises that appeared on the pages of books and publications. Among the sects that contributed a large share to this field were the ((Ismailis)). Yet it is strange that we do not see this group concluding its verbal argument with other groups; instead, another and altogether new kind of debate arose — a debate among

<!-- page 8 -->
those who themselves called for it. In this is clear proof of the intellectual freedom that prevailed in their lives — freedom from the constraints that dominate conservative societies and that impose upon them a balance which obliges them not to be anomalous and to stay away from any departure from inherited tradition.

The first debate, or in more precise terms the first scientific discussion, opened new horizons for the Ismailis and moved them into an atmosphere of broad scientific liberation. It made them a subject of conjecture and talk throughout the Islamic world. This began with the emergence of the book ((al-Mahsul)) (The Harvest).

This book entered circulation at the beginning of the fourth century AH, or the tenth century AD. It is attributed to the great preacher of Khurasan, ((Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Nasafi)), who was credited with converting the Samanid state in Transoxiana to Ismailism, and who was executed in the year 331 AH (942 AD), as stated in the book ((al-Farq bayn al-Firaq)) by its author Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi. It is very strange that ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)), its greatest exponent and its foremost philosopher ((Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani)), who discussed the subject and went deeply into its examination, did not mention the author of ((al-Mahsul)); rather his discussion focused on ((Abu Hatim al-Razi and Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani)). There must then be fundamental reasons for this omission that justify it, and perhaps the book ((al-Mahsul)) is wrongly attributed to al-Nasafi.

Whatever the case, the acclaim that the book ((al-Mahsul)) received among Ismaili intellectuals and scholars of that time prompted Abu Hatim al-Razi, the chief preacher in northwestern Persia, to compose a book in which he discussed what was said in ((al-Mahsul)). His book was called ((al-Islah)). It is clear that the opinions which al-Razi advanced provoked a reaction in another great preacher, named Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani, who regarded them as contrary to the truth and to first principles. This is what led him to write a book in which he challenged the views of al-Razi, called ((al-Nusra)), in which the author defended

— 6 —

<!-- page 9 -->
((al-Mahsul)) and attacked Abu Hatim al-Razi relentlessly. It seems that ((al-Nusra)) is among al-Sijistani's earliest works, as al-Kirmani himself alludes in his book. Not long afterwards, al-Kirmani reviewed the entire subject in a comprehensive review and composed his precious treatise ((al-Riyad)) — a judgment between the ((two reformers)), as he called them (al-Islah and al-Nusra). With great skill he summarized the opinions that appeared in the three books and subjected them to scholarly criticism. He compared them with the ((Law of the Guiding Mission)) — or, in other words, with the philosophical and doctrinal theories officially adopted by the Fatimid court in his day. In general, we find that al-Kirmani always prefers al-Razi's theories against al-Sijistani's, and objects to the latter because they do not conform to truth and tend toward deviation. Moreover, he notes that al-Razi overlooked some of the statements that appeared in the book ((al-Mahsul)), as we note in the tenth chapter of the book ((al-Riyad)).

It is also worth mentioning the scientific ideas these works contain. A philosophical group such as this deserves to be studied, examined, and scrutinized deeply, but unfortunately the books ((al-Mahsul)) and ((al-Nusra)) have been lost. As for the book ((al-Islah)) by al-Razi and ((al-Riyad)) by al-Kirmani, they are still preserved.

((Kitab al-Riyad))

Al-Kirmani's book ((al-Riyad)) is considered one of the important works that express the philosophical theories of Ismailism in the fourth century AH. I have obtained two copies of it: the first from India, and the second from Tehran, sent to me by the Orientalist W. Ivanow, on behalf of the Ismaili Society in India (The Ismaili Society), which wished me to investigate and explain the book and to write its footnotes and commentary as one of the working members of this association.

Before entering into details, I must research and review what is contained in the book in terms of opinions, sayings, arguments, and statements, by way of a brief overview of

— 7 —

<!-- page 10 -->
the lives of the three philosophers who participated in the discussion — namely al-Razi, al-Sijistani, and al-Kirmani.

Al-Razi:

He is Abu Hatim Ahmad ibn Hamdan al-Laythi al-Warsanani al-Razi, and his name in Ismaili circles is "our master (Abu Hatim) the Exalted." He was a preacher of Ismailism in the land of Rayy, then moved to Baghdad and made it his residence. The date of his birth is not known exactly. His activity in the Ismaili da'wa fell during the imamate of Muhammad al-Mahdi Billah (1) (Ubayd Allah), the founder of the Fatimid state in the Maghrib. He was distinguished by the sweetness of his speech and the eloquence for which he is famous. He attracted (al-Marwazi), the prince of Rayy, who was a strict Sunni, and persuaded the princes of those regions to enter into Ismailism, until he became one of the standards of politics and religion, and one of the guides of the Islamic scientific renaissance in the late third and early fourth centuries AH. He took refuge in the theological and philosophical sciences. The great Orientalist Paul Kraus (2) said of him: "He was a leading preacher of the Ismailis and played an important role in political affairs in Tabaristan and Daylam, especially in Rayy and Isfahan, until the chief statesmen responded to him, such as Asfar ibn Shirawayh, Mardawij the commander, and others." In truth, Abu Hatim al-Razi was one of the standards of the scientific renaissance not only within Ismailism, but in the Islamic world more broadly. He contributed to the dissemination of philosophical teachings in all the eastern lands, and especially in the periphery of the general Islamic culture. Despite all this, he

(1) This imam lived in Salamiyya, Syria, and migrated thence to the Maghrib when Abbasid pressure intensified, after the Qarmatians split off and openly rebelled against the da'wa — especially after the great Syrian preacher Abu Abdallah al-Shi'i went over to him and paved the way for the establishment of the Fatimid state in the Maghrib [cf. Our Story from the Levant to the Maghrib — Publications of the Lebanese Book House, Beirut, Lebanon, 1959].
(2) Philosophical Letters — Paul Kraus — p. 291

— 8 —

<!-- page 11 -->
was not spared the persecution of his enemies in Daylam, and was forced into hiding in the last years of his life. He died in 322 AH, after the Fatimid al-Qa'im took charge of the affairs of the Ismaili imamate in the Maghrib, at the age of sixty-two, as some historians have said.

He was a contemporary of Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi, the famous physician and author of well-known philosophical views in which he departed from many of Aristotle's theories — natural and metaphysical — denying the reconciliation of philosophy and religion, and holding that philosophy is the only way to reform the individual and society. Between the two Razis there were violent and repeated discussions, attended by some scholars and political leaders. Abu Hatim recorded these discussions in his book ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)) (Signs of Prophethood). Although he does not name the person he is criticizing, calling him only "the atheist," there is conclusive evidence that this atheist was none other than al-Razi, Abu Bakr.

The great philosopher al-Kirmani (Hujjat al-Iraqayn) has noted in his book ((al-Aqwal al-Dhahabiyya)) (The Golden Sayings) that discussions about prophethood and legal rituals took place between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu Hatim on the island of Rayy in the days of Mardawij, and in his presence. Abu Hatim was able to humble Abu Bakr al-Razi and to refute his views in his book ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)) — pages overflowing with charm and marvels — in which he closed off, for the arrogant and the stubborn, every means of evasion and flight.

His most important works are the book ((al-Zina)) (1) — on the Arabic letters, their derivatives, the reasons for their forms, and the meanings of words. It is a great book on authentic Arabic vocabulary and on the framework within which the Qur'an was articulated and given legal force by the Muslims, and from which Islamic terminology took shape. This book runs to 1200 pages. He dedicated it to the Fatimid imam who succeeded Muhammad al-Mahdi Billah (Ubayd Allah), and to him

(1) This book is being edited by Dr. Hussein Hamdani of Cairo University.
— 9 —

<!-- page 12 -->
also belong the books ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)), ((al-Islah)), ((al-Jami')), and others.

Al-Sijistani:

Abu Ya'qub Ishaq al-Sijistani — or (al-Sijzi) — is considered to be at the forefront of those scholars who struggled, labored, and dedicated themselves to establishing cosmic philosophical principles upon doctrinal intellectual foundations, solid scientific bases, and fixed pillars that do not tremble whatever crises may befall them. Indeed, he is among those who sacrificed all they had in order to spread it and to circulate it in other lands. Late in his life he was accused of blasphemy and atheism by the conservative public, and was finally killed for the sake of his faith. Al-Sijistani played an important role in the field of philosophy and rendered significant intellectual services in the third century of the Hijra. His intellectual impact appeared in his disciple Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani (Hujjat al-Iraqayn), who followed his method, called to his teachings, and continued the flow of his springs. If we recognize that al-Kirmani studied philosophy in al-Sijistani's school of thought, we may place al-Sijistani in the first rank among the famous Muslim scholars, thinkers, and philosophers of the world.

He was a contemporary of the esoteric Ismaili da'wa in the Age of Manifestation — that is, the period of the flourishing of the Fatimid state and its emergence as an Islamic state of civilizational, scientific, social, and political stature — although he lived in a country whose people held to a doctrine that differed from his madhhab. He was forced to adopt (taqiyya) as a cover, and to be very cautious in his movements and his preaching. For this reason his life has come down to us with a certain mysterious ambiguity, and one may not be blamed if we cannot come to know his biography fully or relate everything about him. On the other hand, we have received only a little about the preacher al-Khayr al-Nasafi ((al-Nakhshabi)) the author of ((al-Mahsul)) — beyond what authors have mentioned of his efforts and of his contact with Nasr ibn Ahmad

— 10 —

<!-- page 13 -->
the Samanid in Transoxiana, until that Samanid embraced the Ismaili da'wa. We also know little about Abu Hatim al-Razi, the linguist and author of the oldest work in the science of the Arabic language — the book ((al-Zina)) — and about ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)) Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani, despite the survival of most of his writings and works down to us — even though al-Mu'ayyad fi'l-Din Hibatallah al-Shirazi, the friend and companion of Abu al-Ala al-Ma'arri, wrote his biography in his own hand. We cannot really know much about him, and the same may be said of the obscurity surrounding the lives of the senior Ismaili da'wa men — the hujjas and preachers of Algeria, Syria, Iran, Yemen, and elsewhere. Their lives are even more shrouded in mystery, since the books in which they recorded their biographies have been lost, and only a few of them remain.

He is attributed to (Sijistan), a province in the south of Khurasan, of a Persian family. It is said that he was of the family of the Persian hero ((Rustam)), and there are those who say that he was of Iraqi origin, and that his grandfather came from Kufa and settled in Sijistan. Some researchers who studied his philosophy claim that he died in 331 AH. But this view does not accord with historical reality, for it is known of al-Sijistani that he was the teacher of al-Kirmani, and al-Kirmani remained alive until the year 411 AH. So at what time, then, did al-Kirmani study the philosophical sciences of the da'wa under him? There is an explicit text in the book ((al-Iftikhar)) by al-Sijistani himself in which he mentions that he composed it in 360 AH. The book ((al-Iftikhar)) is in turn mentioned in the book ((al-Riyad)) by al-Kirmani — that is, he was a preacher in the province of Bukhara during the caliphate of the imam ((al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah al-Fatimi)). The meaning of this is that he was a contemporary of the great preacher ((Ja'far ibn Mansur al-Yamani)), and of the scholar and jurist the qadi ((al-Nu'man ibn Muhammad ibn Hayyun al-Maghribi al-Tamimi)), the chief judge of the Fatimid state, and of other great authors and scholars of the da'wa in that golden age of manifest science. There is no shortage of evidence for al-Sijistani's scholarly value from his books and writings, which

— 110 —


<!-- page 14 -->
He left behind these works in Arabic, with some of them composed in Persian. Isma'il ibn Abd al-Rasul ibn Mata Khan al-Abini, who died in 1183 AH, recorded them in his collection and catalogue. Al-Biruni also referred to them in his books, and al-Baghdadi mentioned them in his *al-Farq bayn al-Firaq* (The Difference Between the Sects).

As we said, al-Sijistani left behind a prolific body of scholarly writing that ranks among the deepest works in philosophy — more than thirty titles in number. Among the most famous are *Ithbat al-Nubuwwat* (Proofs of Prophecy), *Kashf al-Mahjub* (Unveiling the Veiled) (1), *Tuhfat al-Mustajibin* (Masterpiece of the Respondents) (2), and a third book (3) — which he divided into forty springs (*yanabi'*), and which has come to be regarded as a work whose depth is rarely surpassed. He appears to have composed it for a particular audience: preachers and those occupying the highest ranks of the da'wa, men whose philosophical studies had reached their climax. His reason for dividing it into forty springs goes back to his wish that each spring should interpret one of the forty limits that compose the council of the highest da'wa.

Among the other books of his mentioned by the great traveler and Persian poet Nasir-i Khusraw in his *Zad al-Musafirin* (Provisions for Travelers) are: *The Foundations of the Da'wa*; *The Interpretation of the Laws*; *The Iris of Blessings*, or *The Iris of Survival*; *The Brilliant Message*; the *Kitab al-Iftikhar*; *The Scales*, which is divided into nineteen scales; *The Ladder of Salvation*; *The Victory*; *The Hooks*; *Amusements of Sorrows*; *Secrets of the Return*; *Sermons on Ethics*; *The Stranger on the Meaning of the Elixir*; *Sociable of Hearts*; *Composition of Spirits*; *Security from Confusion*; *Closets of Evidence*; *The Proof*; and others.

(1) This book was edited and published by the orientalist Henri Corbin in Tehran. Arif Tamir (may Allah be pleased with him) also included it in his volume *Five Ismaili Letters*.

(2), (3) The orientalist Henri Corbin, in collaboration with Arif Tamir, edited these works and included them in *Three Ismaili Epistles*, among the publications of the Institut Française in Tehran, Iran.

<!-- page 15 -->
In what follows we summarize al-Sijistani's views on theology as expressed across most of his writings.

He holds that the Creator of all creators is an Ancient, Exalted, and Primordial Creator, anterior in the order of being; and that the world of beings and creatures is contingent and originated in time, since otherwise something would necessarily have preceded it — even on the supposition that the world is itself old. The Creator is prior to the world, for it is impossible to attribute might to what is co-eternal with Him, or to ascribe His existence to nothingness or to necessity. He transcends every attribute one might affix to Him: He is not attained by sense, does not fall under the eyes, is not perceived by sight, is not called naturalized, is not subject to conjecture, and is not seen. He is not described by the senses, nor grasped by analogy, nor likened to creatures; He is exalted above any rival, equal, or anything of that kind. Exalted is He above the bewildered conjectures of the imagination concerning His might, and above the understanding that falls short of the character of His kingdom — a kingdom whose greatness is seen without being grasped. He has no likeness and no counterpart; He has no equal and no opposite. The very notion of opposition is against Him, for an opposite would be a rival, one that would point to His identity through what He has created and through His names, in the way His prophets indicate. The intellect has no foothold and no analogy by which to attain His heavens, for any analogy that places the creature alongside its Creator is impossible. He is the cause of every existent, since He is the Creator of creators and the Inventor of inventors, the cause of all beings, the Lord of all things, the Originator, the Perfecter, and the One who brings them to the best of their states. He is not bounded by any thought, nor encompassed by any estimation. He has no names — for names are coined for what exists in His creation and not for attributes, and attributes belong to essences — and the letters of language cannot reach the pronunciation of His name, nor name some of Him by them, since the letters themselves are among His inventions. All the names He has created make Him to be that Ancient, Pre-eternal Source, the Owner of the source of primacy in the order of being.

For from Him the first limit emerged and the first One overflowed, while He remains the Creator of creators, the Originator who reveals, the One who brings forth from nothing, and the Perpetual whose being subsists in His own individuality and steadfastness. The first act of creation was accompanied by the first number, which He made the origin of numbers — just as He made the Intellect the origin of created things and the first articulator of the world of religion. Added to all of this, He acquires none of the attributes; He is not a body, nor in
<!-- page 16 -->
a body; He is not rational of Himself; He is not perceived by the senses; He is not in any image, nor in any form, nor in any substance; and there is nothing in any language by which He may be expressed. He exists — but it is not correct to say that He exists in the manner of those existents that have come into being, nor is His existence inferred from the existence of other existents in the manner that an effect is inferred from its cause. He does not come to be without a cause, and exists only as He must exist; while contingent beings depend upon one another in their existence, some resting upon others, and likewise some of them are not fixed in being and do not endure thereafter.

Al-Sijistani then moves on to the existents in their order and sequence, saying:

The Creator brought nothing into being at the beginning of creation except the Intellect, in whose essence are bounded the images of all created things. The name "Pen" is given to the Intellect, because the pen marks the inscriptions of creation from its beginning. It is also called "the Throne" — that is, the seat of the One who sits upon it, whose Majesty knows He is exalted above being settled upon it. The Intellect is also called (1) the primordial from which creatures emerged — meaning all that exists; and what is imprinted upon it precedes the acceptance of the effects of wisdom in advance of the remaining limits, because of its closeness to that source and its union with it, which is knowledge and command taken in one sense. It is therefore permissible to say that the act of the Intellect precedes its power; and this virtue is found in nothing other than it, for all the limits below it have their powers preceding their acts. This virtue belongs to the Intellect in particular, so that perfection may reside in it. The Intellect is also called "the Pronouncement," because by the Intellect the soul resolves to perceive what it learns and to obtain what is required of it; and it is permissible to say that the Intellect is the decree of God Almighty among His creation. The Intellect is likewise called *al-hayuli* (prime matter), meaning that the Intellect has the strength to receive what is reflected upon it of the images; and it is called "the Sun," meaning that the Intellect perceives the realities.

Then the Soul, which is the second creation, is the one eternalized by the first creation; it is called "Soul" (*nafs*) because it always breathes (*yatanaffas*) in order to draw benefit from it,
<!-- page 17 -->
and the frequency of that breathing is the texture of creation. The Soul is also called "the Tablet," meaning what was separated off from the Intellect; from it the dominion of wisdom passes down upon the Soul, and from the Soul flows what it gives forth, in measure of its purity and refinement. The Soul is called "the King," meaning that the Soul is the property of the Intellect and its content, for in the Soul a virtue has appeared that is revealed by the Intellect and is likewise revealed by the King. The Soul is called "the Follower," meaning the one who recites after the Intellect at the threshold of accepting the effects of the Word; and it is permissible to say that the Soul, by its power, recites the Intellect through its own act. The Soul is also called "Destiny," meaning that what unites with the Soul is among the benefits of the Intellect, since estimation and determination encompass it. The Soul is called "the Image," and the meaning of this is that the Soul is conceived from the very essence of the Intellect; and when one tries to follow it down to its station, its light is true to the Intellect's light — just as the moon draws its light from the sun, and when they meet at one place, the moon's light becomes its own. Of the Intellect and the Soul together it is said in one word: "the Two Principles."

These are some of al-Sijistani's philosophical views on theology, presented in summary and joined to whatever we have been able to recover of his biography. The guide that drove him to undertake these sciences, and his habit of laying out their texts in attractive order and exquisite arrangement, is perhaps what carried him to this depth.

**Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani**

A scholarly master whose life history is shrouded in mystery, and a great philosopher who lived in a flourishing scientific age — leaving behind a glorious farewell written across the pages of thought. He undertook deep researches and the most penetrating lines of inquiry, and left to the following generations a body of writings that, at the very least, may be called a precious treasure and an immortal heritage.

The philosophers of the Islamic world consider him the greatest mind produced by the Ismaili school
<!-- page 18 -->
of thought during the era of the Fatimid state. His book *Rahat al-Aql* (Rest of the Mind) (a) is one of those precious works that scarcely finds an equal among the books of the philosophers — or among what remains of their legacy — in strength, durability, and depth. Demand for it was small, its popularity slow, and its readership limited to a special class of brilliant scholars and philosophers capable of mastering it.

The great preacher and Yemeni historian Idris Imad al-Din mentioned him in his book, saying: "He is the foundation of the da'wa upon which it rests and by which it is raised up; through him its beacon was set straight, and by him problems were identified and dilemmas resolved." The Syrian Ismaili preacher Nur al-Din Ahmad described him in his book *Fusul wa Akhbar* (Chapters and Reports), saying: "If the Ismaili da'wa had produced nothing but al-Kirmani, that alone would have been ample pride and glory, and that alone would have been enough."

His impact and stature appeared during the reign of the Fatimid Caliph al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah. His famous title was *Hujjat al-Iraqayn* (the Hujjah of the Two Iraqs), meaning that he had charge of the affairs of the da'wa's cultural centers throughout Persia and Iraq. In Cairo, his standing was that of a Hujjah — one of the twelve hujjahs in charge of managing the affairs of the da'wa, the intellectual vanguard of the world. He was later appointed head of *Dar al-Hikma* (the House of Wisdom) in Cairo, the cultural institution that we may rightly call the first university established in the world.

He travelled in a delegation to Cairo in 408 AH at the request of al-Sadiq al-Ma'mun, then the foremost of the Fatimid state's preachers in the era of al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah — at a moment when religious battles were heating up and new movements were flooding the market of innovation with calls aimed at exaggeration and at deviation from the realities and the foundations of the faith. He gave talks and lectures in *Dar al-Hikma*, and produced a great many

<!-- page 19 -->
researches and books, the most famous of which is *Risalat al-Wa'iza* (the Preacher's Message), composed in reply to al-Hasan al-Farghani, who held that the Ruler al-Hakim was divine by the command of God. Among his other works are the epistles known as *al-Mabasim* (the Good Tidings) and *al-Masabih* (the Lamps), in which he was able to prove the Imamate as a necessary cosmic reality by means of reasoned, compelling arguments — arguments richly furnished with Hebrew, Syriac, and Persian expressions drawn from the books of the prophets of the heavenly utterance. He then took up the Platonic opinions as the basis for his researches and deployed them with a skill no one before him had brought to bear. All of this served as a general call to consolidate the philosophical-intellectual system, to raise its level, and to erase every trace of doubt, controversy, suspicion, and dispute.

In any case, in these lines I will not attempt to introduce the philosopher al-Kirmani merely as one of the Ismaili preachers who played an important role in the field of thought during the reign of the Fatimid Caliph al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah, or merely as one of the philosophers who served the Fatimid da'wa with clear intellectual services. I present him, rather, as a philosopher among the philosophers of the world — one who walked upon the stage of universal philosophy and exerted everything in him to found a philosophical school upon a new intellectual foundation, drawn from the modern, innovative theories of his time and from the mental systems then current. This applies in particular to the theory of the unity of being, which the Second Teacher [al-Farabi] and the Chief Sheikh [Ibn Sina] hold, here expanded by him in exposition and somewhat departing from the general curriculum laid down for the Ismaili preachers — whether those who came before him or those of his own age — who were obliged to follow a well-defined general framework whose limits they could not transgress or cross.

Returning to al-Kirmani's writings, and reading them with careful analysis of what we see in them, he has resolved that there is contradiction, disharmony, and an attempt on the part of some [of the existents] to erase others, yet that these existents persist in spite of this dissonance and this contradiction; for none of them is lost by the presence of an opposite — rather they are all preserved under existence. All of this is identical to the theory of the Second Teacher

<!-- page 20 -->
on creation, in which he says: "The preservation of the continuance of the existence of a thing that does not possess, in itself, a permanence unrelated to any of the contingents — other than the Creator." (1) And Ibn Sina, in the course of speaking of the possible and the necessary among the other existents, gives an account that clarifies the meaning of the continuity of existence among existents, saying: "As for the fact that the contingents can exist in themselves, while not being obligated by the existence of others, this does not contradict their being always present in others." (2) What these three philosophers — the Second Teacher, the Chief Sheikh, and the Hujjah of the Two Iraqs — go to in this regard nearly approaches what the philosopher Descartes said in the modern era: "There is, between the act by which God preserves the world and the act by which He created it, no [real difference]." (3) This is what is known in his philosophy as the theory of *continuous creation*.

In fact, al-Kirmani shared something with the Muslim philosophers, and was influenced by some of them — especially those who took up Greek philosophy. He is distinguished from them, however, by the fact that, when he presented these philosophies, he stamped them with the marks of novelty, wit, and innovation, and was the precursor of the modern European philosophers and the ancient Oriental renewers. Accordingly, one may say that al-Kirmani left behind books and produced ideas that should be looked upon not only as Ismaili intellectual views, but as Islamic philosophical views of a high, public-facing order — looking into the essence of things and into the deep intellectual aspects in a steady, elaborated manner in which genius and brilliance are manifest.

Yes, al-Kirmani spoke within the Neoplatonic systems, and exerted all his efforts to apply some of their materials in his attractive philosophical and rhetorical style; and he worked to prove the matter of Prophethood and the Imamate from both the philosophical and the religious sides.

(1) Al-Farabi, *Uyun al-Masa'il*, p. 60.
(2) Ibn Sina, *al-Isharat*, p. 240.
(3) Descartes, *Discourse on Method* — Section V — and *Principles of Philosophy*, paragraph (21).


<!-- page 21 -->
On this point we can say that he upheld the theory that adherence to the outward Shari'ah leads to scriptural worship, while the further reach of philosophical interpretation and discussion of the boundaries (ḥudūd) is reserved for those permitted ones who have attained a high level in philosophy and science. We find all these features when we look closely at *The Illuminating Message*, *The Lamps in Proving the Imamate*, *Alerting the Guide and the Guided*, and *Rest of the Mind* (*Rāḥat al-ʿAql*). Beyond this, al-Kirmani is a major asset in developing the intellectual system and expanding theoretical discourse — adding new elements, broadening certain topics, laying down the basic rules of interpretation, and treating contradiction and similarity, especially on the subject of the Imamate, which had been the focus of unstable and shifting opinions and theories. For this reason his book *Peace of Mind* defined the rules, origins, ranks, and status of the Imamate, and set new foundations, systems, and order for it.

Al-Kirmani was prominent in the doctrine of the call to existence, in the negation of essence (*aysiyya*) and substance (*laysiyya*), in the doctrine that the divine attributes are subject to absolute negation, in the doctrine of the call to Tawhid, in the doctrine of the call concerning the two principles of origination, in the elaboration of emanation, and in the question of priority between the Imamate and the Prophethood — all in a purely scientific, logical manner. He did not stop there: at the outset he gathered the two first principles — the Active Intellect and the Universal Soul — together with the system of the ten Platonic intellects upheld by the Second Teacher (al-Farabi). He set the world of origination (the world of intellects, the spiritual world) and the corporeal celestial world (the world of stars and planets) alongside the physical world (below the lunar orbit) and the world of religion (the world of the boundaries of religion); he then drew geographical and astronomical diagrams for it, together with terrestrial and corporeal plans that came out highly artistic and creative, showing his ingenuity of expression and his eminence in philosophy.

<!-- page 22 -->
On returning to *Peace of Mind* and reviewing what is stated in it, you will see that it is full of formulations and arguments concerning the apostolic statements about God and the negation of the divine attributes. Among the positions he stated and supported: that no attribute attaches to Allah; that He is not a body nor in a body; that He neither rationalizes Himself nor senses Himself; that He is not in a form or in a substance; that He has no opposite and no likeness; that no language can express Him; and that He has no rank among existents. This indicates the breadth of his knowledge across the various sciences, especially the rational ones. On this the Mu'tazila and the theologians (*mutakallimūn*) agree, and he agrees with Ibn Rushd. On the *aʿtīq* (ancient) argument for the existence of God we see him agreeing with Descartes, even if they differ in the details and the method of expression. If we add a little further research, we see that he folded into his solutions and ideas some of the views of philosophers who preceded him and of his contemporaries — Plato, Aristotle, al-Kindi, al-Farabi, and Ibn Sina. We note from al-Baqillani, al-Baghdadi, and al-Ghazali that they ruled the Ismailis, by their denial of the attributes, to have fallen into invalidity (*taʿṭīl*). But al-Kirmani responded to this charge, saying: "Outright denial is to direct the particle of negation (*lā*, 'no') against the identity itself — as in saying 'He is not' or 'there is no god.' This is not what the Ismailis say. Their negation is the negation of the attributes alone; the direction of the negative particle *lā* for them is toward the attributes, leaving the identity untouched." However the matter stands, al-Kirmani gave special attention to this subject in his book *Peace of Mind*. And in this book we see him departing from all philosophers, writers, historians, and scientists: instead of dividing his book into the usual books, chapters, and sections that other men of thought and authors employ, he likens his book to a city encircled by seven walls. Whoever enters it must pass through the seven walls, and to pass through them he must face seven gates branching off from each wall — except the seventh wall.

<!-- page 23 -->
As for the seventh and last wall encircling the city, it has fourteen "legislators" (*mashārīʿ*). We will not enter here into an exposition of what al-Kirmani meant by his walls and gates, for fear of going on at length; but this does not stop us from affirming his superiority in philosophy, the power of his thinking, and the depth of his grasp of the essence of things.

Al-Kirmani published a number of works, the most important of which include: *The Divine Treatise* (1), *The Treatise of the Systems*, *The Treatise of Light*, *The Illuminating Treatise*, *The Necessary Treatise*, *The Guiding Treatise*, *The Preacher's Treatise*, *The Sufficient Treatise* (*al-Kāfī*), *Alerting the Guide and the Guided*, *Wrists of the Guide* (*Maʿāṣim al-Hudā*), *The Golden Sayings*, *Separating the Discourse and Distinguishing the Manifest Truth from Doubt*, *The Treatise of the Returner*, *The Treatise of the Index*, *Quantities and Facts*, *The Treatise on Tawhid in the Promised Land*, *The Crown of Intellects* (*Tāj al-ʿUqūl*), *The Balance of the Mind*, *The Book of Criticism and Obligation*, *Psychological Adequacy*, *The Book of Scales*, *The Baghdadi and Basran Sessions*, *al-Shaʿarī's Treatise on the Elite*, *Rest of the Mind*, and *The Treatise of the Week of the Role of the Cover* (2).

Finally, it must be said that al-Kirmani is among the philosophers who weigh heavily on our philosophical world; the study of his writings and his output is of great importance. It gives a clear picture of the impact of philosophy on the history of thought for those interested in Oriental Studies and Islamic Philosophy.

**Contents of *Riyāḍ*.** Al-Kirmani opens his book *Riyāḍ* by praising and glorifying God — the customary opening of all scholars in those eras — and finally points to the Imam of his ruling time, ruling by the command of Allah, of the Fatimids. He then says:

(1) This treatise was researched and published by Mohamed Kamel Hussein, Cairo University.
(2) This treatise was verified and published by Aref Tamer.

<!-- page 24 -->
"I saw that Abu Hatim al-Razi had taken up the errors in the book *al-Maḥṣūl* (*The Harvest*). Then Abu Yaʿqub al-Sijistani came and defended the author of *al-Maḥṣūl*, insisting on the soundness of his argument and the strength of his theory, and that the matters he discussed were not Ismaili philosophical side-issues or branches that could be passed over — for to pass them over would be an affront to the creed and a disturbance of the basic principles and foundations.

It seems that Abu Yaʿqub backed certain texts contrary to Abu Hatim al-Razi, attacking him, and in some places speaking in ways that bore no relation to the topic at all. In any event, certain passages in *al-Maḥṣūl* dealing with monotheism made it obligatory for Abu Hatim to correct and discuss them. He did not; he preferred instead to indulge in long stories on subjects of lesser importance — the historical material contained in the Torah — with which he filled his book, leaving *al-Maḥṣūl* uncorrected and neglected. He thereby produced a kind of intellectual confusion among the followers of the quiet daʿwa by setting different and divergent opinions before them, especially once they had taken to reading *al-Maḥṣūl*. This had an effect on the purity of the idea of the paths of monotheism, and led to corruption of the principles of Shariʿa and of the arrangement of the boundaries (*ḥudūd*). And so we see al-Kirmani say: 'For this reason I decided to quote here the original statements contained in the book *al-Iṣlāḥ* that correct some of what is mentioned in the book *al-Maḥṣūl*, along with what has been mentioned in the statements of *al-Nuṣra* that support *al-Maḥṣūl*, presenting my own opinion and comments. My aim is to display the principles in their correct sense and to separate the two positions; once we have reached this point it becomes easy to expose the error, so that the correct teachings remain free of doubt and ambiguity, and far removed from every disparity and confusion.'

Al-Kirmani divided his book *Riyāḍ* into ten chapters, each subdivided into several sections; and with his literary and scientific deftness, the strength of his exposition, and his learning, he selected what he saw as important and fundamental from the books *al-Iṣlāḥ* and *al-Nuṣra* and recast it in his own style —

<!-- page 25 -->
clear and concise — a kind of artistry totally different from al-Razi's style.

The first chapter of *Riyāḍ* is divided into thirty-eight sections, devoted to the subject of the perfection of the soul. The author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* says: the soul is perfect, a perfect source issuing from perfection itself, because the intellect is perfection. The author of *al-Nuṣra* contradicts this, saying that the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* does not realize that "the perfect" is higher and more complete than "perfection," because the second — perfection — is an attribute of the first and is therefore predicated of it. The perfect, which bears this attribute, is therefore higher, and the attribute attached to it is named after it; for the perfect exists, and when perfection exists it becomes the follower, not the followed.

Here the debate between truth and fancy sharpens, and the author of *Riyāḍ* lifts the cover from the logical error: the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* did not mean perfection in general, but the highest perfection — namely the intellect (the *ʿaql*) — which is the cause of the existence of the perfect soul that has come to be characterized by it. And the effect cannot stand higher than the cause. Al-Kirmani's view thus leads us to hold that al-Sijistani's attack on al-Razi was out of place and incorrect. Al-Kirmani then takes up al-Razi's opinion, in which he says: although the soul is perfect, its actions are not, since these actions depend on time, whereas the soul is the emanation of the intellect, and the intellect is a being composed in itself of origination, time, and perfection — unlike the soul, which is emanated within time and depends on the supply that comes from the intellect. This dependence does not impair the soul's substance, nor render it base, just as the intellect's dependence on the divine will does not make it inferior. The substance is not the inferior of matter on account of this perfection or imperfection, which are born of the soul and are not its substance. And it may be said that everything mentioned in the first chapter of the book

<!-- page 26 -->
*Riyāḍ* — namely that these two quotations are drawn from the book *al-Iṣlāḥ* — al-Kirmani strongly criticizes the opinions of *al-Nuṣra*, holding that al-Sijistani misunderstood these theories. He likens him to a newcomer who entered the world of the ideals with no goal but material vanity.

The author of *al-Nuṣra* cited the saying of Empedocles (1) in protest: "It [the intellect] became as it is only because it was not subject to anything but the Almighty; the soul became deficient because it has been subject to nature." We say: the philosophers who preceded them, even if they were virtuous in their time, remained the authority of error in many of the matters they spoke of from their own minds. Had they been in our time, and had they seen the springs of grace and what overflows from the House of Revelation with the facts of things as they are — namely the ʿAlawi daʿwa — they would have used their diligence to do themselves justice, and would have expressed many of their beliefs in the absence of the senses. They would have been among those for whom Allah set a light in the mysteries of things. In their own time, however, they followed their own opinions and their own minds, each going in his belief in the direction his reason pointed him.

(1) A great philosopher who lived around 440 BC, a contemporary of Parmenides though younger than him. His doctrine is connected with that of Heraclitus with a slight modification. He was from the city of Akragas on the southern coast of Sicily. He was a democratic politician and claimed himself to be a god. Legends recount that he performed miracles — sometimes by magic, sometimes by his scientific knowledge. It is narrated that he was able to control the wind, and that he restored a dead woman to life. Everything he wrote was in verse, as Parmenides had done; he was praised by Lucretius, who was influenced by him. Among his contributions to science: he held that air is a self-contained element, a claim he supported with solid scientific evidence; and he recognized that plants reproduce sexually. He held a doctrine of evolution and survival of the fittest. In astronomy he said that the moon is illuminated by rays reflected onto it from the sun. He was the founder of the Italian school of medicine, a school later inherited by Plato and Aristotle. He held that earth, air, fire, and water are the four elements; that the material world is spherical in shape; and he upheld some Pythagorean views and refused to adopt monotheism. He went on to say that the course of nature is governed in its system by chance and necessity more than by any directed end, and in these respects his philosophy is closer to science than the philosophies of Parmenides, Plato, and Aristotle.

<!-- page 27 -->
His works rest on the same ground as the Ẓāhirīs of the Hanafi sect when they relied on their own minds in walking the paths of religion: their opinions diverged, and among them there was a distinction — some closer to right, others closer to wrong — and they relied on what the balance of religion testifies to, namely the horizons and the souls. In light of the foregoing discussion — and the statement that the matter of belief in it, as he has explained, is false, free of exaggeration and prolixity — what then is the meaning of what Empedocles mentioned, beyond what the author of *al-Nuṣra* mentioned in his protest, and the saying of Empedocles at which we are aiming our challenge? We ask God to enlighten our minds and to close our affairs and our acts of goodness with happiness, and to aid us through His kindness and generosity in good works."

The second chapter of *Riyāḍ* is divided into nine sections and is devoted to the discussion of the relationship between the intellect and the soul. Al-Sijistani objects to al-Razi because the latter raised the topic of motion, repose, and stillness — the central topic that so often caused difficulty for the ancient philosophers. Here motion and stillness are taken as two effects united in "the First" and the Second. This supports what Abu Hatim al-Razi said: that the First was made originative and has become attached to and responsible for origination, since the truth of His station is a duality (the originator and the originated), and these two effects are present in "the First." When the Second was brought forth from the First, the bringing-forth itself became dualized (the same emanation and the same emanated), and so the two effects are linked to the two basic principles. As for motion, it is the benefaction (*fayḍ*) that the First releases upon the Second and which acts upon all assets and entities, while stillness is the acceptance of the First's command throughout all the basic entities.

Al-Razi says: I do not say that motion and stillness are generated from the soul by a power received from the intellect; rather, I maintain that both are two effects of the soul, generated by the power produced by the intellect within the form and the image. For this reason, form and image have become the basis of this world. As for al-Sijistani —


<!-- page 28 -->
As for al-Sijistani, he says in his book (Al-Nusra): this argument is delirious, because motion is generated from a specific intention ("to seek something"), and the motion of physical bodies is intended either to fill the void above through the upward movement of flame, or through the downward movement of earth, and so on. From this, al-Kirmani concludes that al-Razi was correct, while al-Sijistani was wrong.

The author of (Al-Islah) says: We hold that motion and stillness are two effects united in the First and the Second. For when the First was creative, it united with creativity, and for that reason the union was affected by power. We called them two effects because creativity and the creative are two: one is the creativity itself and the other is the creator. Yet creativity and the creator, though two, are a single essence, namely the All. These two effects belong first to the First by power, and then are emitted from the First to the Second. Through that emission, two actual effects arose in the Second: one is identical with the resurrection, the other identical with the origin. The two origins are united in matter, and one of the two effects, motion, is higher than the other, stillness, since it is the higher of the two originals — just as the First is higher than the Second.

The third chapter discusses the topic: "Are the soul (al-nafs) and the spirit (al-ruh) one and the same?" This chapter is divided into six sub-chapters and opens with phrases drawn from the book (Al-Nusra), set among the texts cited from the book (Al-Islah), arranged in a way that clarifies their original meaning. Here al-Kirmani says: al-Sijistani criticizes al-Razi for something al-Razi never said.

The author of (Al-Islah), in reply to what the author of (Al-Nusra) had advanced, said: We do not say that nature and the dark skeletons are born from the Second in order to confirm that the Second derives from the First. That higher world is the world of honor and virtue, while this world is the world of darkness and gloom. The Second, though born of the First, resembles Him, and is unlike the case of al-Hayuli. The author of (Al-Nusra) replies: this is a pure mistake, for if that world were illuminated,
- 26

<!-- page 29 -->
there would be no darkness in it, and this world is dark with no light in it, so no resemblance could fall between them.

The fourth chapter, in eight sub-chapters, treats the question of whether souls are parts of the First Truths. Al-Kirmani notes that al-Sijistani alters the meaning of what is stated in the book (Al-Islah) by rearranging its phrases. Al-Kirmani agrees with him on the subject of partial souls, granting that the aggregate of these souls does not constitute the universal soul. Al-Sijistani then returns to speak about this world, alluding to the complete Truths, and says that the human soul belongs to the region between the world of nature and the world of rebirth; it is the compound, known by natural force, and the soul may be raised to the highest world by practicing piety and then by uniting with the Second. Al-Sijistani does not stop there; he says:

The Intellect is the First Resurrection, while the speaking souls are the Second Resurrection. Whatever occurred in the first rebirth is resurrected in the second as well.

Al-Kirmani criticizes this opinion and refers the reader to his own writings, especially to the epistle ((Al-Wa'iza — The Preacher)) and ((Al-Mudi'a — The Illuminating)), in which he says: "The Intellect is nothing but the Word itself, and the Word is nothing but the Intellect itself." He confirms this by citing the saying of our Master, Imam al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah — may God's prayers be upon him — in the book ((Ta'wil al-Shari'a — Interpretation of the Shariah)) (1), where he speaks of the intellects and indicates that the Word is the Intellect and that these names are properly applied to it. Al-Kirmani adds: "In addition, we have already explained and discussed the Creator, the Intellect, the Word, and what may be said of the world of creativity and the First Rebirth in the epistle ((Al-Mudi'a — The Illuminating)) and others. These treatises suffice the researcher."

(1) This book is one of the writings of Qadi al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun al-Maghribi al-Tamimi. Al-Kirmani's reference here to an Imam — i.e., to one classified among them by Imam al-Mu'izz al-Fatimi — indicates that Imam al-Mu'izz is the source of his information.
- 27

<!-- page 30 -->
Al-Kirmani does not stop here. He cites the words of al-Razi as recorded in the book (Al-Islah), where al-Razi says:

Motion and stillness are to the soul as the body and the image are to it; and these last two are like the body of the first two, because the soul is gentler than the body, and the body is denser than the soul. So motion and stillness are gentler than the substance and the image.

It appears that al-Sijistani rejects this analysis, supporting instead the theory of the author of (Al-Mahsul — The Harvest). Al-Kirmani says that both men erred, especially al-Razi, regarding motion. Motion is the action of the mover, which we call the soul. Stillness, by contrast, is the cessation of the mover's action. Therefore, if the mover is the soul, it cannot resemble motion, which is its own action; the same applies to stillness. Moreover, motion and stillness cannot be combined into a single thing, because they alternate as opposites, while the structure and the image are inseparable on the mixed plane and form one thing. This topic has been fully explained in the book ((Rahat al-'Aql — Rest of the Mind)) (2).

The fifth chapter, in seven sub-chapters, treats the theme that the human being is the fruit of the material world. Al-Razi says so, and "we see him as belonging to the whole world, its foundation, and its roots." Al-Hayuli is also the basis of the substrate formed in it by motion, stillness, and certain other factors that lead to the formation of different souls — such as the region and others. Al-Sijistani calls this philosophy the "world philosophy," which does not acknowledge the existence of the soul after its separation from the body. The orientalist ((Goldziher)), in the Islamic Encyclopedia, gives a long explanation of these beliefs

(2) It is very strange to find here that the book Rahat al-'Aql ("Rest of the Mind") was written before Riyad, although al-Kirmani cites Riyad on pages 22, 23 and 364; it occurs to one that the citations in the two books were probably inserted later.
- 28

<!-- page 31 -->
of this category, but without giving a fully decisive answer. It is held that this group follows the school of Democritus and Epicurus, as further pursued by Lucretius. Al-Kirmani says that both schools are right on one side and wrong on another. Man is the result of the interaction of natural-planetary and physical action, and man is the purpose of the Second and the purpose of the work of nature. The meaning is this: man is created and divided into two parts — one part belonging to lifeless nature, and another part belonging to God — and therefore he is the highest of creatures.

We say: how can what he has said be true, when order and arrangement come only from the Intellect? Or how is it correct that universals are made preferable only by the Intellect, while the soul is unaffected by its effects, being on a par with the animals? It is for this reason that God has lifted the pen from the soul until the effects of the Intellect take hold in it. And we have said above that the matter lies in the agents, not in totalities; not every agent seeks, through its action, to achieve a goal or purpose decreed for it. By the same token, the First Intellect itself is not a compound, so it is in order and arrangement, but the Intellect only perceives true perception.

The sixth chapter, in six sub-chapters, rehearses the theme of motion, stillness, and the image. It maintains that what is reported in the book (((Al-Mahsul — The Harvest)) and what Abu Hatim al-Razi and al-Sijistani all transmitted on this score is incorrect, and refers the reader to consult his book ((Al-Masabih — The Lamps)).

Then the author of ((Al-Nusra)) proceeds and undertakes to define body and soul. He defines body by its acceptance of the three dimensions, and then defines the soul as the similarity of the parts, making the soul of the same course as water, fire, and air — each of which is similar in its parts. With this premise, it becomes impossible to say that the soul is not embodied. The soul (al-nafs) and the spirit (al-ruh) are synonymous names of one thing, and that thing is not embodied; for were it a body, it would necessarily possess the nine qualities and accidents that the body requires, and could be apprehended by a sense;
- 29

<!-- page 32 -->
nor would the imagination remain in it as to the nine accidents, or one of them. And when different physical norms hold in it, it must not be a body. Our well-known book ((Al-Masabih — With Lamps)) contains what fully addresses this.

The seventh chapter, divided into seven sub-chapters, deals with the classification of the composite materials of the world. In truth it is dedicated entirely to explaining the status of the human being, who forms a class of his own, and his relationship to all else. For this reason al-Razi says: "Man is a plant that sleeps upon the substances of the world." Al-Sijistani agrees with him on one side, but disagrees on another: the human body disintegrates or dissolves into such substances, and al-Razi classes the human being and likens him to an animal because he possesses the senses and attributes. Al-Sijistani replies that this feeling belongs to the growing vegetative soul. Al-Razi argues that the very speaking human composes a point of difference from the animal, while al-Sijistani counters that the animal too possesses the senses. When al-Kirmani weighs the two opinions, he rules that al-Razi is closer to the truth than al-Sijistani. Finally he says:

The resemblance is not only between man and the material world, but also between man and the spiritual world. The one who is united in himself is such that everything preceded him in the scale of existence, and he displays the analogies between himself and everything in the material and spiritual worlds.

In the world there are different motions, and in the human being there are also six motions corresponding to the six. In the world there is a mover that moves it, and in the human being likewise there is a mover that moves him — namely, the soul. In the world there are the four investigations (i.e., the elements), and in the human being likewise there are the four natures. In the world there are rivers, valleys of different tastes, mountains, and minerals, and in the human being likewise — as the author of (Al-Islah) has shown — there are bones, cartilage, flesh, blood, and what flows from him and issues out of him. In the world there are things that grow and increase without sensation, and in the human being likewise there are things that grow and increase without sensation, such as hair and nails; and his body itself grows and increases from his youth to his old age. In the world there is the sentient animal as well,
- 30

<!-- page 33 -->
and in the human being there are the sentient faculties. The whole world is structures and compositions; it is complete in what exists. From all this comes the analogy with the world — though what we have mentioned is only a summary, which our writers detail under the title "Peace of Mind."

The eighth chapter is divided into twenty-four sub-chapters, and its subject is qada (judgment) and qadar (destiny). These two expressions are mentioned in the Holy Quran and evidence specific synonyms, but both mean (judgment). This topic has had its impact on the long scientific debate that took place in the Islamic era and never reached a decisive result. Thousands of works in Islamic theology have been written on it, but in fact it is a wrong and time-wasting matter to revisit.

Al-Razi, the author of (Al-Islah), says: "In view of the statement of ((Al-Mahsul — The Harvest)) that qada (judgment) agrees with the First and the Preceding, and qadar (destiny) with the Second (i.e., the next) — this is all wrong, because destiny precedes judgment just as the First precedes the Second. The meaning of qadar is estimation — that He made the thing possible — and the meaning of qada is (detail), the separation of the thing or its parts. And the Second, i.e., the manifest qada, cannot be presented over the First." Al-Razi cites several verses from the Holy Quran in support of his interpretation and likens destiny, in detail, to the cloth that is drawn in the tailor's imagination before and after, before being cut. When he has put it into its form — i.e., judges it — it is no longer possible to change it.

Al-Sijistani objects to this explanation in defense of his basic theory, which agrees with what is stated in the book ((Al-Mahsul — The Harvest)). He says that qada does not mean detail, although qadar means estimation; the first describes and obliges, while the second is the judgment, a void and an end. He adds that the tailor analogy does not fit the case, because qada is the idea of the dress that results from the art of tailoring, which is (sewing), and the Second is based on
- 31

<!-- page 34 -->
the soul as the estimation, and so it became destiny. Then al-Kirmani comes and discusses the ideas and opinions. He says: If qada agrees with the First, and the First with the Preceding, and the Following with the Second, then we must admit that the Speaker is suited to the Following, as shown in the book. Although the Speaker is responsible for defining the Shari'a, it is wrong to combine the Speaker and the Following, because the Speaker is concerned with the material world, while the Following — which is the Foundation — is concerned with the spiritual world. The likeness of the Speaker and the Foundation is like the sun and the moon. And here there is a point worth attention: some preachers tended to set the rank of the Foundation above the rank of the Speaker, saying that spiritual worship is higher than legal-natural worship.

The author of (Al-Nusra) reports a story from the scholars: When God Almighty created the Intellect, He emptied the two worlds into it, so that it might be the sum of the images of the two worlds. We say: the Intellect that is the sum of the images of the two worlds is not the First Creative Intellect, nor the Second Intellect, which is the First Emanator; rather, it is the Intellect that ascends from the world of nature by acquisition. In order to parallel the First Emission, a Second Emission was emerging, and thus it became a collection of the images of the worlds, despite their multitude, and it can imprint itself with the images of created things.

The ninth chapter is divided into thirty-three sub-chapters by al-Kirmani. It treats the basis of the principle and the law of Adam, and the laws that came after him, and then proceeds further. In this connection he speaks of the Great Resurrection, saying: The Great Resurrection will come when the doors of teaching are closed, the call that has been perfected ceases, and the Qa'im sets aside the boundaries in the administration of the da'wa. Then it is no longer necessary, wherever a new creation has come.

In this chapter there are also sub-chapters devoted to the question whether Adam, mentioned in the Holy Quran as one of God's interlocutors conveying revelation to humanity and ((the Speaker)) — or, in Ismaili idiom, the one who holds the role — has truly preached the law of
- 32


<!-- page 35 -->
To whom is it given, and how? In this chapter we find no attachment to purely philosophical conjecture — only an effort to collect and explain the few symbols and signs scattered through the Scriptures, and to combine and reconcile those often contradictory references into a convincing theory.

The Christians turned the Adam of the Old Testament — the simple man named in the Torah of the Bible — into a towering figure unlike any other. Islam, alongside a great variety of other views, has inherited this new story of Adam; and of the various groups, the Ismailis came closer to the truth than the rest in defining his role, because they considered him to stand at the very beginning of the world — it being enough that the angels were commanded to prostrate to him in honor. Within the Ismaili system it is acknowledged that the seven speaker-prophets are the first and not the last, so Adam, the first, and the others who follow differ only slightly in their work and their duties: Adam did not bring the closing decree (the *taʿyīn*), and the Qa'im will not come with a new law — which is why Muhammad is taken as the seal of the apostles, and his law as the last.

Al-Kirmani mentions the Great Resurrection because at that time the call (*daʿwa*) will have been completed, the activity of the *ḥudūd* (the ranks of the call) will have ceased, the task of education will have ended, and a new creation will have begun. This idea seems to reverse what is expected from the Ismaili call when it reaches its summit — when the whole world becomes united under their authority and follows a single religious doctrine, namely the true Islamic law that they preach.

The author of *al-Nuṣra*, after his interpretation of God's words in the story of the son of Noah — "I will take refuge on a mountain that will protect me from the water" — goes on to say: "I will seek refuge in him." Then Noah replies: "There is no protector today from the command of God" — meaning that the matter is now received only through the revelation given through those who bear it, for Noah's son did not know the rank of revelation, even if it was a subordinate

The Book of Riyāḍ [3]
- 33

<!-- page 36 -->
rank. Adam already had a revelation for his son so that he might know this.

As for the tenth chapter, which is the last of the book, it is a critique of all the passages cited from the book *al-Maḥṣūl* (*The Harvest*), which Abu Hatim al-Razi left uncorrected before. There are roughly nine passages and fifteen chapters in this section, and we have considered it necessary to provide a translation of the basic passages contained in *al-Maḥṣūl* to give an idea of its style. Al-Kirmani's response in the second section is presented as an example of his method. He says:

In truth, when the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* undertook the propagation of the call and began explaining the foundations and rules of the *daʿwa*, he opened the doors of new departures by writing a number of books for those within it; and the majority accepted his judgment on *al-Maḥṣūl* despite the apology of the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ*. This came as proof of the error and complexity of the subject — and unfortunately the reality of the situation is the opposite of what they saw and imagined. Al-Razi's corrections demonstrate that he did not challenge the value of *al-Maḥṣūl* or its author, neither by exposing faults nor by issuing judgments, because al-Razi was among the men particularly concerned with repairing and elevating religious knowledge, explaining the truth of the faith, and affirming the duties of man toward God.

From the opinions of al-Kirmani recorded in his books, you judge him to be a great author and a writer trained in the manners and the principles. One of his epistles is *al-Mabsam* (*The Preacher*), and from it we can see that he could make an important note and set it down with restraint, where another man would have sent insults and abuse. Here are some of the pieces he corrects:

1. The author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* says, on the subject of monotheism, that God is the Creator of things and other-than-things: of the intelligible, the intellectual, and the rational.
- 34

<!-- page 37 -->
Meaning that such materials or topics may fall under the rule of such elements or may not.

Al-Kirmani replies to this by saying: this contradicts the theory that reason (*ʿaql*) is the foundation of creation, behind which there is no other deity, and that nothing can be prior to the intellect in its existence or alongside the intellect in its existence — for this theory entails an association (*shirk*); it contradicts the law of monotheism. And to speak of creating things and other-than-things, material and non-material, unless one has confused himself and imagined illusions that there is no world other than this one and nothing that negates creation — how then is it possible to say that God is the creative Creator and at the same time deny this?

2. In the fourth chapter, the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says: The Creator made things from nothing alone; there is nothing with Him and He has no end; and if we said only "He and nothing," we would have denied things and other-than-things, and made both ourselves and Him creative. We have said that His Self (His identity), whatever idea it may be, is a simple vehicle (*ṣūra*), and that He made everything continuous and non-continuous, bringing it into this existence by virtue of the infinite and finite effect — because nothing comes after "the thing," and because the negation of a name is itself a name that comes into being once that name exists, and because the word "no" is not an idea but is itself one of the ideas, a negation of every idea. And this is partly *shirk*: he has refuted the theory of monotheism. Al-Kirmani refers the student to his own thesis *al-Rawḍa* and to the book *Tafsīr al-Sharīʿa* by the Fatimid Imam al-Muʿizz li-Dīn Allah.

3. In the eighth chapter, the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says, in the section investigating the First Creator: that God Almighty created the world all at once — meaning that He created the intellect with a single utterance, and by His power and design displayed images within it
- 035

<!-- page 38 -->
of existence, the ideas of the world, and everything it contains, without anything else having priority over it; the intellect has seniority. Al-Kirmani protests against this statement because it implies and decides that the intellect operates independently, relying on its own thinking — and this is a grave error, because the intellect is only a mediator in the divine work and not another creator.

4. The author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says, in the ninth chapter: Eternity and its causality are the unity of God; and the unity of God is eternal. We see al-Kirmani refusing the idea of "eternal unity," regarding it instead as a mediator between God and reason — the second being itself the substance of unity for one primary cause, and at the same time being the first cause of the act of creating and the creature, the perfect and the perfecting, the eternal and the everlasting, the existing and what exists.

5. In chapter 11 the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says: The image is visible in the intellect by way of the unity of God, so the abstract image becomes eternal in it, because the intellect itself becomes wholly eternal and not partial. And al-Kirmani likewise tends to speak about the basic facts even while thinking about the manifestations of the physical world, and suggests that this applies more to the speaker's mind — to the base or the forward — than to the underlying matter. And this is the Nearest Angel, or the Hidden Pen that writes upon the Tablet of existence.

6. The author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says, in chapter twelve: Since the intellect is caused by the eternity which is called "the Word of God," and since there is no third between them, the intellect becomes similar to it. Al-Kirmani objects to this view, saying that the intellect and the Word are one and the same.

7. In the thirteenth chapter the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says that the intellect received the name *Tāmm* (the Complete) because it became the creator, and because creativity was done by the perfect Creator, and that the creator is thereby perfect, which is itself created.
- 36

<!-- page 39 -->
That is, "creativity" can only be complete.

We see al-Kirmani objecting to this and saying: God has no attributes whereby the creator or createdness imposes its superiority over the creature.

8. In the fourteenth chapter the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says: The intellect carries the ideas, and the attic from which it draws is "the Word" exactly — like the sun, which sends out light by its own brilliance and not by any natural friction or material cause. If the work of the intellect is the result of the power of the Word and of the Word's ability to convey it, then it becomes clear that the Word is the cause of everything that proceeds from the intellect, and not the intellect itself; and the second — which is the intellect — is only an intermediary between the Word and the *ḥudūd* (the ranks) beneath it. Here too al-Kirmani objects, saying: such an arrangement applies only to the minds of the speakers.

9. Finally, in the fifteenth chapter the author of *al-Maḥṣūl* says: The act of creativity is a medium between the Creator and the created, and the same is true of the agent who is entrusted with it. He adds: the idea of creativity depends on the agent who influences the image, which is transmitted from the creature to the Creator. The closing section contains scattered sentences that refer to the same topic drawn from different clues. As one would expect, al-Kirmani rejects these statements and says: this does not agree with the belief of the monotheists, because the act of the creature here is taken to be independent of the Creator.

In truth, the idea of monotheism and the understanding of the knowledge of the *ḥudūd* is hard, indeed very hard — and it is by reaching this knowledge that souls survive, grow, and find stability, and that they endure beyond the world of matter.

The author of *al-Maḥṣūl* said: "Creativity is a mediator between the Creator and the created;
- 37

<!-- page 40 -->
it is an effect of the influencer upon the influenced." And he said: "The image of creativity is intermediate between the doer and the effect, its existence proceeding from the side of the creative doer. Therefore the effect of the image is on the side of the creative in the effect, and this image — I mean creativity — has come into the Creative One from the Creative Almighty."

This is the book *al-Riyāḍ* of the great preacher Ḥamīd al-Dīn Aḥmad al-Kirmānī — the sum of the philosophical opinions that were the subject of controversy in his era. Al-Kirmani brought them into the light, drew them with care, refined them, and produced one of the finest philosophical texts in their exposition.

In truth, the author of *al-Riyāḍ* deserves the gratitude of generations and the appreciation of the Islamic world, because his book is a fruit of thought, and a tall tree that grew up in the shadow of this vast world.

**Manuscript and Method of Investigation**

When I was seconded by the Ismaili Scientific Society in India under the great orientalist Prof. W. Ivanow — its secretary — to edit the book *al-Riyāḍ* and supervise its production, he sent me the first copy, the original, designated by the letter (A) and used as the base copy for the edition. After examining it and finding it full of grammatical errors as well as missing and corrupted passages, I was prompted to write back, apologizing that it would certainly not be possible to realize the project and reach the goal of producing a correct text unless it could be checked against at least one other copy. Almost a month passed, and then he sent me another copy, photographed on microfilm from Tehran, which is designated by the letter (B). It served me greatly, because it came to me well-set and reaching its target,
- 38

<!-- page 41 -->
and is more correct than the first — although the first has the more beautiful handwriting and layout, as the photographs show.

**Copy History**

At the end of copy (A) the following is recorded:
The first day of the month of Rabīʿ al-Thānī of the year 1354 of the Hijra, corresponding to the second of August 1935 — in the hand of Adam bin Sheikh al-Majid Muhammad Ali al-Sūrati of India.

Copy (B) is likewise Indian, and at its end it says:
The branches of its copies, on Wednesday the twenty-eighth of the month of Shawwāl in the year 1355 of the Prophet's Hijra. The name of the scribe is not mentioned in it, regrettably.

**Final Word**

This is now added to the series of historical and philosophical books that I have edited — in another work, *al-Riyāḍ* of al-Kirmani. I can do no more than record here a word of thanks and gratitude to Professor V. Ivanow, in recognition of what he has done and continues to do in service of scholarship, striving to make as many Ismaili manuscripts as possible available, correctly edited, to the world of being — all for one specific purpose that does not go beyond introducing the Islamic world to this cosmological philosophy, which has occupied minds from time immemorial, and has been the focus of the attention of scholars and the orbit of their researches in the East and the West.

Aref Tamer
Beirut – Lebanon
1960

The fragment touches on the landmarks of the Hanifi religion, the light of guidance set against the fires of polytheism and *shirk*, and the way that the established structures of polytheism have been worn down and forgotten over time. It speaks of one who set out as a striver (a *mujāhid*) — not under the shade of garments or in darkness — against those who had taken to worshipping rivers, idols, and stones in the name of devotion. Accordingly, (1) *Tawakkali* — may God grant mercy — *Labis N*.

Praise be to Allah, Lord of fathers and mothers, Creator of animals and plants, who praises — to Him belongs all that is in the earth and the heavens, and He bestows favor upon His creation with blessings.

Beings have no existence except by His command, and beings have no existence except by His decree. His destiny is firm, so that the minds do not deny it, and it endures, so that it does not suffer from it.

From God, to His might, His greatness and His grace have been extinguished, and by His command the blessings have overflowed. By His ear, the earth and the heavens were raised; the misguided and the doubtful were lost, and they went astray far away.

I praise Him, thank Him, believe in Him, and I do not disbelieve in Him; and I bear witness that there is no god but God — the true idol who created all things with their different essences, from simple *Dhu Sana'a wa Jalal*, and *Nadhid*, who is your love and perfection.
