<!-- page 50 -->
In its sanctuary an idol stood; the landmarks of the Hanifi (1) religion were threatened; the fire of disbelief and atheism was burning, the edifices of polytheism and idolatry had been raised, and the people had sunk into the depths of misguidance and grown blind in the blindness of adversity. So he rose up for Allah as a diligent striver and contended on their behalf with what was harder than clothing and darkness. He shattered the idols and the images they had been worshipping, and he began to call them to the worship of the Most Merciful and to your rites of faith and Islam — replacing the houses of fire with the Sacred House of Allah, abrogating the rule of the crosses by turning toward the Corner (*al-Rukn*) and the Station (*al-Maqam*), guiding the servants of Allah in His worship onto the straight path, saving them from the claws of the seductive devil, and bringing them by his blessing to the trustworthy refuge. He led them to the courtyard of guidance and meeting, until the word of monotheism shone bright, and the word of *shirk* was humiliated and dimmed, and the tongue pronounced clear verses, and the wind drove the ships of salvation across the seas of dispute. So came the ruling of Allah Almighty (3) in what He willed to afflict the people of opinions and religions — may Allah bless him and grant him peace.

He has a prayer that bears witness for his followers in general, and for the preachers in particular — that He may make easy for them a day on which neither wealth nor children will be of any use, except for him who comes to Allah with a sound heart. And the way of his intercession, and the right to follow him and obey him, will be denied to them (those who do not).

And upon *Qutb al-Din* (the Axis of the Religion) and the pillar of the believers — may his lights meet them — and upon the Guardians (*al-Awliya*) by command of his followers and supporters; and upon *al-Kazim* (4), the suppressor of anger in fulfillment of the covenant of Allah Almighty, the patient one under the humiliation of usurpation for the sake of seeking the face of Allah Almighty — the Commander of the Believers, *Ali bin Abi Talib* — whose exploits are innumerable and whose virtues are without count. And upon their descendants, the righteous Imams (5), whose lights immortalize souls, by whose obedience hope is realized, and by whose intercession perfection is attained,

3 — In copy (A) the word (Almighty) has been dropped.
4 — The *f* of affection in copy (b) is omitted.
5 — In copy (a) it came (which).

<!-- page 51 -->
(1) the ruling Imam (2), the Commander of the Faithful and his fathers, the Guardians of the Faithful — the family of *Taha* and *Yasin*, peace be upon them all.

After this: the most deserving matter for the monotheist is to direct his attention and devote his efforts toward what leads to the Unity of Allah Almighty and the knowledge of His limits — toward a settled system of certain belief, and toward the calling that disperses the confusion (3) and the darkness (4) from the souls of the believers. He also touched upon the unity of the Lord of the Worlds.

When I saw that Shaykh Abu Hatim al-Razi (5), may Allah have mercy on him, had set out to correct in the book *al-Mahsul* (*The Harvest*) (7) what he claimed had been corrupted, and that Shaykh Abu Ya'qub al-Sijzi (al-Sijistani) (8), may Allah have mercy on him, had supported the author of *al-Mahsul* and judged his statement to be correct — I bore witness that what they disputed about was not among the branches in which disagreement is permissible while the soundness of the principles is preserved. And I found Shaykh Abu Ya'qub al-Sijzi, may Allah have mercy on him, in some of what he stated, sincere in the way he intended his rebuttal, yet prejudiced against Shaykh Abi Hatim, may Allah have mercy on him, in some cases — and some (9) of their words were issued in another order. And nevertheless, there was in the book *al-Mahsul*, from the words of its author in the chapter on monotheism — concerning the First Intellect in particular, not its branches — what it was incumbent upon Shaykh Abu Hatim, may Allah have mercy on him (10), to correct and to address. And who was more deserving

than him to address the long passages of his own book? Yet he left it and neglected it. All of that aggregated to make the people of the Guiding Daʿwa angry whenever they stood upon a point of difference — in your paths of *Tawhid* and (1) the knowledge of the limits of imbalance. So I thought to quote the sayings of each one of them in the book *al-Islah* (2) — that is, *al-Islah* (*The Reform*) — and the corresponding passage in the book *al-Nusra* (3), contrasting their objects, and speaking about what is necessary concerning them, what is meant by them, and the face by which it is correct to attribute their meanings to their authors — so that (4) the truth of one may be distinguished from the other, and the prejudices of the rebellious among them may appear, and (5) the meaning of what they differed about may be understood, so that one may then reflect upon it. Then I will speak about what was neglected, of correction in the book *al-Mahsul* (*The Harvest*), among the foundations in which disagreement is not permissible — and I will show the truth in pursuit of reward. So I did this, and gathered all of it in this book, and titled it the book *al-Riyad* (*The Gardens*) — On the Ruling Between the Two (6): the companion of *al-Islah* and the companion of *al-Nusra* — because in what it collects of their sayings and what it quotes (7), it is a separation between them, and a statement of what they continued to err in and what (8) they neglected to correct in the book *al-Mahsul*; and a fulfillment of the duty in it, from what we have benefited of the blessings of the *Awliya* of Allah upon them peace — because it is (10) like *Riyad* (gardens): a vegetable in the eye, a fruit that comes into the hands. And Allah is our helper, and He will guide him in His land in bringing forth the thing (11) from the effects of error, even when we are unaware of it.

The book gathers ten chapters, which together include one hundred and fifty-seven sub-chapters. And Allah is the Helper; He is the Protector and the Trustee.

**Chapter Two:** (1) On what is spoken in the chapter on the First Intellect, which is the First Creator. It includes nine sub-chapters.

**Chapter Three:** What he spoke about (2) in the chapter on the soul and the spirit, and whether they resemble the former or not. It includes six sub-chapters.

**Chapter Four:** Speech about the chapter on the souls as parts and effects. It includes eight sub-chapters.

**Chapter One**

From the book *al-Riyad*, on the ruling between the hunters, in the chapter on what he spoke about the soul, which is the first emanator.

**Section One**

The author of *al-Islah* said: the soul is complete in itself, because it was emitted from the Intellect. The First is perfection, which is the emancipation of the perfect, because the Intellect is perfect.

The author of *al-Nusra* said concerning the soul: he does not know that perfection is better and more complete than the perfect — because (1) perfection is an attribute, while the perfect is what is described; perfection is portable, while the perfect is the carrier; and the described and the bearer are better and more complete than the attribute and what is borne. For the perfect is described by perfection, and bears it; and the perfect is the attribute of the perfect (2), which is predicated upon it — this is what they say.

And we say: what is meant by the owner of *al-Islah* in saying that the soul is complete is that it is emitted — not that perfection is an attribute or no attribute, or (3) that perfection is described or not.

1 — Came in copy (a) as (if).
2 — In year (a) it was mentioned as (to complete).
3 — Inserted (*f*) without (a) in copy (a):
- 53

<!-- page 56 -->
described. Rather what he means is that the perfect is like the cause for which the existence of the effect (occurs). This is because he saw that the perfect is the cause of perfection, and that he is superior to it in rank — for were it not for the perfect, perfection would not be found, as is evidenced by the fact that if perfection is lifted in the imagination, the perfect is lifted (1) with its lifting, like the sources from which the past action, the future, and the subject are emitted; and the effect, if it is lifted, is lifted by the lifting (of its cause). What then is the way to find the First Intellect as the first existent? It was from him that I found —

He said: It is the perfection, and what he called the emanating soul is like the effect. He said: it is complete. And if the intention of the author of *al-Islah* was to say that the First Intellect is truly perfect — and if the matter is really as the author of *al-Nusra* said in this way — then they are prejudiced against the author of *al-Islah*.

**Section Two**

Then the author of *al-Nusra* said: the perfect is better than perfection. Although the words are in this place about the First Principle, which is the First Effect, the First Revival, and not about others, the honor (of being more) must be conceded to the effect — not perhaps because the perfect being perfect was only by perfection, without which it would not have been complete; and if it (perfection) is a thing, then that thing is its cause; and if that is (2) so, then the perfect is the cause of perfection, and perfection is the effect of the perfect. And if the perfect is the cause of perfection, and perfection is the result of the perfect, then he has given honor and credit to the perfect over perfection. It then becomes obligatory for the disabled to be more honorable than what disabled him — and this is impossible. For what was its existence by the existence of (another) — if (the other) had risen, its existence would have risen by its rising. So that existence, by which it exists, is below it in honor and progress. And if that is impossible, then the owner of *al-Nusra* in what he mentioned was prejudiced against the owner of *al-Islah*.

4 — The *f* of affection appeared in copy (A).
5 — Dropped in copy (A).

<!-- page 57 -->
**Section Three**

The author of *al-Nusra* said: the perfect is an attribute, perfection is what is described; the perfect is a portrayal, perfection is a carrier. This is an indication that the perfect is more honorable than perfection, and that the pregnant (the carrier) is more honorable than the carried.

We say: this is not absolutely true in our existence, in terms of the universes and emission. The Second is what is carried, and it is more honorable than the bearer. And that is like the power of the sting, which is (1) the proximate cause of the existence of the developing force and is its bearer; and the developing force (2) is a portable image in it, and is its complement — and the developing is more honorable than that. And like the developing force, which is the proximate cause of the existence of the sensual force, which is its bearer; and the sensuality is a portrayal of it and its complement — and the sensuality is more honorable than that. And like the sensory power, which is the proximate cause of the existence of the speaking force, which is its bearer; and the speaking force is a portable image in it, and is its complement.

And the honor of this is not like the honor of *Hatik* (that case), because it is impossible — from the point of view of creativity and the First Revival — that the First Intellect should be a carrier and the Second Intellect, which is emitted from it, a bearer; or that the perfect (which is the First Intellect) should be an attribute of the perfect (which is the Second Intellect). And if this is so, then the inference of the owner of *al-Nusra* in the rebuttal of what he mentioned was a prejudice against the owner of *al-Islah*. And how could the meaning of what he said in it be true — unless he is dependent on the natural things, which are far from what (3) the author of *al-Islah* is speaking of, namely the creative and emulsive principles?

1 — In copy (B) it is mentioned as (which is).
2 — In version (B) it came as (and the powers).
3 — In year (b) it came as (what he is talking about).

<!-- page 58 -->
The statement of the author of al-Islah is correct with regard to the world of origination and emanation; and the statement of the author of al-Nusra — though the author of al-Islah is mistaken about it — is correct with regard to the world of bodies and their accidents.

Chapter Four

We say, in consideration of this:

"Perfection" is a name applied to the First — which is the First Cause and the First Perfection — and also to the First Effect, which is completed by the addition of its existence to what is above it. Its existence and its completion (1) come about by being added to that which is above it, as in the case of origination and the originated.

On this scheme, the originated is not something that lacks origination; rather, it is that which has the originator as its very essence. It is the same thing: if its existence is added to what is above it, it becomes a creation; and if it is added in respect of the character of the event itself, it is originative.

For the First Intellect is Perfection itself, and it is perfect in truth — but only inasmuch as it deserves to be that which abstains from existing [as derivative]. The perfect is that which has nothing outside its own quality, as with the existence (2) [found] in nature, and likewise in origination. The body, too, is a name applied to that which is perfect in its three dimensions and has nothing of its kind outside itself; whatever resembles it derives from it, and such instances are few. The Sun, likewise, is complete: nothing exists outside it that is like it in quality or kind.

The claim that the existence of something subordinate to the First Intellect is imperfect [holds] — yet that deficiency is not in itself; in addition to what is above it, all things are perfect in

1 — Copy (A) reads "and its stature."
2 — Copy (B) reads "as it exists."
3 — Copy (B) reads "what was . . ."
- 56

<!-- page 59 -->
themselves, because their existence comes from the Perfection that is the First Intellect, and there is no deficiency in them in themselves. If they were deficient in themselves, their existence would have been impossible by the proofs (1) [already given]. It is also impossible that anything pertaining to the deficient should require the perfection of its own self — because the deficient is impossible — just as it is impossible that the existence of something not its own should be ascribed to it. Rather, deficiency is necessary for it only when it is added in relation to what is above it in rank, and not in itself: because the self (2), insofar as it is the subject of a single thing — like numbers, which are perfect in themselves yet diminish only when added to others — exists from what is above it.

Like all the beings of the world, [the Intellect] is complete and lacks nothing where (3) they are subjects; it is only incomplete with respect to their ordering in forms, and the difference among forms when some forms are more noble than others — such as (4) the human being, who is more noble than all the forms. In itself, it is as perfect as the others; it is deficient only in relation to those things above it. And if this is the case in the physical (5) realm, then the manner of contact applies likewise: a body shifts on this scheme in respect of rank, by way of analogy.

And therefore Allah said: "We will show them Our signs in the horizons and in their own selves until it becomes clear to them that it is the truth." (The verse.) That is, We will establish for them the Imams and the limits, in the gross and the subtle, so that the state of the visible bodies and the souls whose actions exist [through them] may be apprehended; so that what they had failed to know — the limits of Allah, which are the truth — becomes clear (6) to them through the form (7).

Chapter Five

The author of al-Islah said:

What is in need of perfection is the activity of the soul, not the soul itself, because the soul's activity can only occur in time, and the coming-to-life of the soul occurs in time — just as the First Intellect and time are one, since the First Originator is origination, and time and perfection are (1) one. The Creator created all the [originated principles] at once; and the soul, even though it needs to derive benefit from the First, is therefore not in itself a diminution, and the First was not deficient — though [the soul] is in need of [its] command. Completion and deficiency (2) appear in the action of the soul, not in itself.

The author of al-Nusra said:

He did not realize that the Intellect does not stand in need of anything in the way that the soul needs to derive benefit from the Intellect. The relation of the Intellect to matter is not like the relation of the soul to the Intellect, since matter, from the standpoint of the Intellect, is nothing but the Intellect itself — their identity is one identity. It is impossible to say that a thing needs what it itself is; need for one thing in relation to another arises only when the other is something distinct. This is what they have said.

We say:

The statement of the author of al-Nusra is a response to what the author of al-Islah maintained — that the Intellect needs matter only. And since this discourse rests on principles that emerge from the natural world, the author of al-Nusra in this matter is closer to the truth than the author of al-Islah; and what deserves to be said is that [the soul] needs perfection, and that this can be brought about only in time, because it is an emanation from the natural world and not from outside it.

1 — Copy (B) reads "(not)."
2 — Copy (B) reads "the soul."
- 58

<!-- page 61 -->
They are the souls that do not attain the degree of perfection — that is, the ascent to the second degree of perfection occurs only in time, and their existence is the existence of emanation. So if the statement of the author of al-Islah is taken in this sense, it is correct.

Chapter Six

The statement of the author of al-Islah — that "Intellect and time are one, because the [First] Originator is origination, and time and perfection are the same" — is not true, because time is the same as the number of motions of the celestial sphere: the imagined duration between the motion of the first degree from it and the motion of what follows from another degree to where the first had been. Origination, which is the Intellect, and its perfection precede the celestial sphere whose degrees' motion brings about the existence of time; and the existence of time is bound up with the existence of the First Intellect. His belief contradicts what is established in the law of the guiding Da'wa. We have shown from [our account of] the universe that time, and what precedes it in existence, are indicated also by the law of the Prophet, in our book known as Peace of Mind (1) — and that suffices for the seekers.

Chapter Seven

The author of al-Nusra said:

If the Intellect did not deny divinity of itself, the next [being] would not have moved its knowledge beyond the level of the Intellect to the Creator. What deficiency is more evident than this — that he knows the Originator only by way of the First?

We say:

1 — Peace of Mind, one of the books of the late preacher Sayyidina Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani (Hujjat al-Iraqayn), edited by Drs. Mustafa Helmy and Mohamed Kamel Hussain and published by the Ismaili Cultural Society in Pompeii, India.
- 59

<!-- page 62 -->
That [belief] is not permissible, because the power active in all the noble beings of the world of unity gives them the knowledge that they have a Creator. Without that power they would not come to be, nor persist, nor pass away. For this reason, every thing is called One; and if it is multiplied in itself, its share of that power remains hidden, while justice stands manifest. It is impossible to imagine that a single individual of the human kind does not know that it has a Creator, or that it fantasizes that it has no Creator. As for the Greater Limit, we say: he apprehends — that is, his very existence apprehends — that he is [the reflection of] the True, even if he is in himself and is his own master. If we apply to the First Intellect what [al-Nusra] mentioned, and to the Next what he said [of the Next], then it is obligatory that [the Next] be the basis for his establishment in the world of the body, being the next in the world of reason. For if it were not for the Speaker, one would not know that one has a God. That is not the case: each of them has a share from Allah, but the Speaker advances in rank, because he is close to the First and is later than him. And so Allah said: "It is not for the sun to overtake the moon, nor does the night outstrip the day; and each is swimming in an orbit." All (1) [are] of one kind, and of differing ranks.

Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said:

And [a further indication] of the deficiency [of the First Intellect] is that all the first ones [the earlier philosophers] held themselves [to the view] (2) that forms are always longing for matter, and matter has no subsistence apart from forms.

We say:

His statement that the soul is the form of the True, and his statement that the Intellect is like body and matter — since the discourse was on the First Intellect — was a [misguided] objection against the author of al-Islah.

1 — Copy (B) reads "and all."
2 — Copy (B) reads "and I longed."
- 60

<!-- page 63 -->
[For] in holding al-Islah to that, [we say:] "intellect" is a shared name, and what [al-Nusra] said is not true of what is supra-natural — namely, the first creative and emanating Intellects — but is true in the world of nature regarding the other intellects: intellect-in-potentia, the intellect [moving] from potentia [to actuality], and the intellect-in-act. If the discourse was on the supra-natural, then he has carried what was said of the natural onto what was supra-natural, and his contradiction was prejudicial. He said: "Forms are always longing for matter, and matter has no subsistence apart from forms." This is incorrect, because forms do not exist as longing for matter (1) for the sake of matter, nor are they that which matter acquires; rather, it is matter that yearns for forms in order to exist — for the sake of the forms — and matter is acquired in addition to forms of inferior nature (2) and to the more noble form (3). And if that is so — and the soul has run the course of form, and the Intellect has run the course of matter, and it has been ruled that matter has no subsistence apart from forms (4) — then it is as if he has made it necessary that the existence of the Intellect be in the Soul. How then can the belief stand that [the Intellect's] existence was an originative existence? Or how does he imagine the Intellect to be the cause of the existence of the Intellect and the Soul, while the discourse is on the supra-natural, which comes out of the world of nature? No — it is known that the Next has its existence after, not before; its existence is therefore [posterior]. This is a manifest fallacy, intended only to make him believe what is said about the natural philosophers.

As for what is in the world of origination and the first emanations, the matter of the originated principles (5) is not from a [class of] nature; it is from the Intellect, and from the Soul, which is like the second emanation — except for what he has mentioned, and from this standpoint it is correct (saheeh).

Chapter Nine

On Emanation

The author of al-Nusra said:

The Intellect is the first mean, the Soul is the second mean, and the first mean is closer to the Cause than the second mean. The proximity and rank of one thing to another can arise only from perfection and nobility, and distance can arise only from lack and absence of nobility.

We say: (2) This statement is not true with regard to the creative and emanative Intellects.

First, being a mean requires two ends; and it is not admissible to say that the First Intellect is a mean unless its existence be in such a state that on one side it has an end, and on the other side too it has an end, so that it stands in a similar state in existence on both sides. How then is it permissible to say of that which is the first of the firsts (3) that it is a mean, when nothing precedes it in existence such that it might be intermediate between that and the two [terms]? Even to suppose that the First Intellect is a mean is impossible, because its existence is a first existence, and what is a first existence is not called a mean. Even the mean deserves the name of mean only by virtue of having beyond it something of its own kind of existence, so that there is an affinity between the two; and likewise something later than it, suitable to it in some respects: an affinity with that one from one face, and with this one from another face — so that it has two faces and is intermediate between the two. As for the First Intellect, there is no affinity at all (4) between it and its Creator, and it is not of the kind of his existence — so it is

3 — Copy (B) reads "(mean)."
3 — Omitted in copy (1).
4 — Copy (1) reads "negation."
5 — Most of its mention is omitted in copy (1).
- 62

<!-- page 65 -->
not [a mean] between it and what is below it; nor is there occasion of [its being a mean] between it and its Creator. As for that which is between it and its Creator that caused it to exist, it is something else; and what is said to be the mediator between it and what is below it is the very essence of origination — the Cause that is said to be the originating [Cause] — as we have shown in the Epistle (The Illuminated) and in our well-known book Peace of Mind. If that is the case, then there is no sense in the inference of the author of al-Nusra in what he said here, for what he has already advanced concerning deficiency and nobility is sufficient. Deficiency is necessary [for the First Intellect] only in addition to its predecessor, not in itself: it is of a rank that is not deficient and that possesses perfection, while in relation to its predecessor it lacks rank — as with the Speaker, the Foundation, the Sun, and the Moon. Each of these — like the Foundation and the Moon — is perfect in itself, lacking nothing in it (1); yet when added to the other that is its counterpart form — the Speaker and the Sun — it is deficient in rank. The author of al-Nusra is forced into [saying that] deficiency arises through the addition to what precedes; and the author of al-Islah is forced into [saying that perfection arises] through itself, because it emanates from the Perfection that is its Cause. So saying this on his [al-Islah's] side is correct, and saying that is also true on its own face; but the author of al-Nusra takes the author of al-Islah from where he did not mean and not from his actual intent.

Chapter Ten

The author of al-Nusra said:

If he says, "I am not saying that the author of al-Islah means the Intellect is like the Soul in the chapter on perfection," we say to him: if it is better than it or less than it, then he should not say [that the two are alike]; rather, by his own reasoning, he ought to say that [the Soul] is deficient.

We say:

The author of al-Islah may not say what the author of al-Nusra takes him to assert. He has said: all things are perfect in themselves, and nothing

1 — Copy (B) reads "in it."
-- Yל -


<!-- page 66 -->
There is a deficiency in it only in addition to that which is higher than it, so it has a degree of rank.
What is not found for this additive — and if it is related to that — is the aspect of
prejudice against him.

Chapter Eleven

The author of al-Nusrah said:
One of the clearest indications of the deficiency of the soul and the integrity of the intellect is the saying of Allah:
"And of His signs are the night, the day, the sun, and the moon."
He set them upon two bases, the sun and the moon upon two bases, so the sun is upon the first
and the moon is upon the next. There is no dispute among the people of knowledge and wisdom that the moon
draws light from the sun — less light and luminance — and that it benefits from the light
of the sun, while the sun benefits it with light. This is what he said.

We say:
It is clear that the moon is of lesser light than the sun, and of lesser luminance, and
that it benefits from the sun in light, and this decrease obliges the addition of it to
the sun. Yet the self is not deficient in itself, for if it were incomplete, it would not have been received from the sun.
When the self is complete, it accepts from it; and if it is so,
then the saying of the author of al-Islah concerned the integrity of the self, and the saying of the author of al-Nusrah concerned
its deficiency in its conditions and its symptoms which are perfect in it — there is a difference between the two statements.
And the author of al-Islah became like one wronged by being prejudiced against, in what was not from
his purpose, in what he says, nor in whom he intends.

Chapter XII

The author of al-Nusrah said:
It is also indicative of the deficiency of the soul that the wise men likened the intellect to
- 64

<!-- page 67 -->
the soul for the soul (1), and they made the soul itself like the body for the intellect. They did so
when they learned of the deficiency of the soul and the perfection of the intellect; so they likened the soul to the imperfect thing
and likened the intellect to the perfect thing.

We say:
That which he added by this saying is a statement of what we have said — that the deficiency is not in itself, but
in other respects, even though the meaning of his words is only directed to the universes
of the second revival. The benefit is only for the souls that are in the world of nature,
in order to reach their second (2) perfection. In itself (3) it is wholly complete, but it is incomplete in some respect.
For it is one of the senses by which (4) the soul's stream runs through the body. As for the second,
which is the first resurrection, it is not the way of these souls, because its power and completeness
were given at the beginning of its existence — not through time and benefit, but only by a lesser degree relative to that which precedes it
in rank, in progression and in lag.

Chapter XIII

The author of al-Nusrah said:
If there is no deficiency in the soul except that nature is upon its horizon, then
the soul has two extremities, one toward the intellect and one toward nature. It is not so
with the intellect (whose two extremities are toward the Word on one side and the Soul on the other)(5).
Nothing clung to the dark nature out of its perfection and completion, even if it clung to nature
as a part of the soul, because nature falls upon its horizon — when it contemplates it,

Kitab al-Riyad [5]

<!-- page 68 -->
the observer (1) stood upon the fact of the deficiency of the soul and the perfection of the intellect. This is what he said.

We say:
This statement is made irregularly, because the occurrence of nature upon the horizon
of that which he called soul does not earn it a deficiency, but rather indicates its completeness — for
its movement, because it is incomplete in addition to it, and it is in addition to her a complete, honorable thing
by its competence in what nature is not specialized in, along with its subjectivity. I mean by this
the self and nature as the two perfections in terms of their emission from perfection. He said:
one is toward the Word and the other toward the Soul — for the intellect is not anything other than
the Word, nor is the Word anything other than the intellect. Rather, the self is one, the eye is
one (2), and the names that are scattered are worthy of meanings, as has been advanced from the saying
in this sense. When mentioning the term "average" is not hidden, he said: why is it not so —
nothing clings to the dark nature because of its perfection and completion, while it has clung to nature
as a part of the soul because nature falls upon its horizon. It is not, therefore, the next that clings
to nature in love, but it is nature that loves it, and it is the one that moves
toward it and its communication. The belief in that, and the perception that the next clung to a part of itself
by nature, is wrong; and the best way to dismiss the meaning of what he said is to interpret that his purpose was
to say that it overflowed over it, so that its overflow would be called "clinging."

Chapter Fourteen

The author of al-Islah said:
What is imperfect is the action of the soul, not the soul itself; rather it completes its action, not its self.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

(2) In copy (B) it is stated with addendums.
- 66

<!-- page 69 -->
This statement is contradictory, because if the action is incomplete, it must be either
from the deficiency of the subject itself, or from the deficiency of the substance from which the verb is
made, or the deficiency of both. If the deficiency of the action is a deficiency
of the doer itself, then he has said that the soul is lacking because it is the doer — although
because of the lack of matter, it has been proved to be an eternal substance, and this is a departure from the doctrine of religion.
He is inclined to the doctrine of (Abu Zakariyya al-Razi) (1) and those who said that it is eternal.
The author of this book (2) imagined that he was inclined to do so, because he made
time eternal alongside the intellect, and that is the lobe of his doctrine. Even if the lack of action is from
both, he said it; and he said that the soul is the doer.
He said, "Eternity of the soul," and whenever he said it, he said: "This is what he said."

We say:
The statement of the author of al-Islah and the statement of his purpose in it, said of deficiency and perfection,
may apply. The saying of the author of al-Nusrah in what he said is fallacy, prejudice, and departure from
the limit of knowledge. Because he first said — after he divided the matter into the three divisions
mentioned above — that if the deficiency of the action is from the doer itself, and the doer
is the soul, then the soul is incomplete. The author of al-Islah has clarified that the deficiency is not
incumbent upon the soul itself, since that is an act in the sense of its effect; so be rid of these.
Secondly, he said that if the deficiency is from the substance itself, then it has been proved to be
eternal — for the perfection and completion, which are the first and the second, by the proof of their eternity, are eternal
primally. So how is it minus? If so, then the decrease is only (3) in the substances
that accept its completeness, not in what accepts their completeness, as is famous in naturalism.
The decrease occurred from the side of the recipients, not from the side of what is accepted from it, as heat

Chapter XV

The author of al-Nusrah said:
As for his claim that the soul has become complete because it emanated from the first intellect —
which is perfect, and it is not permissible for what emanates from perfection to be other than perfect — this is not true.
Because the soul does not have to be complete because it emanates from the perfect intellect; even if
this were obligatory — if the emanating of the soul were also complete, which is the form and image,
and the image is also complete — until it is communicated to the people,
the undivided singular is all complete. It follows from this saying that all
the world is perfect, and there is no deficiency in it, nor in any of it.

We say:
The product of his saying is that if the soul were complete by being emanating from the first intellect,
which is the whole, then the form and image emanating from the soul would be complete as well.
The same applies to what was emanated from all the forms and images that he claimed to be people —
all that is in the world is complete, and there is no deficiency in it. We have shown in the above what the author of
al-Islah said in saying that the soul is perfect, and the statement of the author of al-Nusrah in this chapter (3)

It is indivisible; all people are fragmented into their organs, and the hyacinths are emitted

Chapter Sixteen of Chapter One

Then, when (3) the basis of debt and its losses was upon the balance of assets and their comparisons,
to be an indication of their order upon the assets — from the limits that are invisible to the sight —
the existence of the Book and the Shari'ah was from the side of the speaker, and the establishment of the foundation was a source of inspiration
from the point of view of the speaker. This was a proof that what exists is from the first intellect,
who is in his world like the speaker in the world of the body. But he is the next, which is the soul
and the Hayuli together, like the Book and the Foundation, which exist on the side of the speaker, testify to this —
to what the Prophet did in combining the Book and the 'Itrah which they had from him when
he said: "Indeed, I leave among you the two weighty things: the Book of Allah and my 'Itrah, a rope extended from the sky
to the earth, one end of it in the hand of God and one end in your hands. Hold fast to them, and you will not
go astray as long as you hold to them. I have asked my Lord to answer me concerning them at the basin." Like these
he combined the two rosaries from all his hands and said: I do not say such two,
with his rosary fingers and the middle of his hand, one of them preceding the other.
A helper to me is a helper, and their weakness is mine. He likened them to existence with his fingers.

The two rosaries — because (1) one does not precede the other — so he was disposed in the realm of bodies. Chapter Seventeen of Part One

Then we say:
We have said (4) that the First Intellect does not have alongside it anything of the type of its own existence — for example — because its existence is not generalized to it except as the existence of the First, and the First is not other than one. Otherwise the paths of monotheism would be disrupted. If it had an analogue in its existence (5), then their existence together would not be one, and one would not precede the other, and this is required by what precedes them. What their existence means is that which is restrained from its own existence. The existence of duality is a multiplicity of selves, and the multiplicity of selves does not arise from the multiplicity of souls; rather, its existence is from that which precedes it from the One — specifically on one side, and full of meanings on the other. The One specifically, which is multiplied

in meanings, its existence not being in itself but in others, is not multiplied — neither in meanings nor in itself. Thus it has become clear that if the First Intellect had alongside it something of the type of its existence — such as two accidents about one preceding it — then the existence of that one would be because it is constrained in meanings, not in itself, but in what precedes it and renders it one. And since nothing is above the Intellect, it is not possible that it have an analogue of its existence. Thus it has been proven that it is one. As for the proof that the Intellect is one — there being two things about it, namely the Soul — if the existence of the Soul and the supposed phantoms together rests on what we have already shown, then it has appeared that the claim of the Soul's appearance is wrong.

Chapter Eighteen of Part One

This is what the author of al-Nusra is committed to in speaking about the deficiency of the Illuminated [Intellect] and the Image, and the deficiency of all that is in the world.

We say:
The Intellect and the Image are perfect, and the world is perfect. Its parts are complete in themselves, as evidenced by the fact that the perfect is that outside of which there is nothing of the type of its own existence. The world, which is the sum of the celestial spheres, and the image assembled from the parts of the body — and the body in itself is perfect in its three dimensions — has nothing obliging its limitation unless it is outside it. If it is outside, then it is complete; and if it is complete, then the position of the author of al-Nusra — that the world is complete — is rebutted, and the impossible follows. If among his words there are passages that prejudice him, it is enough to cite what he said in his book known as al-Ikhtiyar, in the chapter of "the command," where there is an indication of the perfection of the world. He said: "The image of the dispensation of the world in its entirety is nothing else, and wisdom suffices with what is in the world's totality — rich and perfect." I do not know how he went on to contradict that; perhaps he classified the book al-Ikhtiyar after the book al-Nusra. As for the disparity that exists in the parts of the world, by which the author of al-Nusra is obliged to claim deficiency — that arises from the addition of some of them
- 72

<!-- page 75 -->
to others in terms of their images and conditions, which is the First Perfection that exists. Some parts of the world have been given their First Perfection, and the second is the second First; and some have been given their Second Perfection, which is the last of the acquisitions. As speech has resulted, the deficiency that the author of al-Nusra speaks of belongs rather to the later states of natural selves which appear when they are added to one another. The Perfect that he speaks of — the author of al-Islah — is the (1) self whose existence is first and original.

Chapter Nineteen of the First Chapter

The author of al-Nusra said:
If it is necessary for the Soul to emanate from the Intellect — which is the Perfect — then the Soul has a stature, because it is also necessary for the Soul to emanate from the Intellect, which is the first intermediate and the first creator; so it must be intermediate like the First and creative, such that the Soul attains the virtues that the Intellect obtains, as does the whole world. This is what people say.

We say:
The statement that it is impossible to say the Intellect is the first intermediate has already been advanced. In sum, what he has mentioned is a corrupt issue. For if something arises from another (2) — one being a cause and the other an effect — it is not necessary that the effect share with it in all its conditions, such that its very identity would be its identity; for then nothing could be said of it except what is said of that particular identity. And when it is more original, and when it is said that it is found about it just as it was, nor when it must share with it in its conditions — it must share with it in some of its conditions; for if it does not share with it in some of its conditions,
1 — In copy (A) it came: "in the year of the self."
2 — Dropped in copy (B).
- 73

<!-- page 76 -->
its conditions — because it would be the cause of its existence, and when it gave it existence — and if the way of the existence of such things is in multiplication, then it must share in the face of it. And if necessary, then the next one must be a soul of things, and it is emanating from the First Intellect, the cause of its existence, so as to be an example of it in one face and in the opposite way. And if it should be an example of it in a different way like its face, it has been shown that it is not necessary, if the existence of what follows — named Soul — comes from the Intellect (1), that it be like the Intellect in all its states. Nor is it necessary to say of what follows the same as what is said of the Intellect in terms of being the cause of its existence. This refutes what the author of al-Nusra said: that if it were obligatory, it would be obligatory.

Chapter Twenty of the First Chapter

The author of al-Nusra said:
For this there are many analogues in the visible scenes, such as the idea and the partial soul. The partial soul that is in us is like the Intellect, and the idea is like the Soul. And the idea — even though it comes from the self — may not be said to be like the self, because the idea perceives what it perceives part by part, time after time, whereas the soul in sum (2) is aware of what it perceives without time. This is what he said.

We say:
The soul — I mean the soul of human beings — has actions, and each of them has a name specific to it. If it requires the realization of something, it is called thought. If it observes what has occurred in itself of the forms of information given to you, it is called memory. And if it engraves itself with the landmarks it captures from the side of inference or from the side of
3 — Dropped in copy (A).
4 — Dropped in copy (B).
- 74

<!-- page 77 -->
the senses, that pattern is called perception. And if it knows that there are things outside it which it wishes to conceive, it is called (1) the will inclined toward them. And if it has actions, the idea is an act of it that does not exceed (2) itself, so that it has an existence other than its own existence. How then is the analogy of thought correct (3) — so that the so-called soul has an existence other than the existence of the Intellect, and is said to perceive part by part, when perception is nothing other than the soul itself acting, which act is called the idea in itself? Or how is it correct that perception of the soul — I mean the soul of human beings — be in sum without time, when there is no way for it to hunt knowledge except from the side of the senses and inference, from the side of their parts to their totality in time? Then it is not the case that the idea, if it is not permissible to say it is like the soul, obliges that the Second Intellect — which is called the Universal Soul — not be like the First Intellect.

If the idea is not from the souls of human beings, the Second Intellect runs the same course as the First Intellect — the First Intellect being the Second Intellect, which is the second existence of origination, self-contained, even though it is in existence without the first rank. And the idea has no existence unique from the soul; rather it is the very identity of the soul, and has no existence of its own. For every act of the soul there is a cause for which it is needed: that it is in the world of nature; and if it is separated from this world, these actions are nullified by the nullification of the cause. What remains is only the perceived self as a pure Intellect. Then he made the Second Intellect — called the Soul — proceed from the First Intellect, and the stream of thought from the Soul. The existence of thought was no existence (5) other than the existence of the Soul which he likened to the Intellect, nor did it have a self perceiving the soul — as if he had denied that the Second Intellect, called the Soul, has any existence

other than the existence of the First Intellect, and no perception like the first perception — and that it is impossible for what follows to have an existence other than the existence of the First, or that it have a perception like the first one. Nor is it appropriate to say that it is creative, just as the First Being does not deserve to be called emanated. Both are pure intellects; each has encompassed itself and preserved itself in itself, and they are similar in one way and not similar in another. If this is so, then there is no point in likening the idea to what he likens it to. Likewise, it makes no sense to make the human soul the First Intellect, nor to make the idea hold the status of the Universal Soul. The honor of the human soul and its movement from potency to act is only in acquisition, and acquisition is only from the senses as work, and from the side of perception as knowledge. If the honor of souls is in the acquisition by which they conceive through thought and reasoning — and the idea belongs to the souls only insofar as it is honored by it — then it is as if he has made the cause of the honor of the First Intellect to be the Second Intellect, called the Soul. This is an inverted issue: the honor of what follows, and its rank, comes from what is ahead of its existence — that is, the previous — because the honor of the previous is arranged by what is behind it. And the ruling on what has another existence in perfection and completion — if it is contrary to what exists in the first place — then it does not run in the same course as this in that. This is not the way to proceed, even if they share on one side. As for the author of al-Nusra, he has judged what its existence was first because of the following: that it must have something whose existence is another existence — and that is impossible.

Chapter Twenty-One of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said:
One of the clearest signs of the deficiency of the Soul and the perfection of the First Intellect is that
- 76

<!-- page 79 -->
the First and the author of al-Islah all said that the act belongs to the Second, and they did not attribute to the First anything — because action is always a sign of deficiency and need, and the abandonment of action is evidence of perfection and richness. That is to say, action is only for the purpose of drawing a benefit or repelling a harm.

Abu Zakariyya said:
The Initiator is already the Soul by way of manipulation (1) and tampering — and the people of religion do not say so. So there remains nothing but the drawing of a benefit or the repelling of a harm; and when this is found, there is deficiency. The Soul, in doing this, seeks to draw the benefit, which is to reach its purpose destined for it; so it begins with action. The Intellect, when it has no rank above it (2), is not deficient and does not move to action in any way. The Soul, when it found a station above it, remained moving and remained in its quest. By this method we say: in the case of the deficiency of the Soul and the integrity of the Intellect, this statement is clear, praise be to Allah.

The result of his saying in this chapter is that the Second is the doer, and that action is only to draw a benefit or to repel a harm; and if the drawing (3) of benefit and the repelling of harm are only for imperfection, then the Intellect is not deficient in action. The reason for its stillness is that it has no rank above it for which to ask; and the Soul has no rank above it to which to move to attain. We say: the words of the author of al-Islah, if they had been about the Soul that is the soul of human beings, would have been a better expression of what the author of al-Nusra said. But when his words were about that which is called the Soul, the saying of the author of al-Nusra [does not hold]. His inference from the state of human souls — that they act only to draw a benefit, to repel a harm, or to tamper — for the invalidity of his statement, and to extend his ruling in the universe to the Following which is complete in its totality, is a clear prejudice. It may be said in sum that what is said (4)
1 — In copy (A) it came: "request."
2 — Dropped in copy (B).
3 — This sentence is completely omitted in copy (A).
4 — In copy (B) it reads: "what was."
- 77


<!-- page 80 -->
As for its existence being first in terms of completeness and perfection, it cannot be said that what is not in being is another existence, nor that its existence was an existence first. The statement of the author of al-Nusrah concerning the act and its divisions is incorrect, for not every act is undertaken to draw a benefit, or to repel a harm, or to engage in vain effort. An act may proceed from an agent in such a way that it is beneficial to the object of the act, not to the one who produced it. The evidence for this is that heat from fire is an act of the fire, yet this act does not return to the fire and is not a reward for it; there is neither benefit nor harm to the fire itself, but rather the benefit and the harm fall upon whatever the heat reaches. The same applies to the act of the sun in illuminating (1) the world. Humans were able to act by their own will, and through their elevation and the existence of the universe of plants, animals, and minerals, the benefits of these acts accrue to others and not to themselves; they do not benefit from this act, nor are they harmed by it. So too with the whales that are in the Sea of Oman, and others called "dolphins," which, if a person of old should drown in the sea, help him escape and push him toward safety — yet that act does not return to them with benefit nor with harm; rather the benefit reaches the one it affects. And if this is the case in the natural world, where it makes no sense to attribute such strange acts to it, even though it is deficient in its conditions and dependent on others in its actions, there is no denying that the pure abstract intellects, which have been granted their perfections in the first instance of their existence, can produce greater acts than those that emerge from the natural realm. Their perfections require this, according to the proximity of their ranks and the elevation of their grades — the noble acts, not for the sake of (2) drawing a benefit to themselves nor warding off harm, but for the sake of others. So these acts are acts of wisdom, not acts of drawing benefit or repelling harm, nor acts of futility. No — the perfect power from which the perfect and well-ordered act is produced exists only in the perfect being. Hence, what he has called "the soul" is not in motion as he claimed; rather, it possesses what is necessary for nature.

<!-- page 81 -->
This motion belongs only to bodies themselves, to their natural forms, and it is of a higher rank than nature, while being below what we have explained in the book concerning the rest of the intellect. From what we have mentioned, it becomes clear that his concession and his protest are not correct against the proponent, and he has surrendered the saying of the author of al-Islah that the next is more complete than the perfect, according to what we have explained. It is not permissible to say what he said concerning the realm of innovation derived from the first and second intellects. As for the upper letters regarding motion and rest, we have shown that they pertain to what is either a body or possesses a body, and that suffices here.

Chapter Twenty-Two of Part One

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The upshot of the statement of the author of al-Islah is that the act of the soul is something done, not the soul itself — even though the soul, with him, is completed; the act of the soul becomes (1) a soul. This is forbidden by manifest abstention, because acts have never been seen, in observable experience (2), to become subjects. For example: the writer and the writing, the builder and the building — writing does not become a writer, nor does building become a builder; rather it is the man, who is a writer in potency and a builder in potency, who becomes a writer and a builder in actuality. And likewise we say of the soul and the intellect: the intellect is already perfect, and the soul is already imperfect, ranked in potency; it becomes already existent if it gains perfection. But if its act were to become perfect, this would be impossible.

We say:

The aim of the author of al-Islah in saying that the act of the soul is what is done, and not the soul itself, is that the second perfection is attained through it — from its side flows that which overflows upon it. Thus it issues forth from potency to the measure of the act, and if the aim of the saying is the act of the soul,

2 - In Copy (A) it is mentioned to catch.
3 - In copy (b) the witness is mentioned.
- 79

<!-- page 82 -->
namely, what issues from its abundance (1) from potency into act, and the second perfection is obtained, and nothing crosses the limit from potency to act except by what already exists — then it is not to be denied. If what was in potency issues forth and attains the second perfection through acquisition, so as to be like that which exists in what was given of perfection in the first existence, then they share in the sense that they already exist, even if each of them has received something. They share in it, though not from the side from which the other received it, and it is not impossible for them to be two equivalents in terms of commonality. It is known that everything is gathered, and any other thing of meaning is, in that sense, like it. The proof is our saying that everything which goes out from the limit of potency to the limit of act is already existent, and everyone who is already existent has had the second perfection occur to him; and everyone who passes from the limit of potency to act has the second perfection occur to him. If the fulfillment of what is already existent is the second perfection, and the existence of what was established in potency has become already established by means of the second perfection, then they are common and similar in attaining the second perfection — though (2) on one side they differ from each other. What we have mentioned is neither impossible nor evil; rather it is necessary. So the statement of the author of al-Nusrah is nothing but prejudice (3).

Chapter Twenty-Three of Part One

Then (4) the author of al-Nusrah said:

This is a manifest abstention, because acts have never been seen, in observable experience, to become agents, given what he has mentioned (concerning the matter of the writer (5) and the builder), with evidence that testifies

1 - In copy (b) it came with its abundance.
2 – In Copy (A) it was stated:
3 - In Copy (B) there is a prejudice.
4 – In Copy (B) it says:
5 – This sentence was completely omitted in the year (b).
- 80

<!-- page 83 -->
to this by abstaining from what has been stated. We say: acts are the subjects of the truth. The writing that he mentioned, which is the act of the writer, is the very thing that addresses its reader and speaks to him; if the writing in its forms and arrangement were not effective upon the reader — even as if it were speaking about its subject and translating it — then its writer would not have engraved it with these forms so that the reader might know what is in the soul of its writer. And the image which the builder creates is what conveys to the viewer the image in his square structures and their disposition, so that he may conceive of it. If the image of the building did not already exist (1), it would not be acquired. The same applies to the observable scenes which carry the pictures of what they depict. So too the striker: the one who strikes is the one who inflicts pain, and the pain itself, as we have mentioned in our two well-known epistles, al-Rawdah and al-Inarah. And the saying of God Almighty — there is in it evidence that acts are also agents, as most of those who speak attest: "And there is nothing but praises Him" — this is the act of the Almighty, and though it is an act, it praises Him. And His saying (interpretation of the meaning): "And We sent the winds" — the wind is one thing, and the insolent thing performs an act through it. The explanation of this is that it is clear that the protest of the author of al-Nusrah against the author of al-Islah in this chapter is impossible. Then the aim of the author of al-Nusrah in saying that we have not seen acts in observable experience become agents at all is that the very acts have not become identical to the very subject in the observable scene — the writing has not become identical to its writer, nor has the building become identical to its builder. This is a correct statement as he said it, because an act does not become identical to the one from which the act issued; rather it is something done, similar to its doer in the existence of the act in it. Even though the ranks differ and the relations differ, the author of al-Nusrah wanted to defend those whom he represents by prejudice against the author of al-Islah — may God sanctify their souls — and that there are many mixtures (2) layered upon one another.

1 – In copy (b) it is as follows: "Indeed".
2 - In Copy (B) it was stated that he wanted to.
The Book of Riyadh [6]

<!-- page 84 -->
Chapter Twenty-Four of Part One

The author of al-Nusrah said:

He likened the intellect and the soul, in their perfection and imperfection, to a man and to sperm, because the sperm is the image of a man in potency in truth, and it becomes a man in actuality. Likewise, the soul is the image of the intellect in truth, and it becomes perfect like the intellect, if it benefits from the intellect — its benefits being measured out to it. This is what he said.

We say:

His statement that the sperm is the image of the man in potency (1) in truth, and likewise that the soul is the image of the intellect in truth, becoming perfect — is not correct. The sperm is not the image of the man, nor is the soul, which is in potency, the image of the intellect. The sperm is matter prepared for receiving form (2), free of forms, which then acquires them in stages. Likewise the soul is a natural preparation, vacant of forms, which would acquire the forms of knowledge so that the soul becomes self-fulfilled by them. Yet even if the soul were the image of the intellect in truth, then the intellect would have to have an image of what it is, so that it would be one thing, and if the image had an image, it would be something else — and how can this be true, when its existence is a creative existence, and otherness has no place in it except by addition, and the essence is one? No. This discourse and analogy applies only to what is natural — except for that which (3) was outside the separation of bodies.

Chapter Twenty-Five of Part One

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Likewise, the rank of the speaker (nāṭiq) in the physical world (4) is like the rank of the intellect in the world

of the spiritual, and the rank of the foundation (asās) is like the rank of the next. Thus, just as it is possible to say that the soul is perfect like the intellect, it is possible to say that the foundation is perfect like the speaker. But this is not correct, because the rank of the foundation is below the rank of the speaker, and this has been explained in the meaning of prayer. This is what he said.

We say:

The second is like the first, even if it is in a lower rank, and the foundation is like the speaker, even if he is in a lower rank. That second is — in its existence — that of which nothing remains in the form of a degree to be attained later by which he would gain perfection, just as the foundation is perfect in its rank, with no degree left for him to ascend to and acquire. Yet the second is not in himself complete. It suffices as witness to this the act of the Prophet and his indication concerning what we have said — namely, that it is obligatory to sacrifice camels, and not to perform the sacrifice on others (2), because slaughter consists in delivering the iron to the head of the fu'ād (3), through the wound, so that no blood remains corrupted — by the degree of the report, the contemplation of the secrets, and the dispelling of doubts and suspicions (4) in the ranks. The sacrifice is grounded in the taking of the covenant and the release in the call, and the slaughter is based upon it. It is as if the Prophet (peace and blessings of God be upon him) had displayed this deed and informed the Ummah who followed him on one side; and the proof of his role is that the basis of his standing in supplication is that he did not establish it in the inner call, which is the continuation of the worship of God leading to the unity of God and the knowledge of His limits, unless he teaches him what is in it of perfection, and informs him of what is in its perfection. So the highest degree is in his reporting, and his path is in attaining perfection and completion — not like any other path of his Ummah, by which he obtains (5) communication and completion
