Peace did not perform this fundamentally except to indicate that the second, which is the foundation in the world of intellect, has attained perfection first. There is no contradiction in taking the covenant first from the sons of the invitation (da'wah), then informing them of the degree of perfection; and the communication is only for the purpose of pointing out and indicating that the natural souls, which are below the second, are first in potency, then issue forth by acquisition into actuality at last — which is their perfection and completion — so that each of them may believe what is fitting to believe in it. And then it was not limited to indicating that he was indicating the completion of perfection on the other side, in the two rak'ahs of the dawn prayer, which are the first and the second. In the first rak'ah, which is the first rak'ah, it is obligatory to recite the praise (ḥamd); in the second rak'ah, it is obligatory to recite al-Qunūt in place of the praise that is in the first rak'ah, so that it is in the same state as the first from the standpoint of perfection. Their distinction is made only in terms of rank in existence: this is the beginning of existence, like the first rak'ah, and that is the second rak'ah, like the second rak'ah.

Chapter Twenty-Six of Part One

Then we say in response to what he said:

If (2) it is permissible to say that the soul is as perfect as the intellect, then it is permissible to say that the foundation is like the speaker — but this is not right. And how could it be right, when the foundation is the one who stands in the place of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his family, even if he falls short of standing in his place, and is not as complete as he is? When God enjoins obedience to him, how could God Almighty enforce obedience to one who is like others in deficiency? Both of these positions are untenable, and it is not permissible in wisdom that the Prophet be a messenger to all those who are in his time and those who are born after him,

1 - In Copy (B) (Follow)
2 - Fell in copy (A)".
- 84


<!-- page 87 -->
And it would be an excuse for His creation, since He sends the community to its appointed lots, and there is no grandfather (jadd) like Him. The proof is therefore established against them, for the Foundation (asās) stands as the one who speaks after him (1) on one side, and as the one spoken for on the other. It is sufficient proof that the disparity between them in rank does not lie in the self, since the rank of the Foundation is below the rank of the Speaker (nāṭiq). He could not invoke anything except by virtue of rank. It is clear from what we have set out that the position of the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* has been upheld against the objection of the author of *al-Nuṣra*, and his statement that the Second is deficient — even though it is the view of the author of *al-Nuṣra* — is correct in the sense that must be said of it.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven of Part One**

The author of *al-Nuṣra* said by way of argument:

That bowing is upon the Foundation, prostration is upon the Speaker, and the supplication in bowing — "Glory be to my Lord the Great, and praise be to Him, and He is the purification of the righteous" — is from the side of his Cultivator (murabbī), the Greatest (2). The implication is that the one being followed (which is the Prophet) and the Great One — while his Cultivator (3) is the Greatest — is the one being followed. And in prostration, "Glory be to my Lord the Most High and praise be to Him" — this is the analogue (taʿziya) of the Speaker, the Prophet, from the side of his Cultivator. He is the Second, and the Higher is upon the prior side, since his Cultivator is the Higher and the Prior. The Speaker (peace be upon him) has mentioned this in revelation.

The sense is that when he says it, he says it meaning that the Speaker here stands in the rank of the Intellect (ʿaql), so that the Foundation hangs from him and is at his corner — these being the two principles — or that the lower one means the Prior. But the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) and his family attained the highest degree of prophethood,

<!-- page 88 -->
to the point that no veil remained between him and the Prior, while the Prior himself had no separation between himself and the Word (kalima). The Foundation has not reached this level and rank. Likewise, the Second has not reached the rank of the Prior, but remains below his rank. This obtains in every pair, because the male is always more complete and more noble, and the female is more deficient. So too with the heavens and the earth: the heaven stands in the position of the male, and the earth in the position of the female; the heaven is more noble than the earth, and the earth more deficient than the heaven. This is what he said.

We say:

The statement of the author of *al-Nuṣra* that the rank of the Prior is higher than that of the Second is a correct statement, and it is what the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* said. The fact that the rank of the Second is below the rank of the Prior is not a deficiency in himself. As for his statement that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) and his family reached the rank of the Prior in perfection, while the Foundation did not reach it — this is hardly correct, because the Foundation, even if he did not reach that degree by himself, has reached it by means of the Speaker: through his teaching and his making him aware of the blessings sent down to him, the Foundation reached the degree of perfection. By his complete acceptance of it, he illumined himself and became as the moon at the full of the fourteenth night.

This is borne witness to by the conduct of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his family in delivering the sermon on Friday before the prayer, and in delaying it on the day of ʿĪd al-Fiṭr — because Allāh made Friday a face of interpretation (taʾwīl) upon Himself: it is one of the seven days. His ascending the pulpit is the first sermon; then he prays, so that he attains the degree of perfection and completion and rises to the highest ranks of honor from the side of the Origins. He was first, then he called for his sharīʿa, for accepting his revelation and obeying his Foundation. As for the Day of Fiṭr, it is upon his Foundation, for it is one of the days of Allāh the Almighty. Through obedience to Him, the religion of Allāh is fulfilled. He offers prayer first on the basis of his Foundation under obedience to Him, and the acceptance of His command and His law, and through that he attains perfection and rises in the ranks of honor. Through his intermediation, he is acquainted with the degree of the Successor (waṣī), who is perfect in himself, even if his attainment of the degree of perfection is from the side of the Speaker.

His rank is below the rank of the Speaker, yet the existence of the female is not a deficient existence, so it is possible

<!-- page 89 -->
to invoke in its support what supports it (1) — for its existence is a complete existence. Were its deficiency due to what it carries out that is exclusive to the male, then the existence of the male would also (2) be deficient in respect of what he carries out that is exclusive to the female. So it is ruled out that the male's existence is complete and the female's incomplete; therefore the male is complete in himself and the female is complete in herself. By the joining of one to the other, their rank is rendered unequal, so the female is ranked below the male. This is sufficient proof that the disparity is in ranks, not in selves. As for his statement, "the Second did not reach the rank of the Prior" —

He said:

The Foundation has not reached this level. Therefore the statement of the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* is correct that the Second is complete.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight of Part One**

The author of *al-Nuṣra* said:

It is a sign of the deficiency of the soul and the perfection of the intellect that many of the speaking souls are subject to nature, and are led by it and by what it commands them, and incline to its desires and pleasures, and forget their own knowledge, light, and splendor, and desire the world of nature — its forms and effects — and remember nothing of the splendor of their own world and its light, to seek it. This is because of their lack of desire for natural desires and pleasures, and their asceticism in their own world and its pleasures, goodness, and splendor. As for the intellect, on account of his agency (3), beauty, and perfection, he is not forgetful of the pleasures of his world and its splendor, and does not incline to nature, nor desire it for its own sake or its desires, and does not look at it; and if he does look at it, he looks at it from the side of

<!-- page 90 -->
pity, mercy, and contempt for what it has. He informed her of himself that she would be little if she were attributed to what is in his world. If the soul were like the intellect, it would be steadfast in what it has seen in its own world, and would not desire nature when affected by it. This is what he said. He said that many speaking souls are subject to nature, led to what it commands, and incline toward it on account of their deficiency.

We say:

The submissive one that is led, and the selflessness and pleasures and following of natural desires and the abandonment of worship — this is the sensual soul (al-nafs al-ḥissiyya), not the speaking soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) to which I have alluded (1). The sensual soul is only persuaded, and it is persuaded by the onset of ugliness and vices, if they (2) are affected by the intellect by way of the bonds of discipleship in education, so that it prevents her from committing what she would otherwise commit. These souls are not the Successor souls that emanate from the First Intellect, upon which the author of *al-Iṣlāḥ* spoke. We have already established his statement of what is implied in it (that the Successor is distinct from these souls and these (3) souls are others), and that the Successor is perfect and complete, in a state in which nothing remains in himself that he has not attained, so that he is deficient in it — as is the case with these souls, which exist in the first perfection and have a second perfection that they attain through time and acquisition.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine of Part One**

Secondly, we say that the Successor is perfect, and the one who confers his perfection

and his completion at the beginning of his existence all at once: he exists, and through him and through his overflow the imperfect attain the acceptance of the degree of perfection. The proof is that the existents in the natural world

<!-- page 91 -->
exist by potentiality first, then by actuality afterwards; and there is no passing of what exists in potentiality into actuality except by the presence of something already actualized. If there is no passing of what exists in potentiality into actuality, the existence of what is already actualized, and external to actuality, exists — for the existence of what is already actualized is a necessary existence. If the existence of what is already actualized is a necessary existence whose existence has not been disturbed, then it is either the First Intellect or another. It is invalid for the First Intellect to abstain from existence in act except by the presence of another, or to require in mind something existing alongside him outside himself, so that he acts upon it. The First Intellect is independent in his action, for there is nothing within him other than himself, since he is the perfect whose existence is from what comes to be (the Almighty being above him), and he grows up as one existence having no parallel in his existence, and nothing else is present with him for him to act through. For if it were, the Almighty would have become an idol worshipped. It has been said that this is sufficient, and we have explained more than this in our book known as *Rāḥat al-ʿAql* (*The Repose of the Intellect*). What is in this position alone — there is nothing alongside him, nor any need for him in his action, but rather he acts by himself. (And if he is acted upon (1) in himself, then he is the one who brings out the existent by potentiality in him, and by his overflow upon another it is ruled out that what is in him is graduated to bring out what exists in potentiality into actuality.) This is the First Intellect. If it is not the First Intellect, then it has been established that he is distinct from him and is the Second. This bears witness to the soundness of our statement that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his family did not invoke the greatness of his rank and integrity in regard to other human beings,

so that he might complete in his place his deed — which is the revelation and the law — and that he (peace be upon him) in that uniqueness of himself and from himself issued forth according to what he derived from what gave him a degree. He found what he had recited and prescribed it perfectly, so he conducted himself in a bodily mode in the descent (tanzīl) along the course of the First Intellect, in the same creative manner, so that his action in its rank led to contemplation of the truth of belief, in knowing what is hidden from the senses

<!-- page 92 -->
of the principles of origination (ibdāʿ) and renewal (inbiʿāth), so that the path of monotheism (tawḥīd) is unaltered.

As for the Foundation, even if he is at the highest degree of perfection, he has not had that degree by which he could be unique in his action in the matter of the call (daʿwa) and interpretation (taʾwīl), of teaching and guidance, by himself. So he did not dispense with what he found with him, which is the revelation and the sharīʿa. I do not mean that the Foundation dispenses with this — that is, with the revelation and the sharīʿa — nor that this is what the Speaker dispensed with something existing alongside him in the bodily world, which he uses to act through, as the Foundation does, and therefore his action follows the course of the Second.

That which obtains in him is what we have mentioned as proof of knowing the rank of the Second: that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) and his family follow the course of the First, who is the perfect one who dispenses with anything other than himself in his action and does not employ anything other than himself. The Foundation follows the course of the Second, who is the perfect one in whom there is no action of his without revelation and sharīʿa — without which he does not dispense in his action. There is no act, and the subordinate community follows a sub-orbital course in the natural world, in which its existents pass from potentiality to actuality through the overflow of the Second.

The souls that are in potentiality need that by which they attain the degree of connection and perfection. From the entity of the Foundation's call — just as the Second is the one who supports the one ready for acceptance from the world of nature, so too (1) is the Foundation, whose place in the call is to make the one willing to obey attain the degree of connection and perfection. That is the pronouncement of what they would have been from the world of nature, had they not been perfected. The high degree is from the side of the First Intellect, but from the side of the Second Intellect first; just as none of us reaches the degree of connection from the side of the Speaker, but only on the side of the Foundation's call first, so they run from those above them as we run from them ("the sole follows the sole"). So the perfection of the pronouncement and the officials are their place, and we have perfected them, having been crammed away from God by them. The result is that these souls are not the Successor.


<!-- page 93 -->
Thus, whatever exists with such conditions is evidence of its deficiency, and the objection of the owner of al-Nusra against the owner of al-Islah is therefore an impossible objection.

Chapter Thirty of Part One

He said: (1)

The latter is incomplete because it forgets its world and does not remember it. How can this be true, since forgetting does not occur except after knowledge? And if these souls existed beforehand while they were knowing, it would have been impossible for them to come into being by descending into the abode of nature after dwelling in majesty and attaining the perfection which is intellectual knowledge — just as the existence of what exists in the world of creativity and emanation from the intellects is an existence resembling that which is necessary in the world of the body, which is not subject to the impossibility of what it was. Such a thing must exist, and it is therefore eternal — and how can the eternal come to be? How can it have the conditions of possessing knowledge at one time and the removal of it at another? And the abode in which it resides is not the abode of the impossibility he has affirmed, by his saying "forget" (2). And the statement is that if these souls were granted to these people from this world, and came to learn (3) and to be instructed in knowledge, then it is not without their being ignorant at the very outset of their existence in it, either in themselves or for themselves (4). If they are ignorant in themselves, then they are barred from knowing anything, and ignorance is intrinsic to them — and that is impossible, since we find that the soul learns after not knowing. And even if it is for an accident, it is not free from that accident being either in that world or in this one. If it is the abode of eternity and perfection, not the abode of impossibility

This is invalid in the case of accepting it casually, whether it was ignorance or general (1) in this world, given that it exists without that. Chapter Thirty-One of Part One

Then if its arrival is from the world of creativity, then its existence is first of all on the side of creativity and emancipation from the highest rank. What already exists in this manner is barred from accepting any accident, since the acceptance of accidents follows from the way existing things are divided — hovering between potentiality and actuality — which belongs to the abode of the body. But that which is in the abode of creativity has had its perfection and completion granted from the very beginning of its existence, which is eternal, and which is in this state. It is therefore impossible for it to accept any accident, or for any accident to befall it impossible to its existence. If that is so, and it is on the view of the author of al-Nusra that

it forgot — that being for it an impossible accident — then cease saying that it forgot its world.

Chapter Thirty-Two of Part One

Then we say:

Forgetting befalls souls that are prepared from the world of nature to accept knowledge by their entering into the accidents of it according to the causes of their existence. There then occur to them states pertaining to its being the richest of souls, if it is contented with the legal rites, and if the things that precede it in existence have been rationally connected to the First Principle — which we call the Intellect, the Second Revival, and the Latter — being the First Resurrection according to what we have explained in our well-known book "With Peace of Mind." It is impossible for these souls to come from the world of intellect, for which there is no evidence at all; and it suffices, from what the author of al-Nusra mentioned in the forty-fourth proposition of Euclid in his well-known book "al-Maqalid," concerning the impossibility of these souls coming from the world of intellect, as an indication of the refutation of what he said in another place. I do not know how that escaped him. And on what I estimate, the book "al-Maqalid" was composed after the book of al-Nusra — may God seat its author with our pure loyalists in the Holy House, and seal for us with goodness in our religion and our world.

Chapter Thirty-Three of Part One

Then he said:

The intellect, in its entirety, does not forget its world, does not incline toward nature, and does not desire it — this is a correct statement. If it is said of souls which have acquired knowledge and works, their perfection, and have thereby become attached to the abode of nature, that they have come to resemble the intellects which have gone forth in perfection — like the souls of the prophets and the foundations who stand in their place, the prayers of God be upon them — and the outgoing intellects, which are the First, the Second, the Previous, the Latter, the Creative and the Emanated, then

- 93 0

<!-- page 96 -->
the First is higher and loftier than to have any share of the natural world, or any inclination toward it; rather, its overflow is connected to it in order to complete what is not yet perfect.

Then he said:

If the soul were as perfect as the intellect, it would have remained steadfast in what it witnessed in its world, and it would not have desired to bring about the effect that it was relieved of. Its condition, that the First does not pertain to it — and it has been said in this sense that it is unique.

And he said:

His statement that it did not desire that effect when it was effected is an affirmation that nature is its effect. But how is it true to say that nature is the effect of souls, and that their existence is in the presence of human beings who belong to the natural world? Or how does one imagine that the universe of the heavens, planets, natures, and births is confined to human beings who can comprehend the number? Or how is it true that the perfect should desire its own condition in what is beneath it? That is impossible — and if it is impossible, then it is necessary that the existence of the natural world together with the First Emanator, even though (1) the perfection of its assets is abundant. And to explain this is a contract that demonstrates the partisanship of the owner of al-Nusra against the owner of al-Islah, even if the words of the owner of al-Nusra are true in the manner we have explained, not in the way he himself described them in the rebuttal.

Chapter Thirty-Four of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said:

The Wise One mentioned in logic that all things exist in four senses: either in the objects of things, or in the idea, or in the words, or in writing. The things in themselves are those that the intellect has encompassed, and it has not failed to encompass them — for there is nothing that has no self, and if it were not the one that has a self, it would have eluded the sphere of the intellect. For the intellect, if it has encompassed it, is gathered

(1) Fell out in copy (B).

- 94

<!-- page 97 -->
in its self-possessed wealth; but the soul has encompassed only what the idea has presented to its encompassing. If it is one of the things that has its self, and the idea is not presented for it to take care of, then the soul has not gathered its own substance, and remains incomplete in degree compared with the intellect, because it has not stooped to the collection of that which has a self — whereas the intellect has encompassed all that has a self. And there is nothing that has no self — this being one of the clearest indications of the perfection of the intellect.

This is what he said.

We say:

The discourse on this matter is irregular. The Wise One meant to say — if he were to say it — that it is to show that the assets are divided into four parts in the nature of things. What he means is: of the things that exist outside the soul and which have a meaning in themselves in it, there is what is like the body, which itself exists outside the soul, and the meaning that it has is what it is in itself — namely length, width, and depth.

He said:

But in the idea — what he means is: of things that have no self outside the soul, but which are present in the mind, like genus, place, and the circles that are drawn in the soul concerning the motion of the imagined creature, and so on.

He said:

As for "saying" — what he means is: of the things that exist in speech, like propositions in the affirmative and the negative concerning subjects in which the subjects do not change.

He said:

But in writing — what he means is: of the things that exist in writing. And the ruling on its existence in writing is like the superabundant divine matters from the world of the intellect bestowed upon the prophets and the supporters — the prayers of God be upon them — which (1), if they are not controlled

(1) It fell out in copy (B).

- 95

<!-- page 98 -->
by the written letters, and if the forms are not deposited in the whole, the soul has no perception of them, and the futility of its existence and its use follow. The Wise One had restricted the assets to these four divisions, although upon investigation they reduce and dwindle beneath them. It is therefore permissible that the Wise One should wish to say what indicates that writing is a deputy for speech (1), and that speech is a deputy for the soul in what it wishes to display, and that the soul likewise represents those things that are outside it by imagining them, and in its own place attaches itself to them in the sense of being the same — through the acquisition of meanings by which they are. And not in the words of the Wise One is there a proof of what he wished to support — that the speaker lacks that great extent. If the words of the owner of al-Nusra are carried upon what is in the natural world of souls that have risen in rank and become already existing intellects called the Second Emanation — which is still a list of forces beneath the First Emanator in the world of Holiness — then he is right.

Chapter Thirty-Five of Part One

The author of al-Nusra has made it obligatory in his statement to explain the words of the Wise One that this is not true — if his words are based on what the owner of al-Islah has to say. The owner of al-Islah speaks: because thought, for the souls that exist in the natural world, is a power belonging to it to acquire what it does not yet possess the image of! And the end of this power, namely its becoming dispensable, is proof that the soul does not need what it has already encompassed, since it has its image with it, and has already risen in it, to think — but it needs thought in the things it has not descended to, and the image of which it has not yet acquired. Through these it attains the degree of perfection and dispensation. And if the soul dispenses with the thought of another —

(1) In the year 2 it was mentioned (about).

- 96

<!-- page 99 -->
We say:

The next thing meant is the flood of blessings upon the world's existents — and I mean the newborns. He has given his power first and enriched it by what we have given of the words we have given for His perfection which He had attained at the beginning of His existence; and it became richer. If it is so, it has become clear that the partisanship of the owner of al-Nusra against the owner of al-Islah, when he discussed him, appears — and the truth of what he said is that it pertains to the natural, not to what is contained in the Holy House.

Chapter Thirty-Six of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said:

As for the one who said that the deficiency is in the action of the soul and not in itself — because the action of the soul can only be done in time — this is mistaken and wrong, as it is not necessary that what he did in the past is incomplete. If the problem lay in the diminution of things only in time, then it would be supposed that what is done not in a long time is deficient, and that the shorter the time, the more complete it would be. But we see otherwise in observation (1) — for things that may be more complete are accomplished in a shorter time, and would be less so if the matter were as the owner of al-Islah described. Then it would have to be against him: time is not the cause of the deficiency of things or of their perfection, but rather the deficiency of a thing comes from the deficiency of the doer, or from the deficiency of the substances, or from the deficiency of all of them.

We say:

The owner of al-Islah did not claim, nor say, that the action is in time. He said that what is implicated in the meaning is attained through time, and the duration is the deficiency. If it is obtained, then it is complete; and everything in observation has its perfection — if it receives it, it is perfect. He has already advanced the saying that the meaning of

(1) In copy (B) "the viewer" came.

The Book of Riyad [7]

- 97

<!-- page 100 -->
his saying that the deficiency is in the action of the soul and not in itself — which is to want the soul to do what attains for it the degree of perfection from its side and to acquire the image (1) so that it becomes complete through its abundance — is nothing other than that the saying of the author of al-Islah that the deficiency is in the action of the soul, not in itself, is only an indication that the deficiency in the capable self comes from the soul that follows the flood. It cannot accept any payment except in time — days, months, and years. And if it is so, then the saying of the owner of al-Islah in the first place, that of which he is speaking, is true; and the saying of the owner of al-Nusra, if it is carried upon the natural, is true. But he wished to be an advocate for his teacher — may God bless them and bring us with the loyal — the prayers of God be upon them.

Chapter Thirty-Seven of Part One

The author of al-Islah said:

The Most High Creator (al-Bari) created the intellect and time together in one fell swoop. The author of al-Nusra said: It is known that time is the measure of motion, and motion belongs only to the heavens; so it must be said that the heavens, and the time generated from their motions, were with the intellect at once. And it is permissible to say that the powerful and the corrupt beings were with the intellect at once, even though it is permissible to say (2) that the intellect and time were together at once. Then this analogy entails that time is deprived of the virtues and lights given to the intellect — as the intellect receives them and is thereby perfected — and that is impossible. Although it may be said that the intellect and time were together at once, they are not without being either two things or one thing. If they are one thing, then the plural

(1) Fell out in copy (A).
(2) See the introduction.

- 98


<!-- page 101 -->
This does not apply to the same thing by name alone, for it is impossible to say that the stone was with the rock, or the tide was with the knife, while they yet remained two distinct things. The intellect did not produce its own mind either without time or with time; the intellect itself is without time. For if it were together with time, then time would precede it — yet what the intellect precedes is not time, but eternity and pre-eternity.

We say:

The substance of his statement, though incomplete in proof and demonstration, is that the existence of time together with the intellect is impossible. This is correct in our doctrine, and it has already been established that it is impossible for anything to be co-present with the first intellect, which is sufficient in significance. However, the matter differs from what the author of al-Islah mentioned in this chapter. The author of al-Nusra said that what precedes the intellect is not time, but the eternal and pre-eternal — and he erred in this, for there is nothing ahead of the first intellect, as we have explained in the foregoing statement of this book and in the book Rahat al-Aql. What is correct is that the law of belief and doctrine concerning the eternal and pre-eternal in the world of origination requires what we have explained in the epistle known as al-Riyad, and in what we mentioned therein, which need not be elaborated at length.

Chapter Thirty-Eight of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said:

(1) Among the proofs that the intellect became perfect is that it was not subject to anything except the Almighty, while the soul became imperfect because it was subject to nature:

(1) See the introduction.

— 99

<!-- page 102 -->
We say:

The philosophers and the most advanced among them, though virtuous in their time, continued to err in many of the matters of intellect they discussed. Had they lived in our time, they would have seen the springs of grace and how they overflow from the House of Revelation. Indeed, were things as they are in the Alawite call, with their diligence they would have done themselves justice and would have corrected much of their belief in what the senses fall short of grasping. With the traces of the One for whom God has made a light in the mysteries of things, they would have been convinced. For in their own times they followed their own opinions and intellects in what they pursued, until each of them in his belief reached only what his works indicate — like the people of the exoteric in the Hanafi school, when they relied on their intellects to follow the paths of religion and their opinions diverged, distinctions arising among them: some more correct, others more in error. He who relies on what the balance of religion testifies — namely the horizons and the souls — finds in the foregoing discourse the explanation that the existence of such a belief is false, and it need not be expounded at length. As for what the author of al-Nusra mentioned in his contention — we say:

We ask God to enlighten our intellects, to seal our affairs with happiness, and to grant us success in good words and good deeds, by virtue of His being, His kindness, and His generosity.

(3) Fell in copy (B).
— 100

<!-- page 103 -->
The Second Chapter of Kitab al-Riyad

On the adjudication between (the disputants) in the chapter concerning what was said about the intellect that is the first creation.

Chapter One of Part Two

The author of al-Islah said:

We say:

Motion and stillness are two effects united in the first and the second. For the first, when it became creative, united with creativity, and that union had two effects upon it in potentiality. We said two effects because creativity and the creative are two, one being creativity itself and the other being the creative itself. Creativity and the creative, though two, are understood as one essence, which is the whole. These two effects exist in the first in potentiality; then the second is emitted from the first, so the emission has two effects upon the intellect — one being the emission itself, the other being the union of the two origins in the matter. One of the two effects is motion, which is higher than the other, which is stillness; and one of them belongs to the first, which is higher than the other, which is the second. The motion of the first is the conveyance of the second of the essences in all, and stillness is the second's acceptance of the benefit of the first of all the essences and its inclusion within it,

— 101

<!-- page 104 -->
upon it. I do not say that motion and stillness are born of the soul by force, learning them from the intellect; rather, I say that they are two effects of the soul by the power learned from the intellect in the image and the form, so that the image and the form became the foundation of this world.

The product of his statement in this chapter is that motion and stillness are two effects united in the first and the second; that they exist in the first in potentiality; that one of them is higher than the other, namely motion; that motion is the benefit of the first, and stillness the acceptance by the second of the benefit; that the soul received them by the power learned from the intellect in the image; and that the first, when it became creative, united with creativity, and that creativity and the creator are two, even if they are one thing.

The author of al-Nusra said:

This statement contains some confusion, for motion is only for the sake of seeking. As for the motion of bodies, it is like the motion of fire upward to seek its place, or the motion of earth downward to seek its place; the motion of souls is to seek benefit. When (1) seeking is established (2), there is need, for seeking arises only from need. It is impossible to say that the intellect needs something for which it moves. Even if it were possible to ascribe motion to the two origins, ascribing it to the soul is more fitting than to the intellect, because the soul needs to benefit from the intellect — moving toward it with the motion of longing, the motion of the effect toward the cause. If it is established that motion belongs to the soul, then stillness belongs to the intellect — stillness in what it receives from the virtues and lights bestowed upon it by its principle, not seeking increase, because its principle has overflowed upon it from the lights and the light whose limit the intellect does not know. Moreover, the intellect would otherwise move in seeking it; yet the intellect has not poured forth upon the soul all that overflowed upon it, but only some of it, so that the soul might dwell upon it and grasp it when it was emitted —

(1) In Nisan (B): 'is established.'
(2) Fell in copy (A).
— 102

<!-- page 105 -->
so that some of them desired to move in their seeking. The substance of the statement is that motion is only from need; that the intellect is not in need of motion; that ascribing motion to the soul is more fitting than to the intellect; that motion belongs to the soul and stillness to the intellect; and that the intellect has not poured forth upon the soul all that it has, but only some — so as to be the cause of the soul's motion in seeking the rest. This is the sum of what they all say.

We say:

As for the saying of the author of al-Islah that motion and stillness are effects united in the first and the second, and that in the first they exist in potentiality and in the second in actuality — this affirms that the first is at rest, moving in potentiality, because motion and stillness exist in it potentially; and that the second is already at rest by virtue of motion and stillness being in it in potentiality, and that the second is moving — and this is impossible in several ways.

The saying of the author of al-Nusra — that the intellect is not in need, and therefore is not in motion — is correct, because motion in all its kinds is found only in natural bodies. The first and second intellects, being beings of the first existence, are not embodied, so it is not permissible that they be in motion or at rest. Proof: the body requires (1) being a substance, and the dependence of one on another in existence such that its existence would not be from God Almighty as the first existence — because what exists from God is the first existence. Its very self is none other than the act of which it is the first existence, by God the Almighty and Great; and the same act has one subject and not two. If its existence were not the first existence, it would be preceded by another in existence, and so it would be something else. If the intellect were a body, it would be preceded in existence by others, and it would not be the first creation. Nothing precedes the first intellect in its existence, since it is itself the act of creation, behind which is only God the Creator Almighty, whose existence is indicated. If it is impossible that anything precede it in existence, then it is invalid that it be a body. And if

(1) Fell in copy (B).
— 103

<!-- page 106 -->
it is impossible to be a body, and there is no motion except when one is a body, then the intellect has no motion; and if this is invalid, then the statement of the author of al-Islah — that the intellect has motion in potentiality — is nullified.

Chapter Two of Part Two

The author of al-Islah said:

We said two effects because creativity and the creative are two — one being creativity itself, the other being the creative itself — and creativity and the creative, even if two, are nothing but a whole, in an image not to be believed.

So creativity and the creator are matters contrary to what he mentioned — that they are two, that creativity is the creator and the creator is creativity, and there is only one. If added to what its existence began with, it is an act of his, and the same as the act; and if added to itself, it is the principle that it is the object of another, whose existence comes from him.

Chapter Three of Part Two

On the statement of the author of al-Islah that motion and stillness in the first are in potentiality, and in the second in actuality:

We say:

What pertains to the first existence is only the mere act and the right of action. Even if it were permissible for motion and stillness to exist in the first in potentiality, the ruling of the second would then be more honorable than that of the first, because the ruling of the one in actuality is more honorable than the ruling of the one in potentiality. It is impossible for the second to precede the first in honor, and what he mentioned is among the attributes of what comes later —

— 104

<!-- page 107 -->
of the existence that needs action in its outward manifestation, and pertains to what already exists. The first intellect is indeed intellectual, and so too is the second; what follows is what is paradoxical for the substances of the intellects. It is not permissible to believe that what necessarily exists from the House of Origination is specialized in matters that fall outside the highest limits.

Chapter Four of Part Two

The author of al-Islah said:

Motion and stillness are effects of the soul by the power learned from the intellect, in the case of the phylis and the image.

What is meant is that the bodies, which are the Aeoli together with the image, are moved — that which is moved among them — by the natural force whose presence within them is from what is outside the first and second intellects; this is a motion of longing (1). So he placed the two effects in the hallways. The image is its mover, and its being moved is impossible. This is learned from the intellect in the sense that its existence is from it, and this is correct in this way, and it is the best interpretation to which what he said may be attributed.

Chapter Five of Part Two

The author of al-Nusra said:

Motion is for the soul and stillness for the intellect, so it is not permissible to believe such things of those transcendent boundaries.

We say in summary:

The world of origination is the collective of before and after, and the pure intellects which are the paradoxical higher beings of the objects; the aim of what is fanciful is motion or stillness. For motion, in that which is moving, is that which is moving in it —

(1) In Version (B) it is mentioned as 'in the Oriental.'
— 105

<!-- page 108 -->
is something else, and they have the existence of the self. If motion is in what is moving, it is the one in which the other resides, having the existence of the self and what it is. As for the existence of the self in the presence of different things, the self becomes lacking and needy. And the next, which he called the soul, being from the world of origination, is specialized in completion and perfection, and the same does not become imperfect and needy. So it is invalid that it differ in itself; and if (1) it were impossible for there to be in it something other than itself, and if it were impossible for it to be what is not itself, then it could not be in motion, and it would be impossible for motion to be in it. For motion is that in which the thing wherein it resides moves away from its first state to the next, either in the self or in its states. The next, which he called the soul, has an eternal existence by virtue of its arising from the side of origination in the second sense — namely, that it is intellectual. If it is eternal, then it is impossible for it to be transferred from what it was upon at first, and it is invalid that there be motion in it; and the saying concerning stillness is like the saying concerning motion. Neither does the first intellect have any stillness, nor does the next, which he called the soul, have any motion.

Rather, the author of al-Nusra wished to say what he said about those souls and intellects that are in the House of Nature, where a second emanation is emitted; the being is moved according to what we have explained in our book known as Rahat al-Aql. Motion and stillness are specialized in the world of the body and what it contains, because they are among the causes of its perfection, not what is specific to the House of Origination. But he conveyed the meaning to assimilation in what he said concerning the deficiency in support of his sheikh, Awham — God make his dignity, and gather them and us with our pure followers (God's prayers be upon all of them).' (2)

Chapter Six of Part Two

The author of al-Nusra said:

Motion is from need, and the soul is more in need than the intellect, as has been shown from the above —

(2) Fell in copy (A).
(1) The sentence is completely omitted in Version (A).
— 106


<!-- page 109 -->

It is said that movement and stillness are appropriate only for what is a body, while the First and the Second, of whom we speak, are not embodied — so attributing movement and stillness to them is excluded, in the manner we have shown in our earlier words and in our well-known book *Peace of Mind*. The statement holds true in what comes about by way of generation: the Second is not a creative agent, and its existence is in the First Emission, not by movement.

As for what he mentioned about everything's need: movement in a natural thing may be among the causes of its perfection — such as the motion of the heavens, which moves not out of need in itself to attain what was not granted at the first instant of its creation, but rather toward perfection. Likewise the movement of the *natiq* (speaker) and his diligence in gathering the word upon worship, and his engagement with the affairs of governance — this belongs to the completeness that is required, because something is required of him after he has attained perfection and risen to the rank of *natiq* while he yet exists.

This shows that the statement of the author of al-Nusrah — that movement for need is not realized at all — is not correct. Rather, his ruling is sound only for some natural things, on the basis that movement is inseparable from them, as we have already mentioned.

**Chapter Seven of Part Two**

The author of al-Nusrah said:

What does he have over the soul, except in some respect? What he meant by saying that the First is distinguished by the rank of priority and the highest standing in what is not specific without it — which he called a soul — is the best thing attributed to him by way of interpretation.

Otherwise, if the matter were as he described, and each of the *hudud* received from the rank above it overflowed onto the rank below, then the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him and his family) would have been deluded, and the blessings that reached him from the world of *al-Quds* (holiness) would have flowed onward to the *wali* and his followers without leaving any trace, and he would not have informed anyone of it — which is invalidated by the invalidity of its use, so the *wali* (peace be upon him)

<!-- page 110 -->

becomes doubtful in his perfection and merit, and his standing in the place of the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him and his family) and obedience to him fall under suspicion. On that view it would not be permissible to believe in the *natiq*, nor in the *wali*, nor in the *imam*, nor in the *hudud*.

Rather, we say:

The blessings overflow from the side of all the *hudud*; the reason being that there is no shortage in those blessings by which perfection is attained in all beings, nor any withholding on the part of the *hudud*. The limitation lies rather in the incapacity of the receivers to accept them — each according to the capacity of the one able to receive. Some receivers accept the blessings fully, others accept only what they are able to, and there are natural learners, and those who require compassionate teachers, and willing learners. It is not permissible for the *hudud* to withhold benefit from one who would benefit from it, so long as the receiver is in his right when willed — lest he fail to ascend to a rank that was his before the spiritual birth.

The Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) said: "Do not kill your children for fear of poverty"; and whoever does that has sinned, exceeded his proper stage, and fallen (1) from the rank of the virtuous. How then could it be permissible to lock up the spiritual wealth — namely the sciences and the blessings of light — from their rightful owners, given the proof that the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) commanded that it be paid to them, as he said: "If you find them of sound mind, then deliver to them their wealth, and do not consume it wastefully and in haste"? No — the blessings overflow only that they may be conveyed to those who deserve them. The record of this stands in its proper place, as we have explained in our hermeneutic letters and in our Basran and Baghdadi assemblies — may Allah and a company of our brethren make us perform the trust to its rightful possessors and to those faithful to its terms. Perhaps the author of al-Nusrah, by way of analogy, has narrated what he said.

(1) Dropped in copy (A).

<!-- page 111 -->

**Chapter Eight of Part Two**

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Likewise, action is the essence of encompassing, and it encompasses things, and this is not the state of that which is movable; for the movable does not encompass things but rather needs something (1) to dwell in, in complete stillness, so that it may encompass the still — though the intellect moves when it encompasses something, and the soul does not encompass itself by benefiting from itself. When we find it an ocean gathering things, with nothing descending and encompassing it, then whoever encompasses what he attains and gains from his light and his virtues, we know that the intellect is utterly at rest, and there is no illusion of movement in it; while the soul moves only to reach the door of the intellect, and then to dwell there. It does not move in order to encompass the intellect by benefiting from it as it would encompass something else. So long as the soul is in motion, it does not encompass; nor does it attain virtues and lights until it dwells by benefiting from the intellect; only then does it encompass things. The soul thus moves a movement of subsistence, which comes to dwell by benefiting from the One who is settled.

This is what he said.

We say:

It might be said that the intellect is not in motion regarding what it does not need to be extended toward, and not — if the intellect is not moving — that it must be at rest in the manner of bodies (which are not free (2) of being either at rest or in motion). Its existence is not like the existence of objects, which are not free of the values already given; this is a sign that movement and stillness do not apply to it. The same principle is required by the saying that, so long as the soul is in motion, it

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) Dropped in copy (B).

<!-- page 112 -->

does not encompass anything and does not attain virtues — for that meaning is not correct, whatever the case may be: neither in what is differentiated from the Second, as we mentioned, nor in what is not so differentiated. For the souls that are in the house of nature are not acquired except by being movable and embodied, using the senses, deductions, and instruments — and the existence of the command, in establishing evidence for it, suffices to show that the error here persists.

**Chapter Nine of Part Two**

Then he said:

The soul moves a movement of subsistence which comes to dwell by benefiting from the One who is settled. He is truthful in this when he has assigned a meaning to it, and he is in error if he carries it on the surface of what he says. As for his being truthful: it is to assign the soul to the class of natural souls — for one says that the soul moves because the natural souls, which exist in potentiality, are moving toward acquisition; and as he says, it is a movement of subsistence, because its acquisition leads it to subsist; and he said it dwells by benefiting, because it passes from potency to act by acquiring and benefiting, by gathering knowledge — virtues in knowledge and in action. As for his saying that it is settled by him from whom already exists [the perfection], and that by which he becomes mistaken: this is that he carries his statement on its surface meaning — for the Second is not in motion, nor is the intellect at rest in the sense in which bodies are at rest. They have no need of movement, stillness, or attentive care. One must take care to know the ranks of the *hudud*, so that there be no confusion about the matter of perceiving things in their realities — for such confusion would lead to the corruption of the essence of souls. May Allah make us light by their means, and grant us success in following the pure stages of the divine sciences and the treasuries of the lights of sanctity; may Allah's prayers be upon them.

<!-- page 113 -->

**The Third Chapter of the Book of Riyadh**

On the judgment among the *hudud* concerning what was discussed about the soul and the lower soul: are they similar to the First or not?

**Chapter One**

The author of al-Islah said:

Regarding what the author of al-Nusrah has proved: we do not say that nature and darkness are born from the Second, just as the Second is born from the First — because that world is the world of honor and virtue, while this world is the world of darkness (1) and ignorance. The Second, being born from the First, resembles him, whereas this is not the case with *al-Hayuli*.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

This statement is wrong, because if that world were light with no darkness in it, and this world were dark with no light in it, then there would be no connection between this world and that world. Rather we say that the soul has two extremes: one of them toward the intellect, and on its horizon is the luminous, noble side; and another part of it toward nature; and nature is on

(1) Mentioned in copy (Nissan), (3): the shade.

<!-- page 114 -->

its horizon — in which there is some darkness, as it were a circle, because nature is on its horizon. We likewise say that nature has two ends: one toward the partial soul, in which there is some light because it is on the horizon of the soul; and the other side facing the elements, which are the darkness in which there is no light. So in these divisions we speak of the soul and of nature, of its darkness and its light, and we do not say that this whole world is the world of light with no darkness in it, nor that this whole world is a world of darkness with no light in it — because if it were as he described, there would be no contact between them, nor any combining.

He said:

It is true that the Talmanic spheres are born of the soul, although they do not resemble it, just as the soul is born of the First, though it does not resemble it — but it has fallen into a state of frailty and darkness because of its distance from the First Cause, in order to be born from the soul; likewise the diminution that fell upon the soul, because it is beyond the First Cause — I mean the One who came before.

This is what he said.

We say:

The wording of what the author of al-Islah said in his book is different from what the author of al-Nusrah said. The gist of his saying, which is an answer to the statement of the author of al-Islah, is that the Second is born from the First and is not like him. He said: I do not say so. Rather, we say that the Second is born from the First, and is not like him, yet resembles him — though the former is of the higher rank and the more honorable station, like the male and the female in every race and species. The male, even though he is higher and more honorable than the female, is nonetheless her counterpart and her likeness.

Then we say:

The Second is more honorable and more elevated, having a relation to itself like its relation to the First — because the First and the Second are two counterparts, which are the mineral of honor, perfection (1), and elevation. There is no honor above them, and Allah has made them the mineral of perfect honor and perfect light,

<!-- page 115 -->

and complete elevation, complete knowledge — with no ignorance there, no cycle, no darkness whatsoever. They founded the higher world, which is the mineral of purity, *al-Quds* (sanctity), and life. *al-Hayuli* is a trace of the Second, which is the essence of this world; it is the essence of darkness, which cannot be described by mercy or power — yet the Second has manifested it: indeed, by means of the power learned from the First, he then brought forth the image from it, which is nature.

Then the author of al-Islah said:

It is not permissible to say that *al-Hayuli* was born from it; rather, we say that it is a trace of it, and therefore does not resemble it — while the Second is born from the First and does resemble him, even though the First is of higher rank. This is what he said. The gist of his saying is that the Second emanates from the First and resembles him, although the First is higher in rank; while *al-Hayuli* is a trace of the Second, and its essence is a dark essence rendered gloomy, because the path of the Divine with the Second is not like the path of the Second with the First. The First and the Second are counterparts, while *al-Hayuli* does not resemble the Second, because it is a trace of it. And if this is the upshot of saying that, then what the author of al-Nusrah answered is no answer, and the author of al-Islah was not obliged to say that this whole world is light and luminous. He said, describing the condition of the First and the Second, that Allah Almighty has made them the mineral of honor and exaltation — not by proof that there is no existence of what is unlike them; for the presence of the indication that *al-Hayuli* is not born from the Second suffices, with no need to belabor the discussion and prolong it with matters we might consider, which are not our concern in this paragraph (1).

We say:

*al-Hayuli* is not like the Second, nor like the First, because their existence is by their emission from

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) In copy (B), the sentence ...

<!-- page 116 -->

the First — at the lower proportion that we have shown in our earlier words and in our well-known book *Peace of Mind* — that is enough.

**Chapter Two of Chapter Three**

The author of al-Nusrah said:

This statement is wrong, because if that world were light with no darkness in it, and this world were dark with no light in it, then there would be no connection between this world and that world.

We say:

This statement is established from the author of al-Islah, unless he is shown to be in error; and in terms of what he said, it is correct. The soul is from the world of creativity and renewal: what is common to it suits what is above it by being emitted, and what is below it suits being a body. It has two aspects, neither of them like the First from which it exists. The Second resembles it on one side and does not resemble it on the other: the side from which it resembles it is that it is emitted; the side from which it resembles [the body] is that it is a natural body — that nature, which was added to the world of creativity and renewal, became a life that is sound from that world without its [direct presence], and gives him what it has to accept from him of backwardness and perception. And if it is added to this world, it would be the principle of movement, and would be in the thing that is in it itself, not by way of presentation, and it would become nature — even if it is emitted in its existence, or a medium between the two emissions, the First and the Second.

**Chapter Three of Chapter Three**

The author of al-Nusrah said:

By the soul we mean the Second, which has two sides: one side toward the intellect, on its horizon,


<!-- page 117 -->
It is the noble luminous limit, and one of its parts is turned toward nature, and lies upon its horizon — the side in which there is some darkness.

We say:

This is not correct, because the proof is that the one possessing parts is the one possessing diameters estimated by area; and the second one called it a soul on the basis of what we have already explained. If it is not an embodiment, then it is a false body, and one is therefore obliged to say that it has a luminous limb that lies upon the horizon of the intellect, and a limb in which there is some darkness, lying within the interpretation of nature that faces the intellect. The first is what was mentioned in the discussion of nature, and it is impossible for the second to be other than what the first agrees upon. For its existence is, at its origin, derived from the noble proportion in the first — namely, that it is a cause. And I have already said that the first is that which exists and is not in itself a cause. The ma'lul, by being a cause in addition to what is below it (which is the lower proportion in the first), and the ma'lul, by being added to itself (which is the lower proportion in the first), is from its own intellect. The same is that it is a cause from which something like itself emanates, for the cause has its effect upon it. And from its own intellect that it is a malīsa from which emanates that which is not like it, by being an emanation from the lower proportion in the first — namely, that it is ma'lul, because the honor of the effect's proportion is greater than that of the cause's proportion. The cause receives what is higher than what comes about from the cause, except for that which is below the effect — what exists about the cause testifies to what we have mentioned, namely that it is upon it as the foundation, which is the likeness of the second in knowledge of the body. Being invisible to the speaker, all that was originally there is present in the speaker in whom the existence of the foundation lay; and likewise, not all that the speaker has is present in the foundation, because the speaker has what the foundation does not share — that is, the visual in which the foundation does not participate. It has been lost, then, that the foundation in which the one within it is a participant of the speaker, and that there is nothing in the speaker that he does not indicate. If he does not indicate it, then it is
- 115

<!-- page 118 -->
the likeness by which the existence of the second is inferred according to its rank — and its rank was the second. It is not made manifest to the first, and if it is not indicated, then its existence is what was mentioned, namely that it lies upon the horizon of nature, which is impossible.

Chapter Four of Chapter Three

The author of al-Nusrah said:

But the hayuli fell into darkness because of its distance from the first cause, in order that the soul might be born from it.

We say:

The fact that the hayuli is not like the first, and the second is not, is not because it is far from the first by the proportion that is the case of the first one — namely, its being ma'lul, since it is a combination of two ratios relative to being a cause. The proportion of being ma'lul, and the existence of something that exists from the proportion that is ma'lul, is not like the existence of something that exists from the proportion that is a cause; for the cause (1) is more honorable than the effect in every respect, and precedes it in rank and nature. From the beginning, what is said to be a cause and what is said to be a ma'lul is but a single self that acquires this proportion in additions.

Chapter Five of Chapter Three

The author of al-Nusrah said:

But the diminution occurred in the soul because it is beyond the first cause, I mean the previous one.

We say:

There is no deficiency in the second, which he called a deficiency, as we have mentioned, nor is it

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
- 116

<!-- page 119 -->
far from the first. Rather, it is like it; and the diminution is necessary, not from its very nature, but it became incomplete — not from the side of its own selves, which is its perfection, but it became incomplete from a side whose second perfection it acquires by way of benefit.

Chapter Six of Title Three

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The proportion of the birth of nature from the soul is no greater than the generation of the soul from the intellect. The soul is less than nature, so that the soul, although it is permissible for it to be generated from the intellect with its perfection and honor and its closeness to the first cause of the soul, it is permissible for nature to be born from the soul, for nature is more like the soul in all of its states — in its actions and powers — than the soul is like the intellect in its actions and powers. For nature, among the early ones, is labeled as the principle of motion. Some of them said that it is the active force; and if you look at nature within these two limits, it is the best fortune and the best portion, because the soul, among the first, is the enduring moving essence, and is nevertheless labeled with the intellect — though not in the way that the intellect is one of the determinations of the soul. The soul also seriously challenges the intellect, since the definition of the intellect is that it is the first of things, that it is the creator, that it is the first effect, and that it is the first middle. It is not permissible to say that the soul is the first thing, nor that it is creative, nor that it is the first effect. So it is permissible for it to be born from the intellect with its perfection and honor and its closeness to the word — like the soul that does not bear the limitation of the intellect — and it is not denied that it is permissible to be born from the soul like nature, which resembles in its limits the limits of the soul. And this is not denied, even if one looks at the matter from the perspective of consideration. This amount of knowledge would not be hidden, even if the author of the Reform stood upon

1 — It fell in copy (A).
- 117

<!-- page 120 -->
the wonders and wisdom of nature, and the splendor and honor in its actions, and the beauty — the beauty from which the partial soul desires to incline toward here and to follow it. For you know and are certain that it is born of the soul, even though it does not resemble it, because effects are never desirable in order to regulate their causes. From this, the partial soul desires to follow nature, and that all goodness, splendor, honor, and adornment is from the higher noble world. If nature were not born of the soul, the soul would not be able to speak of beauty, splendor, and adornment at all. Rather, in order for it to be generated from the soul, she said: what she saw in her world of beauty and honor was an incomplete story — incomplete without the story of the soul. So understand what he said.

We say:

If there is any precedence in the discussion over the intellect, the soul, nature, and the existence of the above — its existence over others — and otherwise, it implies the correct statement, then I quote it and we do not need to elaborate with certainty upon what continued the mistake in perception. And in that is enough.
