<!-- page 121 -->
Chapter Four of the Book of Riyadh

On the judgment between ((the hunters)) — so the discussion concerning it, on the soul being parts and effects.

Chapter One of Chapter Four

The author of the Reform said:

According to what the author of al-Nusrah has proved: as for the parts that are in us, they are traces of the first essence, not parts of it. Our union is with the effects of the first essence, not with its parts; and the union of the first is not by its parts, because the first is united by the very word. The parts that are in us, even though they are not part of it, nor is it united by them, are united by its monuments — as we have shown — by the union of its products with our monuments within it, not by the union of the first essence, which is likewise in the word, because the first essence is united by the very word, until he and the word became one thing. This is what he said.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

This statement is confused and contradictory in appearance, because he said:
- 119

<!-- page 122 -->
that the parts that are in us are traces of the first essence — so how can the parts be relics, yet be essences in their own right? (And the effects do not (1) belong to it by strength in themselves.) And if he says that the parts that are in us are traces of the first essence, after signing the name of "parts," he must indicate that they are parts of an essence. If they were not parts of the first essence, it would not have been said that they are parts of an essence; and since it has been proven that they are parts of an essence, the absurdity of saying that they are traces follows — because the parts of a thing are not traces of another, as he said.

We say:

The statement of the author of al-Nusrah in what he said — that the souls are (2) jewels existing by themselves after acquisition, and that the effects cannot exist by themselves — is true. But the statement of (3) the author of the Reform, in what he said, and his refusal to give souls the partial name of a thing — that is because he saw that the things which are integral are the bodies that are inseparable from the nine accidents, and which are aware of the five senses. The soul is not the body, nor does it overflow with an object, so he did not see fit to give it the name of "part," lest it be reduced to the course of bodies, so that it would not match what is said of it. And when he did not reply to this, he said "traces" and named them as essences and as remaining. What is meant by that is that these souls have their essence and survival together with the effects of those paradoxical essences that reach them from the side of the limits, which are the intermediaries, peace be upon them. If so, then it appears that the intention of the author of the Reform in saying that the effects of what is going on in the soul follow the course of the image — and what is done by it is essential and by it you will remain if you accept it — is what He obliged him to say where he says: but we have said:

(1) The sentence is omitted in copy (A).
(2) In copy (A), the soul is mentioned.
(3) It fell in copy (A).
- 120

<!-- page 123 -->
honor and credit belong to these worlds, because I have accepted (1) the effect of that noble essence as complete, and we were ready to accept it; for it is true that the arch wants what it wants. He accepts it. And what the author of al-Nusrah means when he says that it is part of what is going on in the soul is the stream of the hayuli, which is accepted — and this is by way of analogy and approximation to understandings.

Chapter Two of Chapter Four

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The souls are parts of the total soul, which is not based on evidence. If it is the sum of those parts, then all of them are divided into two parts. All these parts have obliged that the souls are parts of the total soul. The souls in the abode of this world are extended by the evidence that has been given in earlier and later discussions. The second is called the soul whose existence is not in the house of the body, which is the house of the world. And if there is no absurdity in this world's being the second, both of these souls, if it is not possible to be a whole composed of these souls, then these are not parts of them.

Chapter Three of Chapter Four

Then to assert that they are parts of the total soul is not correct with his intention that the total soul is the second. So the matter is not free in the soul, if it has parts, from one of these alternatives: the second must be either the whole of them — which is the second in the house of the world — or some of them, some in the higher world, or else not in the sense of a part in this world. And it is invalid for the whole that is second to be in the whole house of the world, because in this world it stands by potentiality, and there is no way for it to do anything except by what already exists.

(1) In copy (A), we said.
(2) The sentence was completely omitted in (b).
- 121

<!-- page 124 -->
And the first intellect refuses to be that by which the existing souls come out by potentiality into actuality, because of the precedence required of that which is impossible for God Almighty — based on what has been said in books other than this. It is likewise invalid that some are in this world and some in the higher world, because some of them in the higher world — if they are such as those in the world — stand by potentiality for what they are, and this is the case. And this is invalidated by the evidence that the existence of the second is an already existing existence, not like the existence of the abode of the world, which is a potentiality standing. After the invalidity of these two divisions, it has been proven that the second is not part of the world, and it is invalid that the souls should be a part of it.

Chapter Four of Chapter Four

After the parts, he gives what he gives all of them according to them. These souls were parts of the second, and we found them neither knowledgeable nor wise — all of them are parts of this category. A belief such as this, to that extent of greatness, is unbelief; we seek refuge in God if it be known that the materials of the Messengers — God's prayers be upon them — are from it, that being the closest cause of the overflow upon the world below. The souls also (1) — if they are not parts of the following — then when these souls are not parts, we said: the souls belong to the nature of those paradoxical things whose existence is creative; their appearance and existence are only among the natural and phenomenal worlds. It is a natural force that acquires what is gained from the limits through obedience and worship (2). So it emits from itself an abstract remaining image that is like the abstract things in the House of the Hereafter on the one hand, and the opposite of it on the other.

(1) It fell in copy (b).
(2) It fell in copy ((A)).
- 122


<!-- page 125 -->

Hence we say:

This is the second source, and our writings on the Ma'ad include mention of that, God willing.

Chapter Five of Title IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Likewise, our antiquities, joined to our manufactures, do not accept our antiquities after we have finished our work; nor do they accept any artifact ready to be received from us, except by the introduction of corruption into them and the change of them from their states,(1) because they are not parts of us, nor are they existing essences.(2) They are merely ephemeral accidents. So he said.

And we say:

What is meant by this is that bodies are limited in what they can accept — to the point that they receive nothing further. It is like iron held in fire: once it has gone red, that is its end; it grows no hotter. Or like a black body: it does not grow blacker. It may end in a blackness past which it cannot be further blackened. And that is from the very meaning he himself first acknowledged, as his own phrase requires: it is not the traces that are the receiver, but the receiver — that is, the body subject to the succession of accidents and effects.

Chapter Six of Title IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

But the speaking souls within us continue to gain from the light of the Most High and Most Noble world, and they increase in honor in themselves without any change occurring in themselves; nor does any corruption enter into them from the soul when it wishes to benefit them. It is true that they

(1) In copy (b), the same is stated.
(2) In copy (b) it is mentioned as "in itself."

<!-- page 126 -->

are parts and essences of the soul that bear no traces upon them.

We say:

The matter is that the soul is unlimited in its capacity to accept what he described, which is one of the proofs that the soul is not embodied. And the matter, regarding the soul being parts of the Second, is not as he claimed and laid out. The most we can grant him is to say: as for those who wish to call them parts, these speaking souls are receivers if they are rational. As such, the soul and what it has produced are one thing; the speaking soul is identical with it and not distinguished from it except by rank — like the part of a thing in which there exists what is found in the whole, their path in themselves being one and the same from this side.

Chapter Seven of Title IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Also, to claim that the speaking souls existing in this world are not parts of the Universal Soul nullifies prophethood, imamate, walaya, revelation, and the divine laws. For it would not then be possible for the soul to perceive what influences it, nor to encompass it, nor to benefit from it. Yet the prophets (peace be upon them) claimed that they had seen the noble Alawi world, and stood upon the spiritual hudud, and benefited from what they brought back of light and manifestation, from those above them — from the origin and the three branches — and that they had seen the angels. If their pure souls were only traces of the Universal Soul, they would not have been able to comprehend what was inspired in them, nor to stand upon those spiritual objects which are parts of the Universal Soul.

We say:

It has already been said that the souls are not mere traces but parts, and that they are

<!-- page 127 -->

a second emanation. What is meant by the one who said that they are effects is that what is done with them belongs essentially to the souls and earns them their survival. And in the view of those who said they are parts, the souls — by their own intellect — are like the things that have produced them. Prophethood is not invalidated by this, nor imamate, nor anything else, for it has been established that these ranks are not of these. The prophets (peace be upon them) were able to perceive these absent things from the upper world, after they reached the rank of perfection — becoming, at its end, in the Sixties. When they return to themselves, it is as though they have realized that the transcendent selves are one thing in respect of the whole, with no difference except in rank. We have set this out in our well-known book, and the peace of mind that comes of that is sufficient and curative.

Chapter Eight of Title IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Rather, it must be said that the Intellect is creative through the Word, the Soul is the image of the Intellect, nature is born of the Soul, and the speaking souls are parts of the Universal Soul — so that in this order tawhid, reward, punishment, prophethood, imamate, sharia, and revelation become coherent.

We say:

The validity of tawhid, of reward and punishment, of prophethood and imamate, of the message and of the laws drawn from revelation, is not proved from the side that the Intellect is creative through the Word and that the Soul is the image of the Intellect. Rather, it is established from another side, set out in our letters and our writings about them. As for the Intellect, it is not permissible to say that it is creative through the Word, because the Word must be presented to the Intellect, on account of the imbalance in its paths(1) toward

(1) In Version (B), the remote stated:

<!-- page 128 -->

tawhid, according to what we have explained in matters of speech.

Then we say:

The Intellect is nothing(1) but the Word itself, and the Word is nothing but the Intellect itself. It is correct to say that Mawlana Imam al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah (peace be upon him), in *The Interpretation of Sharia*, where he discusses intelligibles, says: the Word is the Intellect, and these names properly belong to it. Our explanation and our discourse about the Creator, the Intellect, the Word, and what is said of the world of creativity, of the first rebirth in the *illuminated message*, and of the rest of the letters, are enough for the researcher. The Soul and nature emanate from the First Intellect, each of them according to its own proportion, so that one of them is more excellent than the other — the first emanation being the Soul, and the speaking souls being the second emanation. What occurs in the first of them is repeated in the last of them. Our messages alone, in the repetitions that mention this, include what is not necessary to dwell on at length; and being extravagant in speech is likewise the case in every forbidden place. A gesture suffices for the seeking student; others reject it because it is hard to reach and hard to know. May Allah help the believer who knows this according to his strength in devotion to Allah and His guardians, and help him if he is sincere to His face, and condemn him and his guardians if he renounces them. May Allah make us among the followers able to fulfill their covenant; we are blessed with His praise and glory.

(1) In copy (b) it is not mentioned.

<!-- page 129 -->

Chapter Five of the Book of Riyad

On the judgment between the two disputants — I speak of him — since human beings are the fruit of the physical world.

Chapter One of Chapter Five

The author of al-Islah said:

Man is the fruit of this whole world, and it is for him that this world has been created in all its parts: its origins, its foundations, the first of which are the principles of the individuals that are born of them by motion and rest; then the individuals, which are the foundation of vehicles; then the compounds, which are the very productions of this whole world; and the natures from which the form of the human being is composed — the form which is prepared to receive the three souls: the vegetative, the sensitive, and the speaking.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

To say that human beings are the fruit of this world is also the saying of the materialists, those who did not affirm the survival of the soul after the dissolution of bodies. For(1) if human beings were the fruit of this world, this world would have held him and would not have let him move to another world.

But we would say:

(1) It fell into copy (b).

<!-- page 130 -->

Human beings are the fruit of the highest world, the honorable and illuminating world; and for this reason the prophets (peace be upon them) came and warned them to provide themselves, from the house of mortality to the house of survival, and from this world to the abode of the Ma'ad — because they knew that this world is not a home, and that man is not the fruit of this world. So he said.

We say:

Each is truthful in what he said on one side, and each is untruthful on another. The side on which they are both truthful is that each of them has said of human beings what belongs to him. The side on which they are both untruthful is in denying the other its share. For man rightly bears the title of fruit of all the worlds, because human beings issue from between the two worlds. From this world he receives his first perfection; from that world he receives his second. For the end of the natural world is to give his existence its first perfection — which is the issuance of the bodily form, by way of the circular bodies, the luminous planets, and the proximate causes, into the form by which he is ready to accept what belongs to him; and he has the right to accept, and it is his fruit, beyond which there is almost nothing. And the end of that world is to give what is beyond the existence of the natural world its second perfection, which is the consummation of the spiritual image,(2) the precursor to the natural thing, intrinsic to it, and which endures: the knowledge of the Oneness of Allah Almighty(3) by means of all the hudud (may Allah's peace be upon them), and obedience to them in both modes of worship — in knowledge and in deed. That is its fruit. This corrects what we have said in that statement of Mawlana Amir al-Mu'minin al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah Ma'ad ibn Isma'il (may Allah's prayers be upon him) in *The Interpretation of Sharia*, where he says that man is the purpose of the second world and the end of the action of nature.

Chapter Two of Chapter Five

As for the statement of the author of al-Nusrah and his objection that, if human beings were the fruit of this world,

(1) Fell into copy (b).
(2) It fell into copy (1).

<!-- page 131 -->

this world would have held him and would not have let him move from it to another world — he has handed his opponent the reversal of the matter against him.

He says:

If, as I have said, man is the fruit of the higher world, then that world would hold him and not let him return to these people, so that he forgets — and that is impossible, as I have written and required.

This is neither a statement that proves what he intends to demonstrate in the abstract, nor is it what he himself has said about the prophets being sent: that their being sent is proof that human beings are the fruit of this world. Human beings are in fact human beings precisely because they are formed by the meeting of the overflow of the worlds: from the natural world, what flows in the channel of the bodies; and from the world of the Intellect, what flows in the channel of the form. And this is in the middle.

Chapter Three of Chapter Five

The author of al-Islah said:

The Soul emanates from the First Intellect in perfection, and nature emanates from the Soul in perfection, and the image emanates from the Hayula in perfection — and the whole was perfect.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

If the matter is as he said about the perfection of the Soul being like the Intellect, then his statement about what is spread out upon the figures, the image, and the compounds is invalid and is not reduced. It is not permissible to imagine that these manufactures arise only from the pairing of one rich and perfect with another deficient and in need, so that a marriage takes place between them, as is found among witnessed things — such as the heavens and the earth. The heavens are like the male: perfect and self-sufficient in all their visible motions and orbits; and the earth is deficient in the rank of the female, and her need for him is established.(1) For this reason a marriage took place between them, and out of that union appeared various kinds of metals,

(1) In Version (A) it came as "constant."

<!-- page 132 -->

forms of differing shape, plants, and animals — none of which actually appears visibly in the heavens, nor in the earth, nor in the mothers, but they were in them in potency. When they were married, all of them appeared in actuality. Even if they had not been, as we mentioned, the one deficient and the other perfect, what appeared from them would not have appeared. So he said.

We say:

As for the statement of the author of al-Islah that the Soul is the second emanation from the First Intellect in perfection — that is correct, according to what has been said above. As for his statement that the Hayula emanates from the Soul: we have shown above that its existence is not from the Soul, for the Soul is the second from the First Intellect, and the second of them is not contingent upon the other on the basis of the other, and there is no existence of one of them in abstraction from the other except in the soul by way of imagination. Outside the soul, they exist only together. As for the statement of the author of al-Nusrah that it is not permissible to imagine that the products issue only from the pairing of one perfect and rich with another deficient and in need, so that they may be coupled by what is between them — and his likening of this to the heavens and the earth — he has refuted himself with the very words that followed: that various kinds of metals appear among them, with their forms, and plants, and animals, none of which actually appear in the heavens, nor in the earth, nor in the mothers, because they were in potency. And this is to say that some of these things — I mean the generated beings — even if they do not actually appear in the heavens and the earth, are there in potency. The heavens and the earth are equal in respect of their being in potency. The fact that one of them did not actually appear, in preference over the other, is not because actual existence is found between two things one of which must be perfect and the other deficient. We have shown that deficiency is found only in things — not in their selves, but in their ranks when they are added to one another, and in their actions when they do not exist except in something other than that which is done with their existence. In itself each is perfect; for if their selves were not perfect in the existence that befits them, nothing could be done by them — neither by themselves nor by another. The state of created things bears witness to this: the heaven, which has been singled out for perfection and artistry,


<!-- page 133 -->
It is incomplete in its action, since the action is not performed except by that which it is performed upon, and the earth is specific in this regard. By way of its diminishing and its need, its deficiency lies not in itself but in its action; and even then, the action is performed only by the one who seeks it. The same holds for the animal and for newborns. If this is so, then the saying of the author of al-Nusrah — that the statement of the author of al-Islah is a contradictory and prejudiced statement — is itself contradictory and prejudiced.

**Chapter Four of Chapter Five**

The author of al-Nusrah said:
He did not answer what was raised at the end of this chapter about the name *al-Tamamiyyah* (perfection) falling upon the Creator Almighty, in order to make it fall upon complete creativity.

We say:
The author of al-Islah mentioned nothing of the sort. The statement he quoted from the sages in the book of al-Islah — that the perfect Creative is complete creativity, and that what is perfect was only perfect — is a saying than which none is more correct. It obliges us to say that the Perfect and the perfected is the Creator Almighty, and that the name *al-Tamamiyyah* is attributed to the Creator (swt). His statement contains nothing of what the author of al-Nusrah ascribes to him. The author of al-Nusrah's claim here is prejudice against the author of al-Islah.

**Chapter Five of Chapter Five**

The author of al-Nusrah said:
We all say that creativity is the effect of the Creator, and that he and the Creator are, on the side of the Creator, one thing — which is perfection; and the Creative with complete creativity is only perfect. Nor do we need to say that the Creator is perfect, or that he is the cause, or that he — glorified and exalted — and the Creative are one thing.
- 131

<!-- page 134 -->
We say:
That is a correct statement, but we do not say that the Creator is complete creativity — only perfection — because creativity is perfection. Rather we say that the Creative is the perfection, only perfect, and the Creator is the creativity; he is the cause, the effect [*al-maʿlul*], the perfect, and the perfected. These names are synonymous according to the additions, because there is a multiplicity of meanings upon which these names fall, yet there is only one thing. We have mentioned this in this book and in others. Enough of the length.

**Chapter Six of Chapter Five**

The author of al-Nusrah said:
If it is proven that this world is the act of the soul — and that it is nevertheless highly ordered and arranged, with perpetual circular motions — we know with certainty that in her action she sought to reach the noble rank before she actually began. Since this was so, no honorable being existed in the world that was not able to receive the effects of the soul from her. We know with certainty that human beings are the fruit of the soul's attainment of her end, in measure of her cause — so understand that.

We say:
We have already said the following: the universal soul, in what is indicated of her, did not seek in her action to reach a rank she had not attained. The universal soul, which is the second, overflows upon this world not to be drawn to it for what is not hers — God enriched her when he originated her, with no need to seek what she was not given. Rather, her action is to establish wisdom in bringing what is in potentiality into actuality, and to make what is
- 132

<!-- page 135 -->
a virtuous mean. His existence is not delayed by what is above him, and if he meditates, the benefit accrues from what comes out of the action, and to the one who is beneath the task of being satisfied in perfection.[^1] No one is without cost, no one without need; and if he believes that in that great expanse there lies a mistake and a sin — if that is the case, then human beings have two sides: one part natural, one part divine. He is not the fruit of this world alone, nor the fruit of that world alone, but the fruit of all the worlds. He is the last in the universes, united in eternity with what has been acquired by time. Therefore it has been said that what came first of them was the last of them. And whoever knew the houses of the Prophets, the guardians, the imams, and the righteous — may God's prayers be upon them — from that, then he is ready for salvation. May God gather us with our pure loyalists in *Dar al-Quds*.

**Chapter Seven of Chapter Five**

As for the saying of the author of al-Nusrah — that every doer does what he does in pursuit of what he wishes to achieve, especially when the action is from the doer in movement, in order, and in affirmation — this is not necessary for the First Creator and Originator, because the action there is metaphorical, not in motion nor in order. Rather, the first is the last; the beginning of it is the middle, and the middle of it is the end. There is no order in it, no system; rather, the systems and order in what comes after benefit from the lights of what is not in order or system — that is the First Intellect. The soul may be very similar to the intellect: if you look to her, you perceive the forces of things that are not orderly and not in arrangement, but you perceive them as a whole. Yet she has that character only if she unites with the intellect and pushes back the deformed religious things[^2] of dark nature.

[^1]: In copy (B) it came as "in attaining perfection."
[^2]: Dropped in copy (A).

- 133

<!-- page 136 -->
We say:
How can what he said be true — that order and arrangement exist only from the intellect? Or how is it true that general things are preferred only by the intellect, and the soul is not affected by the effects of the intellect — so that she and the animals are alike? Why then has God lifted knowledge from the soul until the effects of the intellect bear upon her? We have already said that the issue does not lie with the actors: not every actor seeks by his action to attain a goal or purpose, which one might appreciate by saying that the First Intellect itself is not a vehicle, so it is orderly and arranged. But as for perception — the intellect perceives only the truth.

- 134

<!-- page 137 -->
**Chapter Six of the Book of al-Riyad**

In which he speaks of motion, stillness, hyle, and form.

**Chapter One of Chapter Six**

The author of al-Islah said:
As for the saying about bodies and form: they are the spirit of motion and stillness, just as the body is for them. The first hyle is the imagined motion in the vehicles, and the hyles in the second [rank] are in the third. What has been said about that in this book contains much confusion. In short, I say: motion and stillness are like the soul to form and hyle, because form and hyle are like the body to motion and stillness. The soul is gentler than the body, and the body is denser than the soul; likewise motion and stillness are gentler than hyle and form, and hyle and form are denser than motion and stillness. To represent the gentler with the soul and the denser with the body is premature; but to represent the denser with the body and the gentler with the soul — this is clear, and the proof of it is with him.

The author of al-Nusrah said:
This is not the case of the soul and the body, where the measure between them is only the gentler and the denser.
- 135

<!-- page 138 -->
For if the case were as he mentioned, then the tangible essences would have to be objects of the accidents — since they are denser than the accidents — and the accidents would have to be souls for them, since they are gentler than them. Rather, we must look to the limit of the body and the soul, and then liken what resembles the limit of the soul to the soul, and what resembles the limit of the body to the body. We say: we found the body to accept the three dimensions — length, width, and depth. We found the soul to have parts that resemble each other in like manner. We found motion to be divided into three sections, just as the body is divided — namely, motion from the center, motion toward the center, and motion around the middle. So motion must resemble the body in its division, splitting as the body splits into length, width, and depth. Not so for the hyle: the parts of the hyle resemble each other, and because they resemble motion with the body in the chapter of division, and the soul in similarity of parts, the first thing that resembles motion with the soul is one of them — which is the motion of the indivisible body.[^2] The resemblance of the hyle is undivided, while the other is the body which is divided. This is what they said.

We say:
In this chapter the two of them have strayed far from representation and simile; and between us and the two of them lies a corrupt foundation upon which the proof is built — neither the proverb nor its representation is true. The author of al-Islah has made it obligatory that motion and stillness be like the soul, since the mover is the act of the engine, and the mover is what we call the soul — the soul. Likewise stillness is the engine letting

[^1]: Dropped in copy (A).
[^2]: Dropped in copy (A).

- 136

<!-- page 139 -->
what it moves go. And if the engine is the soul, then to represent the motion which is its act by it is to represent the impossible — and the same goes for stillness. And if this is invalidated for motion and stillness, then it is also invalidated that motion and stillness are hyle and form: the path of hyle and form is not the path of motion and stillness. Motion and stillness, even when they are actualized, are two opposites that do not meet in the same place at the same time. As for form and hyle: their existence together is such that neither is separable from the other, provided that one of them is an actor — which is the form — and the other is acted upon in it — which is *al-hayula*. Our book known as *The Rest of the Mind* contains the explanation of that.

**Chapter Two of Chapter Six**

Then he was obliged to say that the soul is gentler than the body, because the soul is a body — and that is impossible. The soul cannot be embodied with density or gentleness, for density and gentleness are properties only[^1] of that which is a body, just as air, which is a body, is characterized by gentleness, and earth, which is a body, is characterized by density. The soul is the soul, and the proof rests on the fact that it is not embodied. And if the soul is not embodied, then it is impossible to argue that it is gentler or denser, or that it is represented by such terms.

**Chapter Three of Chapter Six**

The author of al-Nusrah came and promised to set the limit of the body and the soul. He limited the body by its accepting the three dimensions, and the soul by the union of its parts in similarity — illustrated by a stream of water, fire, or air, each of which is similar in its parts. It is impossible

[^1]: Quotations appear in copy (A).

- 137

<!-- page 140 -->
to go beyond saying that the soul is not embodied. *Al-nafs* and *al-ruh* are two synonyms for the same thing, and that thing is not a body. For if it were a body, the body would necessarily require of it the nine qualities and accidents that the body needs, and it could be perceived by sense. But since it is not perceived by sense, it is not perceivable, and the nine accidents do not exist in it. Since the physical accidents — the hallmark of being a body — do not exist in it [it is not a body]. Our well-known book *With Lamps* contains what heals this.

**Chapter Four of Chapter Six**

Then he made motion to be divided into three sections similar to the body, as already mentioned — namely, that it is divided into length, width, and depth. This is impossible. The three sections into which the body is allegedly divided — which are the three dimensions — each one of these three dimensions is not embodied; it is not said to be a body. And the motion of each of its sections is itself a motion, and is said to be a motion. So how can it be likened to the body — which is divided into nine sections, as we have shown in our well-known book *Milestones of Religion* — with a motion divided into three sections, or six sections? That is far-fetched. The discussion in this chapter from the author of *al-Mahsul*, the author of al-Islah, and the author of al-Nusrah is a disordered disorder.

We say:
Motion is that by which the same thing changes — either by place, or by universe, or by corruption, or by increase, or by decrease, or by impossibility. Its occurrence is from the mover, which is the principle in the thing in which the motion resides — and of course not by width. Hyle and form are the first emanation of the following proportion, just as the second is upon the noble proportion within it. There is no existence of the soul outside the soul in the first place,
- 138

<!-- page 141 -->
[but only] together with form — including the heavens and the earth and everything between them. God Almighty created all of this from the First Intellect, which was itself created by God, not from any thing, at a great height.

**Chapter Five of Chapter Six**

The author of al-Islah said:
The first cause, which is creativity, is the word of the Almighty and Exalted, and its form is *kun* ("Be!"), which is two letters — the vowelled *kaf* and the *nun*. The consonant — the *kaf* and the *nun* — of the word stand in the position of the body, while motion and stillness are like the soul. This is the example of motion and stillness with the word, and the first cause, which is the word, will be the whole world, by way of example. It must be said that in motion and stillness, in all the essences of the hyles, form is below them in all the worlds; and the whole of the discourse is in the three worlds. The first beings are hyles — they are motion and stillness — and they are the first hyles, their form, and the second hyles, which are individuals and generators of the first hyles. And the third hyles are the four composite mother [elements] of the second hyles.

Then we say:
The hyle and its form are the act of the command, which is creativity. As we have said, the hyles are imaginary; so too is creativity imaginary — it shows itself only in the First Creator.

The author of al-Nusrah said:
As for what was raised by the author of al-Islah concerning *kun*: he said that the *kaf* is movable and the *nun* is stationary, and that this is like the body, and like motion and stillness.
- 139


<!-- page 142 -->
As for the soul, it is not established that these two letters apply to it, for there is no kaf, no nun, no motion or stillness above the First Effect. It cannot be said that above the First Effect there is motion or stillness or anything of that sort. Rather, there is only pure creativity and complete reason. This is what they say.

We say:

The claim by the author of al-Islah — that the First Cause, which is creativity, is a word of God whose image consists of two letters — is a remote analogy, however he may have intended it, and it scarcely holds. If we say "image," that implies something that has an image, and not a word. The image is not a picture; if by "image" we mean what is above nature, we mean the abstract image; and if by "image" we mean what belongs to this natural world, we mean the image that is in matter. And if he says that creativity, which is the word of God Almighty and His command as expressed by it, consists of two letters — one of them movable and the other consonant — to indicate the union of two attributes, then this points to the noble Self and its inferred qualities.

He said:

It combines two states, just as the word "be" combines motion and stillness; the two states are attributed to that Self as it relates to two honorable proportions, one of which is being a cause and the other being effect. The motion that ends in stillness corresponds to the proportion whose cause ends in the effect, and the stillness that ends in motion corresponds to the proportion that is effect ending in cause — not because the First has motion or stillness in itself, but only by way of analogy. And even so, the analogy holds only by being first and by accident, which is what the one who calls the First an actor in this manner must concede.

<!-- page 143 -->
Section Six of Chapter Six

Then he said:

The First is illusory, and he made it motion and stillness, then he made it motion, stillness, and hyle. The image is not the hyles themselves; rather, motion and stillness are the hyles, and the image is an essence emanating from the First in the manner already discussed above. In the lower world, motion and stillness exist in bodies along the principles by which they appear: hyle and image together constitute the body, the body being shape and picture.

Section Seven of Chapter Six

Then he said:

When he made the First Hyle out of the matter which is creativity, the First Hyle is imaginary, just as creativity is imaginary, and only shows itself in the First Creator. He said that this must be in the presence of the Almighty, because of His pride in it — that creativity, before it has appeared, has not yet become creative through creativity. And this is a grave error. For the doctrine and belief in monotheism is that other than Allah Almighty — who is Creative and First — nothing has any reality in existence at all; and if anything did, then creativity would be nothing other than the Creative itself. We have built upon this guide so that we may confine ourselves to our own letters.

Section Eight of Chapter Six

[1 — lacuna in copy (A).]

The author of al-Nusrah said:

What he said, and what the author of al-Islah differed with him about, is correct — except that what he said—

<!-- page 144 -->
—it should not be said that above the First Effect there is motion and rest, because that would assert motion and rest of the First Effect and of what is below it. We have already shown that the First Effect is not movable, that motion does not befit it, and that there is no stillness in it either, because it is other than a body.

Section Nine of Chapter Six

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Contradictions occur in bodies, not in spirits.

We say:

Bodies are bodies because they are objects; there is no distinction between them as such, and no contradiction among them. The difference and the contrast lie in the images within them — what animates them — not in their being bodies.

He said:

And because what is in them resembles what was already there, the matter rests upon what he said, if the very speaker is of that sort.

<!-- page 145 -->
Chapter Seven of the Book of al-Riyad

On the ruling between the two.

I now speak of it in the sections that follow on the world.

Section One of Chapter Seven

The author of al-Islah said:

The whole world is composed of four jewels, four structures, and four classes of beings — three of them without humankind, the fourth being humankind. The knotted and the dissolved are all one essence. We do not say that the pair of humankind is the horse's form; rather, the pair of the horse and its form is what belongs to its essence — just as the pair of gold and silver, the pair of ruby and emerald, so too the pair of the horse and its form. And its shape is that of the animals: the elephant, the falcon, the lion, and so on. Because they are all animals, it is not permissible to say that the horse is the pair and form of humankind, since humankind is alone in possessing the rational soul and has no peer among the parts of the world and its generated beings. Rather, humankind is the counterpart of the world, because it gathers in itself all the jewels of the world. For this reason it is called the small world — the microcosm — because it stands as a counterpart that collects all the world's parts.

<!-- page 146 -->
We say:

His claim that humankind has no peer among the parts of the world, and that humankind is the counterpart of the whole world, follows from his saying that the world is divided into four parts and that the horse is not in the form of humans, and that humans have no peer among the world's parts but are rather the equivalent of the whole world.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The state of pairing is not only the state of sexual union; the state of pairing is also the state of acceptance.

We say:

This dense lower world is the pair of the subtle upper world, and if you look between them you will find more between them than between the horse and the human. The horse is not of the human race, except insofar as it shares in the genus of life. He made this dense world a pair of the spiritual world by virtue of the connection between them in the chapter of benefit and beneficence: the forces of the upper world are linked to this world, and this world receives those forces from the upper world. So he made this world the pair of that world; likewise the human and the horse. But he made the horse the human's pair because it accepts teaching from him more than the other animals accept it — hence his saying that he made the horse a pair for humankind, since it accepts teaching from him as no other animal does.

We say:

The investigators of these matters did not rely on sound research. The author of al-Islah divided the world into four jewels, four structures, and four classes including humankind — and that is impossible, because the divisions of the world are more numerous than what we have mentioned. "World" is a name that refers to many things, from the center to the encompassing ocean — the throne, the planets, fire, air, water, earth, and the born — according to what he estimated and inferred. It is clear from his words that he means the generated beings of the world, not the world and its births themselves, which are three genera with no fourth, each of them having many species under it. The first of these is—

<!-- page 147 -->
—metal, the nearest of the four arches: everything that exists in a specific place in the earth without growing in its parts, gathering together what dissolves and what does not, what breaks and what does not. The second is plant — all that grows from the earth, gathering together the bush and the tree. The third is animal — all that moves of those that have species, gathering together what flies and what does not. The generated beings of the world do not exceed this; and the human, mentioned as the fourth, is of the genus of animal yet distinguished by a kind of consciousness.

Section Two of Chapter Seven

He said:

That humankind has no peer and that the horse has no pair — this is impossible from two angles. The first: humankind is a type within the animal genus, and as a type it is a pair to the other types within its genus, since all types are forms of one another in the chapter on quality — so if humankind is a type, it is a pair to the other types. The second: humankind, since it is similar in its structure, morals, actions, conditions, motions, and natures to all that the universe of existence presents — as he himself mentioned, and as is the very reason it is called the small world — must be a pair to all things, in accordance with the occasion and the case at hand. And if this is so, then humankind, if it is good, virtuous, wise, religious, knowing, and pure, is married to the angels by the occasion between them; if it is courageous, brave, strong, and a helper [1], its pair is the lions; if it is unjust, cruel, and murderous, then its pair is the wolves and the cunning foxes; if it is treacherous and thievish, then its pair is the crows and the hooks; if it is deceitful, cunning, and overpowering, then its pair is the tiger.

[1 — lacuna in copy.]
Kitab al-Riyad [10]

<!-- page 148 -->
If he is adulterous, immoral, given to amusement and play, then his pair is the monkeys. And on this pattern, whatever a person is content to resemble — whether by his actions, his morals, his customs, or in any part of them — that becomes his pair. If all this is established, then what he said is not borne out in the chapter where he claimed that humankind has no peer. Humankind has many forms and many pairs from many sides.

Section Three of Chapter Seven

As for the author of al-Nusrah, he said that the author of al-Islah was correct in the meaning of pairing, and he traced the matter back, saying that he made the horse a pair for humankind because the horse accepts teaching from humankind. But if it is on the basis of acceptance and education that the horse becomes its pair, then the horse has no priority over the other animals to be its pair, for among the animals there are those that accept teaching and discipline other than the mare; and if it is on the basis of quality, again the mare is not unique in being a pair. Such a creature would have priority over all the other animal species, having many pairs rather than a single one.

In sum we say:

In truth, the pair of humankind can only be the angels — and what we mean by "humankind" here are those of all virtues: the prophets, the guardians, and the imams (prayers of God be upon them) and those who follow in their footsteps, uphold their obedience, and worship Allah with both worships. Not those people who are merely in the form of humankind whose souls are the souls of beasts and predators — as we have said and shown — like monkeys, donkeys, foxes, lions, and wolves. As for those who walk in the traces of the Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them and above them) from among the prophets and guardians (may God's prayers be upon them) — their diligence in obedience through the two acts of worship, and their adherence to the command and prohibition, distinguishes them from the beasts even as they resemble them in form. (Peace be upon them.) So they will be saved in their entirety, and none will be lost from them, except as the verse says, "and he who takes them as guardian from among you—

<!-- page 149 -->
—is one of them." May Allah make us among those who become his servants and grant us company with the prophets, the guardians, and the pure Imams, may Allah's prayers be upon them all.

Section Four of Chapter Seven

The author of al-Nusrah said:

He mentioned (Bandhaqlis), who said:

The bestial soul refers to the soul that speaks of love; and as for the speaking soul — if what returns from her is good, she will be kind to her with benefit and victory. Her image is desirable, and there is no better image among the bestial souls than the image of the horse, and none more eager to receive the effects of the speaking soul — so he made the horse the pair for which this is manifest.

And we say:

If the author of al-Nusrah had drawn his evidence from the Book of Allah, Lord of the Worlds, and from the sayings of our followers, the pure Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them all), to support what he wants concerning the matter of the soul from the angle of interpretation, that would be richer than the sayings of the Prophet. If he and his peers had lived in our time, they would have had to draw from the seas of the knowledge of the House of Revelation — which overflows from the reservoir of God in His land — and from our pure loyalist Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them all). They would have spoken differently about much of what they held; being absent from the senses, speaking from the minds and their ranks, the soul and its principles, they would have returned and believed otherwise. They would have grasped that Murad (Banadqlis) said what he said in a sense other than what the author of al-Nusrah took it for — namely, that the bestial soul is the horse and the speaking soul is humankind, if returned to the balance of consideration and taken on the side that is correct. Since the sensual soul, which is bestiality, would accept the ruling of the speaking soul if nothing impedes it, and the speaking soul, when it sees itself as the midwife of—

<!-- page 150 -->
—that ruling, would kindle its judgment in accordance with what it considers, we say: by "the sensual soul" he means those in the form of humankind who would accept education, discipline, and reason — not the horse — and by "the speaking soul" he means the teachers, who are the prophets, the guardians, the imams (peace be upon them), and their followers, who would be kind to those in the form of humankind, teach them their interests in religion and worldly affairs, benefit them in ways they do not perceive with their senses, and raise them in degrees according to their acceptance — being in truth the mothers of humankind. No: everyone agrees with what is indicated by the interpretation of the saying of Allah Almighty, "Reassured soul, return to your Lord, pleased and pleasing" (the verse). To take his statement upon the correct face is preferable to taking it upon a face that is not correct. The horse is not more accepting than those in the form of humans, nor is its image better than the human image; nor are the guardians, imams, and their followers less in sympathy with such people than some humans are in the sport and gentling of the horse — they are far more so. If this is the case, and no plain statement shows the contrary, there is no evidence that the image of the horse is the best among the bestial soul-images, nor that the existing animal images surpass the goodness of the horse's image, nor — as he said — that the horse must be the pair of humankind on the side of education and good image. To invoke this is impossible.

Section Five of Chapter Seven

The author of al-Islah said:

Humankind is the counterpart of the whole world and is preferred to it by a noble essence that is not of this world (1). Its body stands in contrast to all bodies through their perceiving genera.

[1 — lacuna in copy (B).]
[2 — lacuna in copy (A).]


<!-- page 151 -->
and solidity, (3) and the dissolved, because in his body there is something corresponding to each of these — such as bone, flesh, nerves, veins, and cartilage (4) — and all the liquid substances (5) that flow from his body, such as blood, semen, sweat, and what flows from the eye, ear, mouth, and other parts. All of these belong to the essence of bodies and to their kinds, which correspond to the genera of the essence that is one of the three essences of the world: the essence of bodies, which is not joined to any of the three breaths. His growth, set parallel to the growing species — the various kinds of trees — corresponds to the kinds of the second essence, which is joined to the developing soul. His animal sense, in its different kinds, corresponds to the kinds of the third essence, which is joined to the sensitive soul, namely the beasts. This is his meeting-point with the three jewels of the world, and his uniqueness lies in the fourth rank: he became the fourth essence, which is the essence of the speaking soul, and he stands alone in this station. This is the upshot of the author's saying: "Human beings are the counterpart of the whole world; his body stands opposite all the bodies of the world in their different forms, his growth stands opposite the plants, his sense opposite the animal, and he possesses something preferable to all the rest and unique to him, which is the speaking soul that belongs to him alone."

The author of al-Nusrah said:

· As for his saying that the dissolved and the contracted are one essence, this is not warranted. Rather, the dissolved, the knotted, the growing, the sensitive, and the speaker are all one essence by way of genus; as for chapters and properties, the concluded essence is not identical with the dissolved, nor is the dissolved that is not coagulated identical with what is coagulated. They are distinguished only in the locus where they are distinguished, not in the locus where they were not distinguished. I needed to distinguish them because he made man a mediator between the dense physical world and the gentle spiritual world.

3 - Fell in copy (B).
4 - In copy (B), "the expenses" are mentioned.
5 - In the two versions, "the inanimate objects" — which is meaningless.
- 149

<!-- page 152 -->
For in him is everything that is in the world, so that everything in the lower world has its likeness in him; he leaves nothing of it out. He therefore set the bodies opposite his body — and his body is knotted, like the contracted bodies (6) — and then he set the dissolved bodies opposite his growth, because of the dissolution within them. And dissolution occurs only by the motion of the jewels that dissolve. In like manner, growth occurs only by motion; so when they resemble each other in the chapter of motion, each of them is almost identical with its companion. He set the body opposite his sense, and opposite this he set the animal sperm; the rest of the world's bodies, both knotted and dissolved, the developing and the sensitive, are gathered in him, and he is superior to the other essences. This is the upshot of his saying: Humans so closely resemble the world that nothing of it has been left out — his body corresponds to the bodies that are contracted, even though he also corresponds to the dissolved bodies; his sense corresponds to the beasts, his growth to the developing bodies; even if he is set in relation to animals by his utterance, he surpasses in logic all the other jewels.

We say:

The dispute between the two contenders in this chapter is that the author of al-Islah set, in the place of human growth, the plant of the great world that grows and develops; whereas the companion (the author of al-Nusrah), in contrast, set the dissolved bodies there. And the author of al-Islah set, opposite human animal-sense, the great animal world; the author of al-Nusrah set opposite his sense the developing bodies. And the author of al-Islah made of man's speaking soul the very thing that singled him out, preferred him over the world, and made him its master. The author of al-Nusrah made the speaker stand against the animal. The saying of the author of al-Islah is closer, in respect of likening, to the kind of thing he is comparing, and has a conformity that is not present in the opinion of the author of al-Nusrah. In general, the human being is not only a counterpart of this world; he is a counterpart for all the worlds, since he is the collector of all the things that preceded him in existence, similar to what is in him for both of them. The matter rests on this, not on what he said.

6 - Fell in copy (B).
- 150

<!-- page 153 -->
Chapter Six of Section Seven

Rather we said that man, who is a human being, is the counterpart of the worlds because he is the sum of many things that exist between the creation and the second revival. He then became one, and became parallel in himself to the creative and emanative world, and the collector embodies his bodies parallel to the physical world. The two worlds are thus alike. The world is composed entirely of body: of it the gentle, which is the high, and of it the dense, which is the low. The human body is likewise gentle and dense; the gentle is the high. In the world there are planets and stars revolving, and in the human body too there is skin surrounding the whole body and so on, which covers the bones. What runs in the course of the planets, such as the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth, finds its parallel in man as well. In him there is also a moving engine, which is the self; and in the world the four elements; and in the human being also the four natures; and in the world rivers, valleys, and waters of different tastes, mountains, and minerals — and in the human being likewise, as the author of al-Islah notes: bones, cartilage, flesh, blood, and what flows from him and what comes out of him. In the world there is what grows and develops without sensing, and in the human being there is also what grows and develops without sensing, such as hair and nails; and as the world grows and increases from its youth to its greatness, so too in the world there are sensitive animals, and in the human being likewise the strong sensitive faculties. The whole world is composed and complete, and has been supplemented by the existing — and this is an analogy to the world, although all that we have mentioned here is a summary that is detailed in our book known as the Peace of Mind. As for what is similar to it in the world of creativity and emanation: first, that world is the world of intellect, the world of praise and praising and purification, the world of monotheism and divinity, the world of virtues, the world of goodness, and the world of excellences. The human being, who is also a human being, is the collector of these virtues — of intellect, praise, monotheism, divinity, virtue, existence over others and in oneself.

- 151

<!-- page 154 -->
That world has impressed him, and his body has been marked by this world; and given that this is so, there is no distinction between him and what has gone before except by rank alone. May God place us among those who follow the traces of His guardians, and may He not exclude us from the intercession of His followers, by His grace and bounty.

Chapter Seven of Section Seven

The author of al-Islah said:

The human being is preferred over the world by the noble essence that is not of this world — not in the sense that the essence which was forbidden by saying it to be of this world is from that world; I mean the upper world, according to what he has set forth. Yet if he were only of that world and not of this world at all, we would not say that he is of all the worlds. He is in a state of existence between the two worlds, like the state of the boy in his existence between his two parents: it is not permissible to attribute (1) him to one of them alone, because he is from both of them, and from them he came to be. So it is permissible to attribute to this world what runs in him from the course of matter, which is the acceptable, and to attribute to that world what runs in him from the course of the image, which is the acceptable, just as in the case of the child who has, on the one hand, his parents in the highest and most honorable picture, and, on the other hand, the one who delivered that piece — namely the midwife. There are many proofs of this in our book known as the Peace of Mind. May God Almighty guide us to the home of His religion, and grant us the intercession of Muhammad and the Imams of his pure family — peace be upon them all.

(1) In copy (B), "the last of them" came.
- 152

<!-- page 155 -->
Chapter Eight of the Book of Riyad

On the Judgment between the Two Sages

(He spoke about this in the chapter of Decree and Destiny.)

Chapter One of Section Eight

The author of al-Islah said:

As for the saying that the decree is prior and the destiny posterior — this is wrong, because destiny is before decree, and the prior is before the posterior. It is not permissible that the decree, which is after destiny, should be that which is before the posterior. Decree and destiny are two words whose meaning is known from speech: destiny is estimation, and decree is detail. There is no detail except after estimation, and the first is before the second by the farthest measure of priority. The evidence for this is from the Book of God (peace and blessings of God be upon him), when He says: "The matter concerning which you took counsel has been decreed" — that is, separated and finished — and He says: "When you have finished the prayer," i.e., when you have completed it. The image is that of a garment which the tailor first estimates: he estimates it before he cuts it, and it can be increased, decreased, expanded, or narrowed; but if it has been dismissed (separated), it is finished, and increase is no longer possible.

1. In copy (A), "his protection" is stated.
۔
- 153

<!-- page 156 -->
Deficiency follows the same pattern as predestination and decree. This is the upshot of his saying: "Decree is the conclusion of destiny." It is not said of the prior that it comes after destiny, and it is not said of the posterior that it is destiny, because destiny is prior to decree: destiny is estimation, and decree is detail. As long as the thing remains in the stage of estimation, it can be increased, decreased, and expanded, but once it has passed into detail, it cannot be increased or decreased.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Decree is not the detail, because the case of the eye of detail in the decree is no less than the case of the separated; for the eye of the separated is fixed in its form by the making of a judgment that obliges it. Just so, the separated is also established by what has been decreed for it. As for destiny, it is the estimation, according to what he said; but estimation only happens through a judge who obliges that estimation. As for the evidence he has quoted from the Book of God — that the decree is emptying — this is unwarranted, because the wise have said: when God Almighty's intellect created an emptying of the worlds, since it is the sum of the images of the two worlds, and since the images of the worlds before the intellect are not strange to it but are one thing, it is a pure emptying. But the author of al-Islah has misjudged in likening destiny and decree to the dress that the tailor estimates. He made his estimation correspond to destiny and his elaboration correspond to the decree — and he did not get this analogy right. He ought to have made the image of the sewing that lies in the very soul of the tailor correspond to the decree, because it is spiritual; and the estimation by which the tailor estimates that sewing corresponds to destiny by what it represents — also a spiritual thing, which does not yet emerge beyond the limit of intellect, for this is the meaning of the image of originals. Then the elaboration, which he distinguishes after estimation, corresponds to the physical boundaries that emerged from the first and the second. If the matter is as we have described, then decree precedes destiny, just as sewing precedes appreciation; and by that sewing the tailor is prepared for the appreciation by which he appreciates. So it is true that the decree is prior and destiny posterior. This is the upshot of his saying:

- 154

<!-- page 157 -->
Decree is ahead of destiny, and there is no estimation except by judgment, so the decree is prior and destiny is posterior.

We say:

The interpretation of the two contenders concerning decree and destiny, according to what each of them has invoked — even though the meaning of the words destiny and decree, on the basis of what we have shown, makes such an interpretation impossible — is in any case: that decree and destiny are the prior and the posterior. The author of al-Islah is bound by his saying that destiny is estimation and decree is detail, and by the analogy that he has mentioned in the matter of the garment and the image of the sewing — namely, that destiny is what stands in potency, what could be; and the decree is what has been put into act, that has emerged from the door of possibility, which is potency, into actuality. If the meaning of destiny and decree were this, then it is impossible to say of the prior and the posterior that, in fact, in some respects neither of them is in potency, nor that it had emerged into act according to what we have come to know from the evidence — so he has proceeded.

Chapter Two of Section Eight

The author of al-Nusrah said that there is no estimation except by a judge who obliges that estimation; and by representing the decree with the craft of sewing for whose sake destiny is destined — which he made posterior, with his submission that the meaning of destiny is, in the author of al-Islah's view, the existence of an existent by potency. It is not permissible to say this of the posterior, since it has been established from what we have said above that it is already in place; and if it is permissible, then destiny is not posterior. If destiny were not posterior, then decree would be the prior — and it is better for there not to be, if it is ever by potency, that it then go out into act. So what his pronunciation performs corresponds to what is upon it in the prior state. It was also impossible that

- 155

<!-- page 158 -->
it be said that decree and destiny are the prior and the posterior. Even if it were impossible, we say that their saying is correct in the way they have been explained, not according to what they have said, because the decree has things in common in its meaning, and these things all belong to the world of the body, not to the world of creativity — and likewise destiny.

Chapter Three of Section Eight

As for the decree, it indicates the speaker who was forced to it by the support of God Almighty, and those who take his place — peace be upon them — who have reached the level parallel to the first revival. All of them are, in their act, command, prohibition, and judgment in the call already in existence (best prayers and peace be upon them) (1), having been in potency, identical with the meaning indicated by the word "judge." This is what the author of al-Nusrah opposes, and therefore it is valid to say it and to present it to the decree.

Chapter Four of Section Eight

Secondly, decree is what emerges from the potency of revelation and the Shariʿah — the sciences that are not explained in them but are present in them by potency — into explanation and act, by which souls are taken out of the limit of potency into the limit of act. This is what the author of al-Islah means by his interpretation of the word "decree."

Chapter Five of Section Eight

Thirdly, decree is what emerges from action, and the latency of events,

(1) Fell in copy (A).
- 156


<!-- page 159 -->
And the impossibilities estimated in the world to move the moving and to still the still consonants — those that admit no reversal — therefore, when it happens and comes to pass in the verb of one who decrees a matter that is not due to Allah's decree, these are the things that are said to require recompense.

Section Six of Chapter Eight

As for the qa'im, he is first the promised Qa'im, promised by Adam (peace be upon him) and by his summons — the one who guards it — and by the Imams and Messengers (peace be upon them) in the period of concealment, extending the force of the fact that he is destined to come out from estimation to action and existence. His existing summons is, in terms of the limits of the role of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) and his family in the concealment, that which follows the course of the qa'im potentially, not the apparent course that actually exists. Therefore, the interpretation of Laylat al-Qadr (peace be upon it), and the references of the shaykhs to him in their sayings, became "(al-Qadr / destiny)."

Section Seven of Chapter Eight

The qadar is, second, upon us — what the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) brought to him and to his family from revelation and Shari'ah: the ruling and the parables that have the force of the sciences and the knowledge that the soul needs in its passage to maturity and action, by being in plural form. All of this — and therefore there is no knowledge of its outer face except by interpretation of it. The statement of the author of al-Islah toward that refers to this meaning of qadar.

Section Eight of Chapter Eight

The qadar is, third, for the world with its parts that are in its power to come out into the universe

- 157

<!-- page 160 -->
and into action upon the passage of days, months, years, events, and worlds whose presence is necessary from it in the constant movement of the movers below it. Because it is a decree (1), it is said: what Allah has decreed will be, for it is predestined being in terms of the necessity of its existence; and if it lasts for a long time, it is like the being in actuality. Hence Allah said: *"They see it as far, and We see it as near"* (Quran 70:6–7). There was no estimation but that if there is one, it is a judgment; if the word "judgment" is an indication that what was not in power has gone out to action — these are the meanings of qada' and qadar.

If you consider the words of the author of al-Islah, and his saying that qadar precedes qada' in respect of authenticity — its meaning is correct only in what the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) brought from revelation and the Shari'ah, which precedes the judgment that is the interpretation, as he did, and the first mercy of Allah upon him. And the saying of the author of al-Nusra, that qada' precedes qadar — if you hope to take it, its meaning is correct only in the speaker (peace be upon him), the already-existing one, which is the advanced elimination of what came from the revelation and the Shari'ah, which is qadar.

Section Nine of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said:

A report about the wise scholars: that when Allah Almighty created the intellect, He completed the worlds, because it is the assembly of the images of the worlds.

We say:

The intellect that holds the images of the worlds is not the first creative intellect, nor is it the second intellect, which is the second emanated; rather, it is the intellect that ascends from the world

(1) Fell in copy (A).
(2) In copy (A), "the first" is mentioned.

- 158

<!-- page 161 -->
of nature by acquiring work and knowledge in parallel with the first emanation, so it was raised a second raising, thus becoming a compendium of the images of the worlds despite their multitude. It may be that there is imprinted upon it the form of all the essences as it stands in the case of the enunciators, the executors, and the supports — Allah's prayers be upon them. The first intellect is above this kind of multiplication, since its existence is innovative, and innovative existence multiplies only according to the reason of itself: that it is a cause and an effect, and that its existence is what was received in it from what is outside of it only. And the rest of the beings — I mean the intellects that are emanated after the first — multiply according to what I said of their self, and to what their existence has been preceded by, going back to the first intellect; their existence, nonetheless, is from what is poured into them from outside of them, and they are later in existence, multiplying not as the first multiplied in existence. Their need to multiply, for the sake of the intellection of all beings, is necessary, while what is separated from this need is the first in existence completely. If that is the case, then it turns out that the one that is a compendium of the images of the two worlds is not the first intellect, but the intellects that emerge from the abode of nature second.

Then we say:

The first intellect does not need the intellect below it, and it does not intellect anything, because by virtue of being in its own self of eternity, completeness, subsistence, and unity, and abstention — but for the sake of what is found, and if it is completely eternal. There remains one only, one who refrains from impossibility, not for the sake of what he has found; it is impossible for him to intellect anything other than himself, because there is no need for that.

We also say:

The one who is later in existence from the second raising needs to intellect what precedes it in existence as a compendium of the images, because the perfection in him — his subsistence — he acquires through his intellect (I mean over what his existence has been preceded by). And since he needs, in his completeness, his intellect (which is already existent before him from the limits) — who

- 159

<!-- page 162 -->
the hermeneutic summons was established upon (which is the second worship), in order to clarify their ranks, he must teach them and have them intellect. For if they know and intellect, the same multiplies — from one side, by the multitude of its intellected objects; and on the other side, it is united (which is the same), and it resembles what precedes it in existence. Our book known as *Rahat al-'Aql* (*The Peace of the Intellect*) contains, in evidence of legal proofs for this and of religious depictions, what suffices and heals the inquirer from his adversary. Allah has made us one of those who follow His pleasure in what has appeared and what is hidden, and who follow His guardians (peace be upon them) regarding him.

Section Ten of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Islah said:

But it is the mistake of those who made qada' the prior and qadar the posterior. Because the scholars have called the posterior by the name of qadar, which is the name of truth — because all the essences were emanated from the prior. That emanation was qadar itself, so the wise called it qadar. And in the words of the people, qada' and qadar came in like manner: I find them in the words "the qada' of qadar," and it is said "this is qada' and qadar." The meaning of qada' and qadar was hidden from them, so they made qada' the prior and qadar the posterior. And a mistake has been made on this side, as we have said: that all the essences were emanated from the precedent qadar, and that emanation was qadar itself, which is the assembly of the essences; testifying to it is the saying of Allah Almighty: *"Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure"* (Quran 54:49). Just as all the essences were emanated from the first, which is the command of the estimated power, and it was qadar itself, so what emerged from the second — indeed, it was the deed of qada' and qadar. It contains changes and alterations, which is the book of which Allah Almighty said: *"Allah erases what He wills and confirms"* (Quran 13:39). Qadar is posterior, as we have said, and qada' is what was done, because

- 160

<!-- page 163 -->
the posterior is the assessment of the product of what he said in this chapter:

Qadar is the name of the posterior, because all the essences were emanated from the precedent predestining, as Allah says: *"Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure"* (Quran 54:49).

The author of al-Islah said:

As for what he invoked in the matter of qada' and qadar — Allah's saying, may He be exalted: *"Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure"* — he has erred in the interpretation of this verse and in its interpretation of what we have explained, because it is purely innocent of his reading.

We say:

The meaning of "We" by which He intends a subject not tainted by description or attribute, and "every thing" by which the intellect (which is the totality of things) is intended, and "We have created it" by which an image is intended — the two worlds are among them. And the measure by which the posterior is intended, upon which the matter is stabilized — all the spiritual and physical images appeared among them in the testimony, which is the pen, the tablet, the writing, and the writer. The writer is the face of "We," and He is pure eternity. The pen is the pen of the intellect, which is the pen of the pen, and "We have created it" is the figure of the tablet. The pen, in counterpart to the letters that appear upon the tablet — and the measure is what is in front of the tablet, receptive to all the forms of the letters. As all the physical and spiritual things, the simple and the dense, are located under these intelligible letters that appear from the writer with the pen upon the tablet, so likewise all the upper and lower images were shown by Allah Almighty by means of the intellect and the soul, and He has not singled out anything between them — just as He does not single out anything from the ordered letters that actually appear from the writer upon the tablet. This is the view of the author of al-Nusra in the refutation.

We say:

The authors have mentioned in this chapter what is unsustainable, no more than this in al-Islah, nor in this case of refutation; because the author of al-Islah did not make it obligatory that qadar

And the author of al-Nusra made his counter (1) a saying:

The author of al-Islah's saying about the verse is not in a comparative or congruent manner. This is because he said:

Allah Almighty by ((We)) intends the pure religion that is not tainted by an attribute, and He does not intend by it that there is no attribute. And the word "We" is only to the extent that the Prophet derives, by establishing his rank in the world — not pure non-attribution. And how can "We" fall upon pure non-attribution? Between Him and her is the middle of negation; but she is upon the middle that is near to the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) in the commentary upon him, and the meaning is the Prophet as he is the one who acts in His stead.

Section Eleven of Chapter Eight

He said:

"Every thing" is what the intellect intends, which is the totality of things; so every thing in this place is not the intellect. We have shown that the first intellect does not multiply, not as multiplies that whose existence is "every thing" — which is what the community needs from the two worships that combine the sciences outwardly by work, and inwardly by perception that leads to salvation from the torment of the worlds.

Section Twelve of Chapter Eight

He said:

"We have created it" — He does not intend by it what he went to, because "We have created it" means "We have predestined it"; I mean: We have made the form of revelation and the Shari'ah a form in whose power is every thing of the sciences and the religious works that the community needs to ward off the pain of the soul in
